The Blessing of Intellect and Necessity of Revelation
Maturidi introduces intellect and revelation as complementary guides.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why both intellect and revelation are necessary for human salvation according to Imam Maturidi.

Core Idea
Intellect and revelation together light the path to true happiness.

Imam Maturidi opens his work by grounding theology in two essential gifts: the intellect and revelation. He presents a vision of human dignity and the limits of unaided reason. This passage sets the stage for understanding why divine guidance is not optional but indispensable.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation. Let the author's words and rhythm sink in before interpreting.
Notice the formal opening with praise and blessings.
2
Identify the three main components
The passage has three main parts: God's countless blessings, the intellect as the greatest blessing, and the sending of messengers to guide the intellect. Identify each.
3
Consider the meaning of happiness
The passage says humans cannot attain happiness in this life or the next except through revelation and intellect. Reflect on what 'happiness of temporal and eternal life' means for you.
This is a key theological claim.
4
Reflect on the relationship between intellect and revelation
Think about how intellect and revelation work together according to the passage. Does one replace the other? Are they partners?
The passage says revelation illuminates the intellect's path.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تصدير
الحمد لله رب العالمين والصلاة والسلام على سيد المرسلين محمد وآله وصحبه ومن سلك هديه في منهج التوحيد إلى يوم الدين.
بنعم
لقد أنعم الله تعالى على خلقه لا تُحصى. ومنح ابن آدم الذي خلقه الله جسميا في أحسن تقويم وكرمه معنويًا على سائر المخلوقات بنعم تفوق ما سواها؛ وعلى رأس تلك النعم العقل أو الوعي الذي يعتبر وسيلة لتحمل الأمانة الإلهية. هذا، وقد أرسل الله تعالى رسله الكرام لكي يضئ بذلك طريق العقل ويرشده فيما يفوق قدراته. فالنوع البشري لن ينال سعادة الحياة الفانية والسرمدية في مستوى الفرد والأسرة والمجتمع إلا عن طريق الوحي والعقل الواعي المدرك له.
Foreword. Praise be to Allah, Lord of the worlds, and prayers and peace be upon the master of the messengers, Muhammad, and his family and companions, and those who follow his guidance in the path of tawhid until the Day of Judgment. With blessings. Allah Almighty has bestowed upon His creatures blessings that cannot be counted. And He granted the son of Adam, whom Allah created physically in the best stature and honored spiritually above the rest of creation, with blessings surpassing others; and at the head of those blessings is the intellect (al-ʿaql) or consciousness (al-waʿy), which is the means of bearing the divine trust (al-amāna al-ilāhiyya). This, and Allah Almighty sent His noble messengers to illuminate thereby the path of the intellect and guide it in what exceeds its capabilities. For the human species will not attain the happiness of the temporal and eternal life at the level of the individual, family, and society except through revelation (al-waḥy) and the conscious intellect that comprehends it.
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage is a theological introduction that establishes the roles of intellect and revelation. It begins with the standard Islamic invocation of praise and peace upon the Prophet. Then it enumerates God's countless blessings, specifically highlighting the dignity of humans who are created in the best form and honored over other creatures. The greatest of these blessings is the intellect (al-ʿaql) or consciousness, which is the instrument for bearing the divine trust. However, the intellect has limitations. God therefore sent messengers to illuminate the intellect's path and guide it in areas beyond its reach. The conclusion is stark: human happiness—both in this temporary life and in the eternal afterlife, at every level of society—cannot be attained except through revelation (al-waḥy) combined with a conscious intellect that comprehends it. This establishes the necessity of prophecy and scripture alongside reason.

Key Terms
العقل (al-ʿaql)
الوحي (al-waḥy)
الأمانة الإلهية (al-amāna al-ilāhiyya)
سعادة الحياة الفانية والسرمدية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think intellect alone suffices for salvation.
Correction: The passage emphasizes that revelation is indispensable; happiness is only attained through both.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary purpose of sending messengers?
A) To replace the need for intellect
B) To illuminate the path of the intellect and guide it beyond its capacity
C) To establish political authority
D) To perform miracles

Key Takeaway
True happiness in this life and the next requires both revelation and a conscious intellect working together.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
After learning that true happiness in this life and the next requires both revelation and a conscious intellect, ask yourself: In which areas of my daily life am I relying solely on my own reasoning and ignoring the guidance of the Quran and Sunnah? What is one specific change I can make today to submit my intellect to Allah's revelation?
?
The passage describes the intellect as the means to bear the divine trust. Am I using my intellect to fulfill that trust by seeking knowledge and obeying Allah, or am I using it to justify my desires? Write down one way you will use your intellect today to draw closer to Allah.

The Preeminence of al-Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid
Editor argues Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid is the perfected foundational reference of Sunni kalam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the editor's argument that al-Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid achieved perfection in methodology and is the foundational reference of Sunni kalam, challenging the common view that al-Ash'ari is the sole founder.

Core Idea
Al-Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid, not al-Ash'ari's work, represents the perfected methodology of Sunni kalam.

In this lesson, we examine an editor's historical argument about the origins of the Sunni kalam school. While many assume Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari is the founder, the editor of Kitab al-Tawhid presents evidence that al-Maturidi's work reached methodological perfection first. This passage challenges us to reconsider the standard narrative and appreciate the foundational role of Maturidi's text.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once. Notice the overall claim the editor is making about the two scholars.
Pay attention to the phrase 'وصل إلى مرتبة الكمال في التأليف' (reached the level of perfection in authorship).
2
Identify the two figures
The passage mentions Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari and Abu Mansur al-Maturidi. What does the editor say about each? Note the common opinion about al-Ash'ari and the editor's counter-argument about al-Maturidi.
3
Trace the argument
Outline the editor's reasoning: common opinion → challenge (careful examination) → evidence (perfection of al-Maturidi's work) → conclusion (Kitab al-Tawhid is a fundamental reference).
4
Reflect on implications
Think about why this argument matters. Does it diminish al-Ash'ari? Or does it simply highlight al-Maturidi's overlooked contribution? Consider how this affects the study of Sunni kalam.
This is an historical evaluation, not a theological proof.
5
Check your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to see if you grasped the key point about the common opinion and the editor's alternative view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العقيدة السنية الرسمية بعد مرور الثلث الأخير من عمره. فكلهم قد تبنوا منهجا كلاميًا مماثلاً وطبقوه ، فاستطاعوا بمرور الزمن أن يشكلوا مدرسة كلامية سنية انتسب إليها أكثر من التسعين بالمائة من مسلمي العالم الإسلامي. ومهما كان الرأي المعتاد بأن أبا الحسن الأشعري يعتبر مؤسس المدرسة الكلامية السنية»، نستطيع القول بأن الاطلاع الدقيق للتراث الكلامي في المرحلة الأولى قد يؤدي بنا إلى الاعتراف بأن ما ألف في هذا التراث ووصل إلى مرتبة الكمال في التأليف هو ما ألّفه أبو منصور الماتريدي في ذلك. فبالتالي إن كتاب التوحيد لأبي منصور الماتريدي في منهجه ومحتواه وطرق الاستدلال فيه يعتبر بلا شك مرجعا أساسيًا بين أصول المصادر الكلامية التي ظهرت فيما بعد من التراث الكلامي.
The official Sunni creed after the last third of his life. For all of them adopted a similar kalam methodology and applied it, so over time they were able to form a Sunni kalam school to which more than ninety percent of the Muslims of the Islamic world belong. And whatever the common opinion that Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari is considered the founder of the Sunni kalam school, we can say that careful examination of the kalam heritage in the first stage may lead us to acknowledge that what was authored in this heritage and reached the level of perfection in authorship is what Abu Mansur al-Maturidi authored in that regard. Consequently, the Kitab al-Tawhid of Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, in its methodology, content, and methods of inference, is undoubtedly considered a fundamental reference among the foundational kalam sources that appeared later in the kalam heritage.
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage is a historical-argumentative statement by the editor about the relative status of al-Maturidi versus al-Ash'ari. It begins by asserting that after a certain period (the last third of his life?), the Sunni official creed emerged as a school with a common kalam methodology, and this school now includes over 90% of Muslims. Then it challenges the common opinion that al-Ash'ari is the founder of the Sunni kalam school. The editor argues that a close study of early kalam literature shows that the work which achieved perfection in authorship is actually al-Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid. Therefore, this book is a foundational reference later kalam sources depend on. The argument is that while al-Ash'ari is widely recognized, in terms of methodological perfection, al-Maturidi's work is superior. This is not a theological proof but an historical evaluation of the text's importance.

Key Terms
مدرسة كلامية سنية
كتاب التوحيد
أبو منصور الماتريدي
أبو الحسن الأشعري
مرجع أساسي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari is the sole founder of the Sunni kalam school, and his works are the foundational references.
Correction: The editor argues that careful examination shows al-Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid reached perfection and is the foundational reference, implying Maturidi's work is more methodologically sound and influential.

Checkpoint
According to the editor, what is the common opinion about the founder of the Sunni kalam school?
A) Abu Mansur al-Maturidi
B) Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Al-Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid is considered by this editor to be the perfected foundation of Sunni kalam methodology, challenging the common focus on al-Ash'ari.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever passively accepted that al-Ash'ari was the sole founder of Sunni kalam without examining the evidence? What does this lesson reveal about the danger of relying on popular narratives without verification? Commit to investigating the primary sources before forming conclusions about scholarly authority.
?
The editor argues that 'careful examination' leads to a different conclusion. In your own religious learning, do you prioritize careful examination of original sources, or do you rely on summaries and common opinions? How can you change your study habits today to ensure you seek the perfected understanding?
?
If the Kitab al-Tawhid is indeed the perfected foundational reference, what responsibility do you have to engage with it directly rather than through secondhand accounts? What step will you take this week to access and study this work or its teachings?

The Role of Maturidi's Students in Developing the School
Explains how Maturidi's students clarified, revised, and expanded his school.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi's students actively developed his school through clarification, revision, and respectful disagreement, based on the principle of scholarly sincerity.

Core Idea
Students of Maturidi perfected his school by respectfully disagreeing when needed.

This passage shows that the Maturidi school was not static; its early students engaged critically with the imam's works. They refined and expanded the school while upholding the principle of scholarly sincerity, ensuring its vitality in Transoxiana.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Notice the parallel drawn between Maturidi's work on Abu Hanifa and the students' work on Maturidi.
The passage uses a comparative structure to highlight continuity.
2
Identify the students' actions
List the specific verbs describing what the students did: clarifying, making accessible, revising, developing, adding, and disagreeing. Notice that each action shows active engagement, not blind transmission.
3
Understand the underlying principle
The passage states that the students affirmed 'the truthfulness of scholars, their sincerity, and their certainty that human effort is prone to error.' Reflect on how this principle justified their critical work.
This principle distinguishes scholarly development from passive imitation.
4
Recognize the outcome
The passage concludes that these efforts made the school a driving force that supported, preserved, and spread the school in Transoxiana, giving it life and longevity.
This shows the practical impact of scholarly independence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكما صنع الماتريدي مع شيخ شيوخه الإمام أبي حنيفة، صنع تلاميذ الماتريدي مع آثاره؛ فقد نهض منهم جيل بعد جيل - في حلقة متصلة - بتوضيح مذهب إمامهم، والعمل على تيسيره وتنقيحه وتنميته، مضيفين إليه على طريقه ما قد يكون قصر فيه، من غير تحرج في أن يختلفوا معه فيما يرونه يستحق المخالفة أو يستوجبها، مقررين بذلك مبدأ صدق العلماء، الحقة، وإخلاصهم، ويقينهم بأن الجهد البشري يحتمل الخطأ كما يحتمل الصواب؛ فكانوا بذلك قوى دافعة تدعم المذهب وتحمفظه وتذيعه في بلاد ما وراء النهر، وتحقق له الحياة وطول العمر.
And just as Maturidi did with the imam of his shaykhs, Imam Abu Hanifa, so did the students of Maturidi with his works; for generation after generation - in a continuous chain - arose to clarify the school of their imam, and to work on making it accessible, revising it, and developing it, adding to it, in his way, what he may have omitted, without hesitation to disagree with him in what they considered deserving of disagreement or necessitating it, thereby affirming the principle of the truthfulness of scholars, their sincerity, and their certainty that human effort is prone to error as it is prone to correctness; thus they were driving forces that supported the school, preserved it, and spread it in Transoxiana, and achieved for it life and longevity.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage explains how Maturidi's students continued his work. They did not merely transmit but actively developed the school, even disagreeing when necessary, based on the principle of scholarly integrity. They affirmed that human effort can be correct or erroneous, so they felt free to improve upon Maturidi's works. This attitude ensured the school's vitality and spread in Transoxiana.

Key Terms
تيسير
تنقيح
تنمية
صدق العلماء
بلاد ما وراء النهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Maturidi school was rigidly followed without any critical engagement by its early students.
Correction: The passage shows that students actively clarified, revised, developed, and even disagreed with Maturidi when necessary, based on the principle of scholarly sincerity.

Checkpoint
What principle did Maturidi's students affirm by disagreeing with him when they saw fit?
A) The infallibility of scholars
B) The principle of scholarly truthfulness and sincerity
C) The school must never change
D) The necessity of blind following

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's students ensured the school's vitality by critically engaging with his works, adding corrections, and spreading them in Transoxiana.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that Maturidi's students added to his works and even disagreed with him, do you have the courage to respectfully question a beloved teacher or scholar when you see something that needs correction? Or do you remain silent out of false respect?
?
The passage says scholars affirmed that human effort is prone to error. Have you ever admitted being wrong about a religious understanding you held firm? How does that knowledge change the way you learn from scholars today?
?
Are you actively clarifying, making accessible, and developing the knowledge you have, or are you content to passively receive it? What step will you take this week to actively engage with what you learn?

Maturidi's Works in Uṣūl al-Fiqh: al-Jadal and Ma'ākhidh al-Sharā'i'
Lists Maturidi's two works on uṣūl al-fiqh and their transmission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two works Maturidi authored in uṣūl al-fiqh, their current state of loss, and how his views were transmitted through later Hanafi sources.

Core Idea
Maturidi's usul works are lost, but his legacy endures through later citations.

This passage introduces Maturidi's two foundational works on Islamic jurisprudence, noting that they have not survived. Despite their loss, later Hanafi scholars preserved and transmitted Maturidi's ideas through citations and commentaries, especially in relation to his Ta'wīlāt al-Qur'ān. The passage provides a concrete example of this transmission.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the enumeration of the two books and the subsequent remarks about their fate.
2
Identify the two books
Find the two works listed: Kitāb al-Jadal and Ma’ākhidh al-Sharā’i‘. Understand what uṣūl al-fiqh means.
3
Understand their fate
The passage states that nothing of these works has come down to us, nor is there any description. This is important for evaluating Maturidi's direct textual legacy.
4
Trace the transmission
Notice how later Hanafi works transmitted Maturidi's views, either through citation or detailed discussion, often attributing them to his commentary on Ta’wīlāt al-Māturīdī.
5
Examine the example
The passage gives the example of ‘Abd al-‘Azīz al-Bukhārī in Kashf al-Asrār, who cites Maturidi's opinion on the solitary report (khabar al-wāḥid). Reflect on how this shows the indirect survival of Maturidi's thought.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(بـ) أصول الفقه . فقد ذكر جميع من ترجموا له كتابين له في أصول الفقه، هما: ١ - كتاب الجدل ٢ ـ مآخذ الشرائع ولم يقع لنا شيء من هذين المؤلفين، ولا وصف لهما. بيد أن بعض كتب الأصول والفقه الحنفية قد نقلت عن الماتريدي في ذلك الميدان إما بطريق الاستشهاد وإما بطريق البسط والتفصيل . غير أن ذلك كان ينسب إلى شرح تأويلات الماتريدي في الغالب، كما فعل عبد العزيز البخاري في كشف الأسرار عن أصول فخر الإسلام البزدوي أثناء الحديث عن خبر الواحد إذا خالف عموم الكتاب أو ظاهره وبيان الرأي في صحة تخصيص هذا العموم.٢٣
(B) Uṣūl al-Fiqh (Foundations of Jurisprudence). All those who wrote his biography mentioned two books by him on uṣūl al-fiqh: 1. Kitāb al-Jadal (Book of Disputation), 2. Ma’ākhidh al-Sharā’i‘ (Sources of the Laws). Nothing of these two works has come down to us, nor is there any description of them. However, some books of Ḥanafī uṣūl and fiqh transmitted from al-Māturīdī in that field, either by way of citation or by way of elaboration and detail. But that was usually attributed to his commentary on Ta’wīlāt al-Māturīdī, as did ‘Abd al-‘Azīz al-Bukhārī in Kashf al-Asrār ‘an Uṣūl Fakhr al-Islām al-Bazdawī during his discussion of the solitary report (khabar al-wāḥid) when it conflicts with the general or apparent meaning of the Book, and the exposition of the view on the validity of specifying that general meaning.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage presents Maturidi's two known works in the field of uṣūl al-fiqh: Kitāb al-Jadal (Book of Disputation) and Ma’ākhidh al-Sharā’i‘ (Sources of the Laws). It notes that all biographers mention these two books, but no copies or descriptions of them have survived. Despite the loss of the originals, some later Ḥanafī works on uṣūl and fiqh transmit Maturidi's views in this area, either by citation or by detailed exposition. However, such transmissions are usually attributed to his commentary on his own Ta’wīlāt al-Qur’ān (known as Ta’wīlāt al-Māturīdī). The passage gives an example: ‘Abd al-‘Azīz al-Bukhārī in his Kashf al-Asrār, a commentary on al-Bazdawī’s Uṣūl, cites Maturidi's opinion on the issue of a solitary report (khabar al-wāḥid) conflicting with the general or apparent meaning of the Qur’an, and discusses the validity of specifying the general meaning.

Key Terms
أصول الفقه
كتاب الجدل
مآخذ الشرائع
شرح تأويلات الماتريدي
خبر الواحد
عبد العزيز البخاري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi's usul works are still available or their content is known.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that nothing of these two works has come down to us, nor any description.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the two books Maturidi wrote on uṣūl al-fiqh?
A) Kitāb al-Tawḥīd and Kitāb al-Jadal
B) Kitāb al-Jadal and Ma’ākhidh al-Sharā’i‘
C) Bayān Wahm al-Mu‘tazila and Kitāb al-Imāma
D) Ma’ākhidh al-Sharā’i‘ and Kitāb al-Imāma

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's two works on usul al-fiqh are lost, but his influence persisted through later Hanafi citations, especially in commentaries on his Ta'wilat.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The works of Māturīdī in uṣūl al-fiqh are lost, yet his teachings survive through others. What knowledge have you been given that you have neglected to pass on? Are you guarding your knowledge as a trust to be transmitted, or are you letting it die with you? Write down one specific piece of beneficial knowledge you can actively teach to someone this week.
?
The passage shows that Māturīdī's opinions are cited not from his original books but from his commentary on his own work. How often do you rely on secondary sources or summaries rather than engaging directly with primary texts? Are you willing to dig deeper to understand the original sources of your faith, even when the path is harder?
?
Māturīdī's works are lost, but his legacy endures through citations. If all your writings or teachings were lost, would your impact on others be enough to preserve the truth you stand for? What concrete steps will you take today to ensure that your knowledge benefits others beyond your own lifetime?

Maturidi on the Deep Understanding of the Salaf
Quotes Maturidi's interpretation of verse 30:17-18 regarding five daily prayers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's view on the interpretive depth of the early Muslims in deriving religious obligations from the Quran.

Core Idea
Understanding the Quran requires deep insight, as shown by the Salaf's deduction of prayer obligations.

Imam Maturidi, in a statement recorded in Badā’i‘ al-Ṣanā’i‘, reflects on a verse from Sūrat al-Rūm that mentions glorification of Allah at different times. He notes that the early Muslims saw in this verse the obligation of the five daily prayers, while later people might only see a command to glorify. This lesson explores that contrast and what it teaches about interpretive methodology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own words come through before you interpret.
Notice the contrast Maturidi draws between two groups.
2
Identify the two groups
Maturidi compares the early Muslims ('they') with the people of his own time. Identify what each group supposedly understood from the verse.
The early Muslims understood the obligation of the five prayers; contemporaries understood only glorification.
3
Understand Maturidi's praise
Reflect on why Maturidi praises the early Muslims. Their understanding went beyond the apparent meaning to derive a legal obligation. This demonstrates their deep interpretive ability.
4
Apply the lesson
Consider how this affects your own approach to Quranic interpretation. Are there verses where you might only see surface meaning, missing deeper implications?
This connects to the broader theme of tafsir methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجاء في كتاب بدائع الصنائع في أثناء استنباط أوقات الصلوات الخمس من قوله تعالى: ﴿فسبحان الله حين تمسون وحين تصبحون وله الحمد في السموات والأرض وعشيا وحين تظهرون﴾ [الروم، ۱۷/۳۰ - ۱۸]؛ قال الشيخ أبو منصور الماتريدي السمرقندي: إنهم فهموا هذه الآية فرضية الصلوات الخمس، ولو كانت أفهامهم مثل أفهام أهل زماننا لما فهموا منها سوى التسبيح المذكور.
And it is mentioned in Kitāb Badā’i‘ al-Ṣanā’i‘ while deriving the times of the five prayers from the words of the Most High: 'So glorify Allah when you enter the evening and when you enter the morning. And to Him belongs praise in the heavens and the earth, and at nightfall and when you enter the noon.' [Sūrat al-Rūm, 30:17-18]. Shaykh Abū Manṣūr al-Māturīdī al-Samarqandī said: 'They understood from this verse the obligation of the five prayers. If their understanding had been like the understanding of the people of our time, they would have understood nothing from it except the mentioned glorification.'
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage quotes a statement of Imam Maturidi reported in the Hanafi fiqh work Badā’i‘ al-Ṣanā’i‘ by al-Kāsānī. The context is the derivation of the times of the five daily prayers from Quranic verse 30:17-18. Maturidi remarks that the early Muslims (the Companions and early generations) understood from this verse the obligation of the five prayers. He contrasts their deep understanding with that of the people of his own time, implying that later people would only see a command to glorify Allah (tashīḥ) without inferring the specific prayer times. This demonstrates Maturidi's high regard for the interpretive capacity of the Salaf and his criticism of the decline in understanding among his contemporaries.

Key Terms
الصلوات الخمس
بدائع الصنائع
التسبيح
الروم
الفرضية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi is criticizing the early Muslims
Correction: Maturidi is praising the early Muslims' deep understanding and criticizing his contemporaries for their shallow understanding.

Checkpoint
What did Maturidi say the early Muslims understood from the verse in Sūrat al-Rūm?
A) The glorification of Allah only
B) The obligation of the five daily prayers
C) The prohibition of polytheism
D) The times of fasting

Key Takeaway
Maturidi emphasizes that the Salaf's depth of understanding allowed them to derive the obligation of the five daily prayers from a verse that appears to only command glorification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Quran, do you often stop at the surface meaning, or do you strive to uncover deeper implications like the Salaf did? What holds you back from deeper reflection?
?
The Salaf understood the obligation of prayer from a verse about glorification. Is there a verse you have read many times without noticing its deeper guidance? What might you be missing in your daily recitation?
?
Maturidi contrasts the Salaf's understanding with that of later generations. Where do you see your own understanding falling short of theirs, and what concrete step can you take this week to deepen your connection to the Quran?

The Two Sources of Knowledge: Reason and Revelation
Maturidi states the two sources of religious knowledge and describes the social nature of inherited beliefs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's assertion that religious knowledge depends on two sources: reason ('aql) and revelation (sam'), and how inherited beliefs are a social phenomenon.

Core Idea
Religion is known through reason and revelation together, not one alone.

Al-Maturidi begins his discussion by stating the foundational principle: the knowledge of religion relies on two sources. He then observes that human societies inherit their beliefs from ancestors, often without rational scrutiny. This inherited tradition, though widely accepted, is not necessarily reliable. This lesson introduces the two sources and sets the stage for the rational argument that follows.

Guided Path
1
Identify the thesis
Read the first sentence of the Arabic passage. Notice that Maturidi states that religion must be known through two sources. Find the names of those sources.
The two sources are ‘aql (reason) and sam‘ (revelation/transmitted tradition).
2
Understand the social observation
Maturidi notes that every society has its own beliefs, often inherited. Why does he call this a social phenomenon? What does he say about how these beliefs are transmitted?
He points out that beliefs are passed down from fathers and forefathers, and that rulers and claimants use them to organize society.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider the implication: if beliefs are merely social inheritance, they may not be true. This sets the need for a reliable source of knowledge—reason and revelation together.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقول أبو منصور الماتريدي في موضوع الحاجة إلى الدين بأنه لا بد من الاعتماد على مصدرين من مصادر المعرفة أي إن أصل ما يُعرَف به الدين وجهان، أحدهما العقل، والآخر السمع. وهو يرى بأن المجتمعات البشرية لها معتقداتها المتعددة والمختلفة؛ فهناك فرقة بالمجتمع تدعى أن ما يعتقده ويعتمد عليه فهو صحيح ويدعو غيره إليه وأن المعتقدات الأخرى معتقدات يعكسها الصواب. فتلك العقيدة بالتالي ظاهرة اجتماعية لا نستطيع أن ننكر بأنها انتقلت إليهم من آبائهم وأجدادهم. ويعنى ذلك أن السمع الذي لقي القبول التام في مجتمع ما، قد جرت عليه سياسة ملوك الأرض حول تسوية أمورهم، وكذلك شأن هؤلاء الذين ادعوا الرسالة والحكمة، وهؤلاء الذين قاموا بتدبير أنواع الصناعة والمهن بالمجتمع. وأما العقل الذي يعتبر جزءًا من عالمنا هذا ، فيحكم بأن وجود هذا العالم ليس للفناء، بل ينبغي أن يكون مؤسسا على الحكمة فيكون بالتالي إنشاء العالم للبقاء لا للفناء. ثم نشاهد بأن العالم بأجمعه مبنى على عناصر تتكون من طبائع مختلفة ووجوه متضادة؛ فالإنسان الذي نعتبره صاحب عقل به يستطيع أن يجمع بين المجتمع ويفرق بين الذي حقه التفريق، وبعبارة أخرى هو الإنسان الذي سمته الحكماء بالعالم الصغير، لو تُرك هو أي هذا العالم الصغير) وما عليه في حاله بناءً على أهواء وطبائع وشهوات لَدَخَل الأفراد والمجتمعات في ساحة التنازع، فيعقب ذلك التفاني والفساد. غير أن النظام المحكم الذي يسود هذا العالم ليس بذلك هو الهدف من وجوده. ويعنى ذلك أن المدبّر الذي أوجد العالم والجنس البشري الذي يعيش بداخله ليس من الحكمة أن يترك تلك المجتمعات من غير مرشد. لذلك، أرسل إليهم رسلاً يثق الناس فيهم وفي دعواهم من ناحية، ومن ناحية أخرى جعل لهؤلاء الرسل دليلاً وبرهانا يعلمون به صدق المرسلين ؛ وأهم دليل في ذلك هو العقل الذي يتميز به الإنسان . فقد حاول الماتريدي بذلك أن يستدل بأن العقل والنقل مصدران من مصادر المعرفة ودورهما في ذلك.
Abu Mansur al-Maturidi says on the topic of the need for religion that it is necessary to rely on two sources of knowledge, i.e., the origin by which religion is known is two aspects: one of them is reason, and the other is revelation. He holds that human societies have diverse and different beliefs; there is a group in society that claims that what it believes and relies upon is correct and calls others to it, and that other beliefs are beliefs that are contrary to truth. That belief is consequently a social phenomenon that we cannot deny has been transmitted to them from their fathers and forefathers. This means that revelation which has received complete acceptance in a society has been subject to the policy of the kings of the earth regarding the ordering of their affairs, as well as the case of those who claimed prophethood and wisdom, and those who undertook the management of various crafts and professions in society. As for reason, which is considered part of this world of ours, it judges that the existence of this world is not for annihilation, but rather it should be founded upon wisdom, so consequently the creation of the world is for permanence, not for annihilation. Then we observe that the entire world is built upon elements consisting of different natures and opposing aspects; man, whom we consider to possess reason by which he is able to unite society and separate what deserves separation, in other words, man whom the philosophers called the microcosm, if he (i.e., this microcosm) is left as he is based on whims, natures, and desires, individuals and societies would enter the arena of conflict, and then annihilation and corruption would follow. However, the precise order that prevails in this world is not that; the goal of its existence is not that. This means that the Administrator who created the world and the human race living within it is not wise to leave those societies without a guide. Therefore, He sent to them messengers whom people trust in their persons and their claim on one hand, and on the other hand He made for these messengers a proof and evidence by which they know the truthfulness of the messengers; and the most important evidence in that is reason, which distinguishes man. Thus al-Maturidi attempted to argue that reason and transmitted knowledge are two sources of knowledge and their role in that.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage is a philosophical argument by al-Maturidi establishing the necessity of two sources of religious knowledge: reason ('aql) and revelation/sacred tradition (sam'). The passage begins by stating the core thesis: religion must be known through two sources, reason and revelation. Maturidi then observes that human societies have diverse beliefs, each claiming truth, and these beliefs are often inherited social phenomena, not necessarily based on rational proof. This inherited tradition (sam') has been used by rulers and claimants of prophethood to organize society, but it is not inherently reliable. Reason, on the other hand, provides a universal judgment: the world is not created for annihilation but for permanence based on wisdom. Reason also observes that the world consists of opposing elements, and humans possess reason to unite and differentiate. If humans were left to their desires, society would collapse into conflict. Therefore, the ordered existence of the world indicates that its Creator would not leave humanity without guidance. Hence, messengers are sent with trustworthy claims and evidence, the most important of which is reason itself, which enables humans to recognize the truth of the messengers. Maturidi's argument thus demonstrates that reason and revelation are complementary, with reason establishing the need for revelation and then confirming the truth of prophetic claims.

Key Terms
العقل
السمع
المعرفة
الدين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi believes that reason alone is sufficient for religious knowledge.
Correction: Maturidi actually argues that reason and revelation are both necessary and complementary.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what are the two sources of religious knowledge?
A) Reason and experience
B) Reason and revelation
C) Revelation and intuition
D) Reason and society

Key Takeaway
Maturidi establishes that religious knowledge requires both reason and revelation, as inherited beliefs alone are insufficient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Can you name one belief you hold about Allah or Islam that you have never seriously questioned with your own intellect? What would it take for you to test it against reason and revelation, rather than just family tradition?
?
If your entire community suddenly abandoned a practice you grew up with, would your faith remain unshaken? Why or why not? Where does your true anchor lie—in the consensus of those around you or in a conviction you have personally verified?
?
Maturidi warns that revelation itself has been exploited by rulers for worldly order. Have you ever accepted a religious ruling because it was convenient or authoritative without wrestling with it yourself? What step will you take this week to own your faith intellectually?

Reason's Role in Establishing the Need for Prophethood
Maturidi presents a rational argument for prophethood and the role of reason.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses rational arguments to prove that the world's order implies a Creator who sends messengers, and that reason itself is the primary evidence for their truthfulness.

Core Idea
Reason shows that the world is created for a purpose and that humanity needs divine guidance.

Having introduced the two sources, Maturidi now presents a rational argument. He observes that the world is built on opposing elements and that humans possess reason to unite and differentiate. If left to desires, conflict and corruption would result. The precise order of the world indicates that its Creator would not abandon humanity without guidance. Therefore, messengers are sent, and reason is the most important proof of their truthfulness. This lesson traces that argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Trace the rational argument
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Maturidi says reason judges that the world is not for annihilation but for permanence. What does he mean by this? How does he support it?
He contrasts the world's nature of opposing elements with its order, implying intentional design.
2
Identify the role of humanity
Maturidi describes man as a microcosm with reason to unite and separate. Why does he emphasize that if left to desires, conflict arises? How does this lead to the need for guidance?
The natural order of the world suggests that its Creator would not leave societies without guidance.
3
Locate the conclusion
Find the part where Maturidi concludes that God sent messengers. What evidence does he give for their truthfulness? How does reason function as that evidence?
He says the most important evidence is reason itself, which distinguishes humans.

The Two Foundations: Reason and Transmitted Tradition
Maturidi enumerates the two sources of religious knowledge and critiques mere transmission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Maturidi establishes reason and transmitted tradition as the two essential sources of religious knowledge, and why inherited beliefs alone are insufficient.

Core Idea
Religious knowledge requires both reason and transmitted tradition.

Maturidi begins his discussion of religious epistemology by laying out the two fundamental sources of knowledge. He observes that societies inherit their beliefs from ancestors, which suggests that mere transmission is not enough. This lesson introduces the dual foundation and the problem of uncritical acceptance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the main point: two sources of religious knowledge. Then read the translation.
Notice the direct statement: 'أصل ما يعرف به الدين وجهان'.
2
Identify the two sources
The passage explicitly names reason (العقل) and transmitted tradition (السمع) as the two foundations. Mark these terms.
3
Observe the social critique
Maturidi notes that beliefs are often inherited from ancestors and shaped by political powers. Reflect on why this makes transmitted tradition alone unreliable.
He gives examples of kings and craftsmen.
4
Connect to the need for reason
Consider how the observation about social transmission implies that reason must evaluate and complement transmitted tradition.
This sets up the argument for reason's role.

Abu Mansur al-Maturidi says on the topic of the need for religion that it is necessary to rely on two sources of knowledge, meaning that the foundation by which religion is known has two aspects: one is reason (al-ʿaql), and the other is transmitted tradition (al-samʿ). He holds that human societies have their multiple and different beliefs; there is a group in society that claims that what it believes and relies upon is correct and invites others to it, and that other beliefs are beliefs that correctness opposes. That belief is consequently a social phenomenon that we cannot deny has been transmitted to them from their fathers and forefathers. This means that the transmitted tradition (al-samʿ) that has received complete acceptance in a society has been subjected to the policy of the kings of the earth regarding settling their affairs, and likewise the case of those who claimed messengership and wisdom, and those who managed the various types of craft and professions in society. As for reason (al-ʿaql), which is considered a part of this world of ours, it judges that the existence of this world is not for annihilation, but rather it should be founded upon wisdom, thus the creation of the world is for permanence, not annihilation. Then we observe that the entire world is built upon elements composed of different natures and opposing aspects; man, whom we consider as possessing reason by which he can unite society and separate what deserves separation, in other words man whom the sages have called the small world (al-ʿālam al-ṣaghīr), if he (i.e., this small world) were left as he is based on whims, natures, and desires, individuals and societies would enter into a field of conflict, followed by destruction and corruption. Yet the precise order that prevails in this world is not that—it is the goal of its existence. This means that the Governor (al-mudabbir) who created the world and the human species living within it is not wise to leave those societies without a guide. Therefore, He sent messengers whom people trust in themselves and their claims on one hand, and on the other hand He provided these messengers with a proof and evidence by which the truthfulness of the messengers is known; the most important proof in this is reason, which distinguishes man. Thus, al-Maturidi attempted to argue that reason and transmitted tradition (al-naql) are two sources of knowledge and their role in that.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This is an expository passage in which Maturidi establishes his fundamental epistemology for religious knowledge. He begins by stating that religious knowledge requires two sources: reason (al-ʿaql) and transmitted tradition (al-samʿ). He then observes that societies inherit their beliefs from ancestors, implying that simple transmission without rational scrutiny is not sufficient. Maturidi then turns to reason's judgment about the world: reason sees that the world is created with wisdom, for permanence rather than annihilation. The world's composition from opposing elements and human nature—man called the 'small world'—would lead to conflict if left to whims. Since the world has a precise order, the Creator would not leave societies without guidance. Therefore, He sends messengers, and provides rational proofs of their truthfulness. Maturidi concludes that both reason and transmitted tradition are sources of knowledge, each playing a role. The passage thus establishes a dual-source epistemology: reason and revelation work together.

النقل

Correction: Maturidi explicitly states that transmitted tradition is equally necessary; reason and revelation work together.

?

?

The Rational Argument for Prophethood
Maturidi presents a rational argument from the world's order to the necessity of prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's rational argument that the world's order and purpose necessitate divine guidance through messengers.

Core Idea
The world's wisdom and order prove that God sent messengers with rational proofs.

Maturidi continues by presenting a rational argument for the necessity of prophethood. He observes that reason judges the world to be created with wisdom, not for annihilation, and that the world's precise order demands a guide. This lesson walks through the logical steps leading to the conclusion that God must send messengers with clear proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage to understand the flow of the argument. Follow the reasoning from premises to conclusion.
2
Identify the first premise
Reason judges that the world is created with wisdom and for permanence, not annihilation. This is the starting point.
Look for 'يحكم بأن وجود هذا العالم ليس للفناء'.
3
Observe the second premise
The world is composed of opposing elements; man left to whims would lead to conflict. Yet the world has order.
Note the contrast between potential chaos and actual order.
4
Draw the conclusion
Because the world has order, its Governor would not leave humanity without guidance. Therefore, He sends messengers with rational proofs.
The most important proof is reason itself.
5
Reflect on the role of reason
Reason is both the source of the argument and the key evidence for verifying messengers. Consider why reason is central.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقول أبو منصور الماتريدي في موضوع الحاجة إلى الدين بأنه لا بد من الاعتماد على مصدرين من مصادر ،المعرفة أي إن أصل ما يُعرَف به الدين وجهان، أحدهما العقل، والآخر السمع. وهو يرى بأن المجتمعات البشرية لها معتقداتها المتعددة والمختلفة؛ فهناك فرقة بالمجتمع تدعى أن ما يعتقده ويعتمد عليه فهو صحيح ويدعو غيره إليه وأن المعتقدات الأخرى معتقدات يعكسها الصواب. فتلك العقيدة بالتالي ظاهرة اجتماعية لا نستطيع أن ننكر بأنها انتقلت إليهم من آبائهم وأجدادهم. ويعنى ذلك أن السمع الذي لقي القبول التام في مجتمع ما، قد جرت عليه سياسة ملوك الأرض حول تسوية أمورهم، وكذلك شأن هؤلاء الذين ادعوا الرسالة والحكمة، وهؤلاء الذين قاموا بتدبير أنواع الصناعة والمهن بالمجتمع. وأما العقل الذي يعتبر جزءًا من عالمنا هذا ، فيحكم بأن وجود هذا العالم ليس للفناء، بل ينبغي أن يكون مؤسسا على الحكمة فيكون بالتالي إنشاء العالم للبقاء لا للفناء. ثم نشاهد بأن العالم بأجمعه مبنى على عناصر تتكون من طبائع مختلفة ووجوه متضادة؛ فالإنسان الذي نعتبره صاحب عقل به يستطيع أن يجمع بين المجتمع ويفرق بين الذي حقه التفريق، وبعبارة أخرى هو الإنسان الذي سمته الحكماء بالعالم الصغير، لو تُرك هو أي هذا العالم الصغير) وما عليه في حاله بناءً على أهواء وطبائع وشهوات لَدَخَل الأفراد والمجتمعات في ساحة التنازع، فيعقب ذلك التفاني والفساد. غير أن النظام المحكم الذي يسود هذا العالم ليس بذلك هو الهدف من وجوده. ويعنى ذلك أن المدبّر الذي أوجد العالم والجنس البشري الذي يعيش بداخله ليس من الحكمة أن يترك تلك المجتمعات من غير مرشد. لذلك، أرسل إليهم رسلاً يثق الناس فيهم وفي دعواهم من ناحية، ومن ناحية أخرى جعل لهؤلاء الرسل دليلاً وبرهانا يعلمون به صدق المرسلين ؛ وأهم دليل في ذلك هو العقل الذي يتميز به الإنسان " . فقد حاول الماتريدي بذلك أن يستدل بأن العقل والنقل مصدران من مصادر المعرفة ودورهما في ذلك.
Abu Mansur al-Maturidi says on the topic of the need for religion that it is necessary to rely on two sources of knowledge, meaning that the foundation by which religion is known has two aspects: one is reason (al-ʿaql), and the other is transmitted tradition (al-samʿ). He holds that human societies have their multiple and different beliefs; there is a group in society that claims that what it believes and relies upon is correct and invites others to it, and that other beliefs are beliefs that correctness opposes. That belief is consequently a social phenomenon that we cannot deny has been transmitted to them from their fathers and forefathers. This means that the transmitted tradition (al-samʿ) that has received complete acceptance in a society has been subjected to the policy of the kings of the earth regarding settling their affairs, and likewise the case of those who claimed messengership and wisdom, and those who managed the various types of craft and professions in society. As for reason (al-ʿaql), which is considered a part of this world of ours, it judges that the existence of this world is not for annihilation, but rather it should be founded upon wisdom, thus the creation of the world is for permanence, not annihilation. Then we observe that the entire world is built upon elements composed of different natures and opposing aspects; man, whom we consider as possessing reason by which he can unite society and separate what deserves separation, in other words man whom the sages have called the small world (al-ʿālam al-ṣaghīr), if he (i.e., this small world) were left as he is based on whims, natures, and desires, individuals and societies would enter into a field of conflict, followed by destruction and corruption. Yet the precise order that prevails in this world is not that—it is the goal of its existence. This means that the Governor (al-mudabbir) who created the world and the human species living within it is not wise to leave those societies without a guide. Therefore, He sent messengers whom people trust in themselves and their claims on one hand, and on the other hand He provided these messengers with a proof and evidence by which the truthfulness of the messengers is known; the most important proof in this is reason, which distinguishes man. Thus, al-Maturidi attempted to argue that reason and transmitted tradition (al-naql) are two sources of knowledge and their role in that.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This is an expository passage in which Maturidi establishes his fundamental epistemology for religious knowledge. He begins by stating that religious knowledge requires two sources: reason (al-ʿaql) and transmitted tradition (al-samʿ). He then observes that societies inherit their beliefs from ancestors, implying that simple transmission without rational scrutiny is not sufficient. Maturidi then turns to reason's judgment about the world: reason sees that the world is created with wisdom, for permanence rather than annihilation. The world's composition from opposing elements and human nature—man called the 'small world'—would lead to conflict if left to whims. Since the world has a precise order, the Creator would not leave societies without guidance. Therefore, He sends messengers, and provides rational proofs of their truthfulness. Maturidi concludes that both reason and transmitted tradition are sources of knowledge, each playing a role. The passage thus establishes a dual-source epistemology: reason and revelation work together.

Maturidi's Three Sources of Knowledge and Sensory Perception
Maturidi rejects false methods and describes sensory perception as a source of necessary knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's classification of reliable sources of knowledge and his defense of sensory perception as a source of necessary knowledge despite its potential errors.

Core Idea
Sensory perception is a reliable source of necessary knowledge, but not infallible.

Before exploring the three sources of knowledge, Maturidi clears the ground by dismissing false methods like personal sensation, inspiration, divination, and physiognomy. He then presents the three valid sources: sensory perception, reports, and reason. In this lesson, we focus on his detailed discussion of sensory perception and how he defends it as a source of necessary knowledge, while acknowledging its occasional errors.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the overall argument. Notice the structure: rejection of false methods, statement of three sources, then focus on sensory perception.
The passage is from Kitab al-Tawhid by Imam al-Maturidi.
2
Identify the false methods rejected
Maturidi begins by listing methods that are not valid for knowledge: personal sensations, inspiration, divination by lot, and physiognomy (face and footprint reading). Understand why these are insufficient.
3
Learn the three valid sources
He then states that the way to know realities is through three sources: sensory perception (al-ʿiyān), reports (al-akhbār), and rational reflection (al-naẓar). Commit these to memory.
4
Explore sensory perception as necessary knowledge
Maturidi explains that sensory perception provides necessary knowledge (ʻilm ḍarūrī) that cannot be doubted. Its opposite is ignorance, and one who denies it is merely stubborn (mukābir).
5
Understand the exceptions and Maturidi's conclusion
He acknowledges that sensory perception can err in cases of defective senses, dreams, or objects that are distant or small. Despite these, he reaffirms his trust in the senses as a valid source of knowledge.
This shows his balanced view: senses are reliable but not infallible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد أشار الماتريدي بأن المناهج التي تعتمد على الأسس المبنية على الأحاسيس الشخصية، أو الإلهام الموهوب الذي لديه أو عملية القرعة، أو الأحكام الصادرة عن خطوط الوجه أو آثار الأقدام، لا تفيد أهمية من ناحية مدارك العلوم فيعنى ذلك في نظره أن السبيل الذي نصل به إلى حقائق الأشياء والأحداث يدل على أن مصادر المعرفة بها ثلاثة : إدراك الحواس (العيان)، والأخبار، والنظر . فالعِيان الذي يعتبر مصدرًا أساسيا في المعرفة، فهو الأصل الذي نصل به إلى العلم بالضرورة، وفيه لا نستطيع أن تتحدث عن جهل يعتبر ضد هذا العلم. فمن ذهب إلى القول بضده من الجهل فهو الذي قال بذلك معاندًا فنسميه مكابرًا. غير أن بعض الأحوال قد تعكس إدراك الحقائق فيكون على غير الذي حسبه المتأمل فيه، مثل كون وسائل الإدراك خاطئة أحيانا نحو المؤوف، أو الذي تنازع المرء نفسه في المنام مثل ما يحدث من الكابوس أثناء النوم، أو كون الجسم المدرَك يبعد عنه أو يدق عن الإحاطة بسبب حجمه الصغير . ومع ذلك نستطيع أن نرى أن أبا منصور الماتريدي، لدى تعرضه لموضوعات عديدة تتعلق بمدارك العلوم، يؤكد ثقته الكاملة لإدراكات الحواس من حين لآخر
Al-Maturidi indicated that the methods that rely on foundations built upon personal sensations, or the gifted inspiration one has, or the process of divination by lot, or judgments issued from facial lines or footprints, are of no importance from the perspective of the sources of knowledge. This means in his view that the way by which we arrive at the realities of things and events indicates that the sources of knowledge are three: sensory perception (al-ʿiyān), reports (al-akhbār), and rational reflection (al-naẓar). Sensory perception (al-ʿiyān), which is considered a fundamental source of knowledge, is the origin by which we arrive at knowledge necessarily (bi-ḍarūra), and in it we cannot speak of ignorance that is opposed to this knowledge. Whoever goes to claim its opposite from ignorance is obstinate, so we call him a stubborn denier (mukābir). However, some circumstances may reverse the perception of realities, so that it is not as the reflector supposed, such as when the means of perception are sometimes faulty, like the defective (al-maʼūf), or when one disputes with oneself in a dream, like what happens from a nightmare during sleep, or when the perceived object is far away or too small to be encompassed due to its small size. Nevertheless, we can see that Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, when discussing many topics related to the sources of knowledge, affirms his complete trust in the perceptions of the senses from time to time.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage is a methodological statement in which Maturidi first dismisses unreliable methods of acquiring knowledge: personal sensations, inspiration, divination by lot, physiognomy, and footprint reading. These yield no real knowledge. He then states the three valid sources: sensory perception (al-ʿiyān), reports (al-akhbār), and rational reflection (al-naẓar). He focuses on sensory perception, explaining that it is a source of necessary knowledge—knowledge that cannot be doubted—and its opposite is ignorance. Anyone who denies it is merely being obstinate (mukābir). However, Maturidi acknowledges situations where sensory perception can be mistaken: faulty senses (e.g., defective eyesight), dreams (e.g., nightmares), or objects that are distant or too small. Despite these exceptions, Maturidi reaffirms his general confidence in sensory perception as a reliable source, consistently across his works.

الأخبار
النظر
الضرورة
المكابر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi considers sensory perception infallible.
Correction: Maturidi acknowledges that senses can err in certain circumstances, yet he still considers them a valid and primary source of necessary knowledge.

Checkpoint

A) A skeptic
B) A mukābir (stubborn denier)
C) An ignorant person
D) A heretic

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The passage emphasizes that sensory knowledge is necessary and its opposite is ignorance. In your daily life, do you act on what you know for certain from your senses and the revealed sources, or do you let doubts and excuses paralyze you? Name one area where you need to move from knowing to doing, and take a step today.

Maturidi's Classification of Knowledge Sources
Maturidi rejects false methods and states the three valid sources of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Maturidi rejects unreliable methods and identifies three valid sources of knowledge: sense perception, reports, and rational inference.

Core Idea
Knowledge comes from senses, reports, and reason—not whims or divination.

Maturidi begins by clearing the ground. He dismisses methods that lack epistemic rigor: personal feelings, inspiration, divination, and physiognomy. He then presents his famous tripartite classification. This passage establishes the foundation for Islamic epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how Maturidi contrasts unreliable methods with the three valid sources.
2
Identify the rejected methods
List the methods Maturidi rejects: personal sensations, gifted inspiration, casting lots, judgments from facial lines and footprints. Why are they unreliable?
These are based on subjective or arbitrary foundations.
3
List the three accepted sources
Maturidi states that the path to truth reveals three sources: sensory perception (ʿiyān), reports (akhbār), and rational inference (naẓar). Write them down.
This tripartite division is foundational in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد أشار الماتريدي بأن المناهج التي تعتمد على الأسس المبنية على الأحاسيس الشخصية، أو الإلهام الموهوب الذي لديه أو عملية القرعة، أو الأحكام الصادرة عن خطوط الوجه أو آثار الأقدام، لا تفيد أهمية من ناحية مدارك العلوم فيعنى ذلك في نظره أن السبيل الذي نصل به إلى حقائق الأشياء والأحداث يدل على أن مصادر المعرفة بها ثلاثة : إدراك الحواس (العيان)، والأخبار، والنظر . فالعِيان الذي يعتبر مصدرًا أساسيا في المعرفة، فهو الأصل الذي نصل به إلى العلم بالضرورة، وفيه لا نستطيع أن نتحدث عن جهل يعتبر ضد هذا العلم. فمن ذهب إلى القول بضده من الجهل فهو الذي قال بذلك معاندًا فنسميه مكابرًا. غير أن بعض الأحوال قد تعكس إدراك الحقائق فيكون على غير الذي حسبه المتأمل فيه، مثل كون وسائل الإدراك خاطئة أحيانا نحو المؤوف، أو الذي تنازع المرء نفسه في المنام مثل ما يحدث من الكابوس أثناء النوم، أو كون الجسم المدرَك يبعد عنه أو يدق عن الإحاطة بسبب حجمه الصغير . ومع ذلك نستطيع أن نرى أن أبا منصور الماتريدي، لدى تعرضه لموضوعات عديدة تتعلق بمدارك العلوم، يؤكد ثقته الكاملة لإدراكات الحواس من حين لآخر
And al-Maturidi indicated that methodologies based on foundations built on personal sensations, or gifted inspiration that one has, or the process of casting lots, or judgments issued from lines of the face or traces of feet, do not yield importance from the standpoint of the means of knowledge. So this means in his view that the path by which we arrive at the truths of things and events indicates that the sources of knowledge are three: sensory perception (ʿiyān), reports (akhbār), and rational inference (naẓar). For ʿiyān, which is considered a fundamental source in knowledge, it is the origin by which we arrive at necessary knowledge, and in it we cannot speak of ignorance that is contrary to this knowledge. So whoever goes to claim its opposite out of ignorance, he is the one who says that as an obstinate opponent, so we call him a stubborn denier (mukābir). However, some conditions may reverse the perception of realities so that it is contrary to what the contemplator assumed, such as when the means of perception are sometimes faulty, like the diseased [eye], or when a person struggles in a dream like what happens from a nightmare during sleep, or when the perceived object is far away or too minute to be encompassed due to its small size. Nevertheless, we can see that Abū Manṣūr al-Māturīdī, when he addresses numerous topics related to the means of knowledge, affirms his complete trust in sense perceptions from time to time.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage presents Maturidi's classification of the sources of knowledge. He first rejects unreliable methods (personal sensation, inspiration, divination, physiognomy, footprint reading). Then he states the three valid sources: sense perception (ʿiyān), reports (khabar), and reasoning (naẓar). He then focuses on ʿiyān, describing it as a source of necessary knowledge (ʿilm ḍarūrī) that leaves no room for ignorance on its own level. He introduces the term mukābir for one who denies obvious perceptual truths out of obstinacy. Then he addresses potential objections: errors can occur due to sensory defects, dreams, distance, or small size. Despite these exceptions, Maturidi reaffirms his confidence in sense perception as a reliable source.

The Nature and Reliability of Sense Perception
Maturidi explains ʿiyān as necessary knowledge, defines mukābir, and addresses sensory errors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Maturidi considers sense perception a source of necessary knowledge, introduces the term mukābir for its obstinate denier, and addresses potential errors without undermining its reliability.

Core Idea
Sense perception gives necessary knowledge; only a stubborn denier (mukābir) rejects it.

Having identified sense perception as one of the three sources, Maturidi now explains its special status. He argues that it yields necessary knowledge—knowledge so certain that ignorance of it is impossible. He then anticipates objections about sensory errors and explains that such errors are due to external factors, not the faculty itself.

Guided Path
1
Understand ʿiyān as necessary knowledge
Read how Maturidi describes ʿiyān as the origin of necessary knowledge, where ignorance is impossible.
Necessary knowledge (ʿilm ḍarūrī) is self-evident, requires no proof.
2
Note the term mukābir
Maturidi introduces the term mukābir for someone who obstinately denies perceptual truths. Why is this significant?
It distinguishes willful denial from genuine ignorance.
3
Examine the exceptions
List the three conditions that can cause perceptual error: faulty sense organs, dreams, distance or small size. How do these affect the reliability?
Errors are due to conditions, not the sense faculty itself.
4
See Maturidi's reaffirmation
Despite these exceptions, Maturidi reaffirms complete trust in sense perception. Why?
He consistently relies on sense perception in his works.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد أشار الماتريدي بأن المناهج التي تعتمد على الأسس المبنية على الأحاسيس الشخصية، أو الإلهام الموهوب الذي لديه أو عملية القرعة، أو الأحكام الصادرة عن خطوط الوجه أو آثار الأقدام، لا تفيد أهمية من ناحية مدارك العلوم فيعنى ذلك في نظره أن السبيل الذي نصل به إلى حقائق الأشياء والأحداث يدل على أن مصادر المعرفة بها ثلاثة : إدراك الحواس (العيان)، والأخبار، والنظر . فالعِيان الذي يعتبر مصدرًا أساسيا في المعرفة، فهو الأصل الذي نصل به إلى العلم بالضرورة، وفيه لا نستطيع أن نتحدث عن جهل يعتبر ضد هذا العلم. فمن ذهب إلى القول بضده من الجهل فهو الذي قال بذلك معاندًا فنسميه مكابرًا. غير أن بعض الأحوال قد تعكس إدراك الحقائق فيكون على غير الذي حسبه المتأمل فيه، مثل كون وسائل الإدراك خاطئة أحيانا نحو المؤوف، أو الذي تنازع المرء نفسه في المنام مثل ما يحدث من الكابوس أثناء النوم، أو كون الجسم المدرَك يبعد عنه أو يدق عن الإحاطة بسبب حجمه الصغير . ومع ذلك نستطيع أن نرى أن أبا منصور الماتريدي، لدى تعرضه لموضوعات عديدة تتعلق بمدارك العلوم، يؤكد ثقته الكاملة لإدراكات الحواس من حين لآخر
And al-Maturidi indicated that methodologies based on foundations built on personal sensations, or gifted inspiration that one has, or the process of casting lots, or judgments issued from lines of the face or traces of feet, do not yield importance from the standpoint of the means of knowledge. So this means in his view that the path by which we arrive at the truths of things and events indicates that the sources of knowledge are three: sensory perception (ʿiyān), reports (akhbār), and rational inference (naẓar). For ʿiyān, which is considered a fundamental source in knowledge, it is the origin by which we arrive at necessary knowledge, and in it we cannot speak of ignorance that is contrary to this knowledge. So whoever goes to claim its opposite out of ignorance, he is the one who says that as an obstinate opponent, so we call him a stubborn denier (mukābir). However, some conditions may reverse the perception of realities so that it is contrary to what the contemplator assumed, such as when the means of perception are sometimes faulty, like the diseased [eye], or when a person struggles in a dream like what happens from a nightmare during sleep, or when the perceived object is far away or too minute to be encompassed due to its small size. Nevertheless, we can see that Abū Manṣūr al-Māturīdī, when he addresses numerous topics related to the means of knowledge, affirms his complete trust in sense perceptions from time to time.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage presents Maturidi's classification of the sources of knowledge. He first rejects unreliable methods (personal sensation, inspiration, divination, physiognomy, footprint reading). Then he states the three valid sources: sense perception (ʿiyān), reports (khabar), and reasoning (naẓar). He then focuses on ʿiyān, describing it as a source of necessary knowledge (ʿilm ḍarūrī) that leaves no room for ignorance on its own level. He introduces the term mukābir for one who denies obvious perceptual truths out of obstinacy. Then he addresses potential objections: errors can occur due to sensory defects, dreams, distance, or small size. Despite these exceptions, Maturidi reaffirms his confidence in sense perception as a reliable source.

Key Terms
العيان
الضرورة
المكابر
المؤوف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Since senses can be mistaken, they are completely unreliable.
Correction: Maturidi shows that errors are due to specific conditions and do not negate the fundamental certainty of sense perception.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the term for someone who obstinately denies the knowledge from sense perception?
A) Jāhil
B) Mukābir
C) Kāfir
D) Dāll

Key Takeaway
Sense perception yields necessary knowledge, and its occasional errors due to special conditions do not undermine its reliability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what area of your life are you most tempted to deny what your senses or reason clearly show because it conflicts with your desires? How can you guard against becoming a mukābir?
?
When you encounter a seeming contradiction between your senses and revelation, do you trust that overall knowledge from sense perception is reliable, or do you let exceptional cases breed doubt? What would change if you fully believed Maturidi's assurance?

Importance and Classification of Khabar
Maturidi defines khabar and classifies it into three types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the significance of khabar as a source of knowledge and its threefold classification according to Maturidi.

Core Idea
Khabar is the basis for knowing our identity and the world; it has three forms.

In this passage, Maturidi introduces the second source of knowledge: reports (khabar). He explains that khabar is how we know our lineage, names, and history. Then he classifies reports into three categories, setting the stage for a deeper discussion of each type.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then the translation.
Note how Maturidi emphasizes the practical role of khabar.
2
Identify the role of khabar
Notice that khabar is essential for knowledge of lineage, name, and essence (māhiyya).
3
List the three types
The passage explicitly states the three types: reports of messengers, mutawātir reports, and solitary reports.
These form the basis for the following discussion.
4
Reflect on the classification
Consider why Maturidi separates reports of messengers as a distinct category.
This relates to their divine support through miracles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الخبر فهو من وسائل المعرفة، وبه يعرف المرء قبل كل شيء نسبه واسمه ومائيته وكذلك العرق الذي ينتمي إليه، ويصل به إلى معرفة اسمه الشخصي وأسماء كل شيء من الخلق، ويؤمن بذلك العلم بمنشأ الحياة البشرية واستدامتها. فالأخبار ثلاثة أنواع: أخبار الرسل أي الأخبار الآتية عن طريق الرسل، والخبر المتواتر أي الخبر الذي ثبت عن طريق التواتر، وخبر الواحد. إن الآيات والمعجزات هي التي توضح صدق الرسل؛ فالأخبار التي تصل إلينا عن طريقها لزم قبولها بضرورة العقل، إذ الخبر الوارد من قبل هؤلاء الرسل متى بلغ درجة التواتر تنبع منه المعرفة الصادقة فالتواتر يعني النظر بالعقل إلى هؤلاء الناقلين للخبر كما وكيفا ، ثم الحكم بصدقه . وكذلك يذهب الماتريدي إلى الاعتراف بأن الإجماع المبنى على اجتهادات العلماء يعتبر في حد التواتر؛ إذ يقول بأن هؤلاء العلماء، رغم انفرادهم بتفرق الهمم واختلافف الأهواء، فإن اتفاقهم في رأي مشترك لن يكون دون لطف الله العلي الذي يملك إظهار حقه وعصمة خلقه فيما شاء. ويعني ذلك أن الخبر المتواتر الحاصل بالإجماع شبيه عنده بأخبار الرسل. وأما عن خبر الواحد الذي يعتبر من أهم الموضوعات التي تطرق إليها الباحثون في علم الكلام والفقه اللذين يشكلان محور العلوم الإسلامية المنشقة فعند الماتريدي ينبغي التعرض له من خلال الاجتهاد والنظر في أحوال الرواة، وكيفية ظاهر محتواه الذي يتضمنه، ووضعه أمام السمع الذي ظهرت قطعية حكمه ؛ ثم يُعمَل بما يغلب عليه وجه العمل أو وجه الترك وإن احتمل الخطأ أحيانًا في الترجيح بينهما. ويعنى ذلك أنه ربما يُعمَل بأسلوب الترجيح هذا في العلم المدرَك بالحواس التي تعتبر أرفع مصادر المعرفة، مثل كون الحواس ضعيفة في الإدراك، أو كون المحسوس بعيدا عن الحاسة، أو كونه قد يخفي عن الإدراك
As for the report (khabar), it is a means of knowledge, and by it a person knows, before all else, his lineage, his name, his essence (māhiyya), as well as the race to which he belongs, and he arrives through it at knowledge of his personal name and the names of everything in creation, and he believes through that knowledge the origin of human life and its continuation. Reports are of three types: the reports of the messengers, i.e., the reports coming through the messengers; the mutawātir report, i.e., the report established through tawātur; and the solitary report (khabar al-wāḥid). Indeed, the signs (āyāt) and miracles (muʿjizāt) are what clarify the truthfulness of the messengers; so the reports that reach us through them must be accepted by necessity of the intellect, for when the report coming from these messengers reaches the level of tawātur, truthful knowledge emanates from it. For tawātur means examining with the intellect these transmitters of the report in terms of quantity and quality, then judging its truthfulness. Likewise, al-Māturīdī holds the view that consensus (ijmāʿ) based on the ijtihād of scholars is considered at the level of tawātur; for he says that these scholars, despite their individuality in the dispersion of aspirations and the difference of inclinations, their agreement on a shared opinion would not be without the grace of God the Most High, who has the power to manifest His truth and protect His creation in what He wills. That means that the mutawātir report obtained through consensus is similar in his view to the reports of the messengers. As for the solitary report (khabar al-wāḥid), which is considered one of the most important topics that researchers in kalām and fiqh—which form the core of the derived Islamic sciences—have addressed, according to al-Māturīdī it should be addressed through ijtihād and examination of the conditions of the transmitters, and the manner of the apparent content that it contains, and placing it before the hearing whose judgment has become definitive; then one acts upon what is likely the stronger aspect of acting or abandoning, although error is sometimes possible in the weighing between them. This means that one might act by this method of weighing in the knowledge perceived by the senses, which are considered the highest sources of knowledge, such as when the senses are weak in perception, or when the sensed object is far from the sense, or when it may be hidden from perception.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage covers the second source: khabar. Maturidi emphasizes its importance for knowledge of identity, lineage, and origins. He classifies khabar into three: reports of messengers (supported by miracles), mutawātir reports (transmitted by so many that collusion on falsehood is impossible), and solitary reports (khabar al-wāḥid). He states that reports from messengers, once they reach tawātur, yield necessary knowledge. Tawātur involves intellectual scrutiny of the transmitters in terms of number and character. Maturidi also equates scholarly consensus (ijmāʿ) based on ijtihād with tawātur, because agreement despite differing aims and inclinations indicates divine grace. For solitary reports, he prescribes ijtihād and careful examination of transmitters and content, then acting on the preponderant assessment, while acknowledging the possibility of error.

Key Terms
الخبر
أخبار الرسل
الخبر المتواتر
خبر الواحد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think khabar is only about religious matters, but Maturidi shows it covers basic human knowledge like name and lineage.
Correction: Khabar is a general source of knowledge, not limited to religion.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, how many types of reports (khabar) are there?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
Maturidi classifies khabar into three types: reports of messengers, mutawātir, and solitary, each with distinct epistemic value.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your name, your lineage, even your sense of your own essence—you did not discover these through reason or experience; you received them through reports. If those reports were false, who would you be? Now consider: the reports of the messengers claim to tell you who you truly are and where you come from. Are you investigating them with the same seriousness as you would if someone doubted your own name?
?
Maturidi states that through khabar we come to believe in 'the origin of human life and its continuation.' If you neglect or dismiss the reports from the messengers, on what basis do you hold any belief about your origin? Is it possible that you have accepted a comfortable story without demanding the chain of transmission? What would it cost you to verify that chain today?
?
You trust reports every day: the names of objects, the history of your family, the news of distant places. But when it comes to the report of messengerhood (risala), do you grant it the same epistemic weight? If not, what is the real reason for that double standard? Could it be that you are unwilling to let a divine report redefine your identity? Are you ready to let it?

Reports of Messengers and Mutawātir
Maturidi explains how miracles authenticate reports and defines tawātur.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how reports from messengers and mutawātir reports yield necessary knowledge through miracles and rational scrutiny.

Core Idea
Miracles authenticate messengers; tawātur guarantees truth through mass transmission.

After classifying reports, Maturidi focuses on the first two types. He explains that the truthfulness of messengers is proven by miracles (āyāt and muʿjizāt), and their reports, when reaching tawātur, produce certain knowledge. He then defines tawātur as rational examination of transmitters in quantity and quality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Focus on the role of miracles and the definition of tawātur.
2
Understand the role of miracles
Miracles are the evidence for the truthfulness of messengers.
They establish the credibility of their reports.
3
Learn the concept of tawātur
Tawātur means examining the transmitters in number and character, then judging the report true.
It leads to necessary knowledge.
4
Connect these two
Reports from messengers must achieve tawātur to yield necessary knowledge.
The combination of miracles and mass transmission ensures certainty.

As for the report (khabar), it is a means of knowledge, and by it a person knows, before all else, his lineage, his name, his essence (māhiyya), as well as the race to which he belongs, and he arrives through it at knowledge of his personal name and the names of everything in creation, and he believes through that knowledge the origin of human life and its continuation. How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Think of a belief you hold about religion that you have never critically examined. Based on this lesson, would that belief pass the tawātur test? If not, what are you going to do about it?
?
