Commentary
This passage describes the editor's approach to punctuation and the handling of multiple viewpoints in the text. The editor first emphasizes the critical role of punctuation (like commas) and diacritical marks in conveying the correct meaning. He notes that missing or extra punctuation can completely reverse the intended meaning, and proper placement removes ambiguity. Then he addresses a common problem: different views or possibilities (وجوه واحتمالات) are often presented without clear separation, or are interwoven, making them appear as a single view. This leads to confusion and contradiction. To resolve this, the editor committed to explicitly identifying each distinct view or probability, using square brackets for insertions within the text or providing clarification in the margins. The editor's method is systematic and deliberate, aiming to present the text as clearly as possible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Editorial additions in brackets are part of the original text.
Correction: They are the editor's clarifications inserted to distinguish viewpoints and should not be mistaken for original wording.

Checkpoint
Why does the editor consider punctuation and diacritics important?
A) To make the text look more professional.
B) Because missing or extra punctuation can reverse the intended meaning.
C) To distinguish between different authors.
D) To fulfill a requirement of the publisher.

Key Takeaway
Careful punctuation and explicit labeling of different viewpoints are crucial for correctly understanding a text that contains multiple voices or interpretations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

If so, what is one specific passage you will revisit to re-examine with the editor's method in mind, and how will you ensure you do not repeat the same mistake?

Editor’s Policy on Grammatical Corrections
Editor's policy on diacritical and grammatical corrections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the editor's systematic approach to correcting grammatical errors while preserving the author's style.

Core Idea
The editor corrects only errors that distort meaning, not stylistic choices.

This passage reveals the careful balance between accuracy and respect for the author's voice. The editor explains his criteria for when to correct and when to leave the text untouched. Understanding this methodology helps us appreciate the trustworthiness of the edited text.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on grammatical corrections
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Focus on the types of errors discussed and the editor's action for each.
The passage uses a conditional structure: if error type X, then action Y.
2
Identify the three categories
Notice the threefold classification: diacritical errors, blatant grammatical errors, and tolerable/stylistic errors. For each, note what the editor does.
The editor's principle is to preserve meaning and the author's style.
3
Reflect on the editor's philosophy
The editor states his role is to transmit the text as the author wrote it, not as he deems better. Consider how this limits editorial intervention.
This distinguishes textual editing from literary revision.

ولم نلتزم بالإشارة إلى نقص أو خطأ ورد في تنقيط النسخة الخطية أو المطبوعة، إذا كان هذا لا ينشئ كلمة ذات معنى ; أما إذا كان هذا الاختلاف ينشئ كلمة يتحملها سياق النص فقد أشرنا إليها في الهامش. وقد قمنا أيضاً بتصحيح الأخطاء النحوية الفاحشة التي لا تتحملها اللغة وقواعدها، ثم أشرنا إليها في الهامش ; إذ لما لاحظنا وقوع كثير من الأخطاء النحوية والصرفية، عملنا على تحرير عبارات الكتاب على ما تقتضيه قواعد اللغة العربية نحوا وصرفًا ففي أحيان كثيرة نجد اتفاقا بين النسخة الخطية والنسخة المطبوعة على تعبير يتضمن خطأ نحويا لا شبهة فيه من رفع منصوب واجب النصب، أو نصب مرفوع واجب الرفع، أو نحو ذلك، أو يتضمن خطأ لغويا كذلك، فنصوب العبارة في صلب الكتاب وننبه إلى ما صنعناه في الهامش. وأما الأخطاء التي تتحملها اللغة أو التي تتعلق بتركيب الجملة، فقد تركناها كما هي حتى لانقضى على أسلوب المؤلف نفسه في طريقة التعبير عن مسائله التي تتعلق بالموضوع ذات اهتمامه. فمن الواضح أن عمل المحقق إنما هو تأدية نص المؤلف إلى القارئ كما صنعه المؤلف، لا كما يستحسنه المحقق . ولاحظنا أنه يشير في بعض الأحيان إلى رأى بعض المخالفين، ويذكر رده عليهم، أو طرفًا من رده; فالتزمنا بالتعرف على رأي المخالفين في مختلف المظان، ثم نربط الرد الذي أورده بذلك الرأي، حتى نزيل عن الكتاب ما قد ينشأ من غموض أو اضطراب، وحرصنا على أن نذكر ذلك في الهامش هذا إلى ما قمنا به من تحرير بعض آرائه العقدية، بالرجوع إلى ما ضمنه سائر مؤلفاته وإلى ما أورده أبو اليسر البزدوي في كتابه أصول الدين، وأبو المعين النسفي في تبصرة الأدلة، ونور الدين الصابوني في كتابيه البداية في أصول الدين و الكفاية في الهداية من تلك الآراء; مع حرصنا على أن لا نتجاوز في ذلك حدود الضروري للتحقيق حتى لا تطغى هذه التعليقات على أصل الكتاب المحقق.
And we did not commit to indicating a deficiency or error that occurred in the diacritics of the manuscript or printed copy, if this does not produce a meaningful word; but if this difference produces a word that the context bears, we indicated it in the margin. And we also corrected the blatant grammatical errors that the language and its rules do not tolerate, then indicated them in the margin; for when we observed the occurrence of many grammatical and morphological errors, we worked to refine the phrases of the book according to what the rules of the Arabic language require in grammar and morphology. For in many instances we find agreement between the manuscript and the printed copy on an expression that contains an unquestionable grammatical error, such as raising a nominative that must be accusative, or nominating an accusative that must be accusative [sic], or similar, or it contains a linguistic error as well, so we correct the expression in the text and alert to what we have done in the margin. As for errors that the language tolerates or that relate to sentence structure, we left them as they are so as not to violate the author's own style in his manner of expressing his issues that relate to his subject of interest. For it is clear that the editor's work is only to deliver the author's text to the reader as the author made it, not as the editor deems good. And we observed that he sometimes refers to an opinion of some opponents, and mentions his refutation of them, or a part of his refutation; so we committed to identifying the opinion of the opponents in various sources, then we link the refutation he mentioned with that opinion, so that we remove from the book any ambiguity or disorder that may arise, and we were careful to mention that in the margin. This is in addition to what we did in editing some of his creedal opinions, by referring to what he included in his other works and to what Abu al-Yusr al-Bazdawi mentioned in his book Usul al-Din, and Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi in Tabṣirat al-Adilla, and Nur al-Din al-Ṣabuni in his two books al-Bidaya fi Usul al-Din and al-Kifaya fi al-Hidaya of those opinions; with our carefulness not to exceed in that the limits of what is necessary for editing so that these comments do not overshadow the original edited book.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage details the editor's policy on grammatical corrections and handling of references to opponents. First, the editor states that minor diacritical errors that don't change meaning are ignored, but if they create a different meaningful word, they are noted in the margin. For clear grammatical errors (e.g., wrong case endings) that are agreed upon between manuscript and printed copy, the editor corrects the text and flags the change in the margin. However, errors that are still grammatically possible or related to style are left untouched to preserve the author's voice. The editor emphasizes his role is to transmit the author's text, not to improve it subjectively. Next, the editor discusses how the author sometimes mentions opponents' views and his refutations. To clarify these, the editor identifies the original sources of those views and links them to the author's refutation, adding notes in the margin. He also corrects and clarifies the author's creedal positions by consulting the author's other works and major Maturidi sources like al-Bazdawi, al-Nasafi, and al-Sabuni, but without overburdening the text with excessive commentary.

Key Terms
تنقيط
أخطاء نحوية
أسلوب المؤلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The editor corrects errors based on personal preference.
Correction: The editor only corrects errors that violate clear grammatical rules; he preserves tolerable and stylistic variations to respect the author's own style.

Checkpoint
How does the editor handle diacritical errors that do not produce a meaningful word?
A) He corrects them and notes the change in the margin.
B) He ignores them.
C) He rewrites the word.
D) He deletes the diacritic.

Key Takeaway
The editor corrects only errors that affect meaning or are grammatically indefensible, leaving tolerable errors to preserve the author's style.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you notice a mistake in someone else's speech or writing, do you correct it immediately? How does this lesson's principle of preserving the author's style challenge your impulse to correct? What would it take for you to hold back, respecting their unique way of expressing themselves?
?
The editor leaves tolerable errors to preserve the author's style. Think of a relationship where you have tried to 'correct' someone's habits or words. What might you be destroying in that person's unique expression? Will you choose to accept some of their 'tolerable errors' starting today?
?
The editor's role is to deliver the text as the author made it, not as he deems good. In your own life, do you ever try to mold others into your ideal of how they should be, rather than accepting them as they are? How can you apply this lesson's principle of faithful delivery to your interactions with family, friends, or colleagues?

Linking Refutations to Opponents’ Views
Editor's method for clarifying opponents' views and editing creedal opinions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the editor clarifies the author's references to opponents and edits creedal opinions using other Maturidi sources.

Core Idea
The editor links refutations to original sources to remove ambiguity.

In this part, the editor explains how he handles the author's mentions of opponents' views and his refutations. He also describes his method for verifying the author's creedal positions against his other works and major Maturidi authorities. This ensures clarity without overwhelming the text.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on opponents’ views and creedal editing
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice two main tasks: linking refutations to opponents' original views, and editing creedal statements by consulting other authoritative sources.
The editor aims to remove ambiguity and disorder.
2
Understand the linking process
The editor identifies the actual opinions of opponents mentioned by the author, then connects the refutation to those opinions. This clarifies what the author is responding to.
This is done in the margin so as not to disturb the text.
3
Examine the use of external sources
The editor edits creedal positions by referring to the author's other works and to works by al-Bazdawi, al-Nasafi, and al-Sabuni. He limits his commentary to what is necessary.
The goal is to avoid overshadowing the original text.

Editorial Conventions in the Critical Edition
Editor explains his use of quotation marks, brackets, and double footnotes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the editor's use of quotation marks, curly brackets, and double footnotes in the critical edition, and how they help the reader engage critically with the text.

Core Idea
Curly brackets {} signal phrases the editor considers not original to the author.

This lesson explains the editorial methodology used in preparing this critical edition. Understanding these conventions—quotation marks, curly brackets, and double footnotes—allows you to read the edition with insight, distinguishing between the original text and later additions, and to appreciate the editor's effort to present a reliable text.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three conventions
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Look for the editor's discussion of quotation marks, curly brackets, and double footnotes. Notice the specific purpose of each.
The editor starts with quotation marks, then addresses the suspect phrases, then describes footnotes.
2
Understand the purpose of curly brackets
The editor uses curly brackets {} to enclose phrases like 'said the Sheikh' that he believes were added by the scribe or copyist, not the author. This is a key editorial judgment.
Curly brackets are not for general insertions but specifically for words the editor suspects are not original.
3
Explore the double footnote system
The editor arranged footnotes in two sets: one for textual variants (furooq) and one for comments (ta'liqat). He used signs for missing (-) and extra (+) text. However, he did not differentiate the numbering for these two types, leaving the reader to distinguish them.
This system aims to involve the reader actively in textual criticism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد استعملنا أحيانًا في المتن علامات التنصيص من فاصلتين مزدوجتين، توجد بينهما العبارة المعنية في النص ..... ; فهي علامة تفصل بين الكلام المقتبس عن الغير وذلك من قبل المؤلف في النص، كما تستعمل في إبراز بعض الكلمات في المتن وإظهارها . هنا وقد لاحظنا ورود عبارة قال الشيخ رحمه الله ، أو قال الشيخ أبو منصور رحمه الله، أو قال الفقيه رحمه الله في صلب الكتاب فرأينا أن هذا القول ليس معقولاً صدوره عن المؤلف حتى لو كان ممليا، وإنما هو إما من صنع المملى عليه أو من صنع الناسخ فيما بعد. ومن رأينا أن نضع هذه العبارة أو تلك بين قوسين معقوفين {} مع التنبيه هنا إلى ذلك. وأما عن الهوامش التي وُجدت في أسفل كل صفحة أثناء التحقيق، فقد رتبناها في صورة هوامش مزدوجة; فأشرنا فيها إلى الفروق، كما وضعنا فيها التعليقات اللازمة لشرح بعض ما ورد في النص وتوثيقه. غير أننا لم نفرق في نوعية أرقام الهامش المزدوج هذا فيما يتعلق بالتفريق بين الفروق والتعليقات، وتركنا للقارئ التنبه بها تسهيلاً له في المنظر العام للهوامش . وقد أشرنا في الهامش إلى الكلمة أو العبارة الناقصة في إحدى النسختين الخطية أو المطبوعة بإشارة ، وإلى الكلمة أو العبارة الزائدة بإشارة + ; ثم وضعنا بعد كل منهما الكلمة أو العبارة فقد كنا نستحسن أن يكون كل أولئك في أسفل كل صفحة تيسيرًا للقارئ الدارس الذي ينبغي أن يكون ناقداً، غير متأثر برأي غيره أو وجهة نظره ; إذ المفروض في أغلب الكتب المحققة أن يكون القارئ في درجة التبصر، وفي طبقة رفيعة من تحرّر الفكر، فيشارك هو بنفسه أثناء القراءة أغلب الخطوات العلمية التي مر بها المحقق. وكذلك نرجو أن يكون الأسلوب هذا قد وضع كليهما في مجلس واحد، يتنافسان في تثبيت النص، ويتشاركان من مستوى واحد فيما يخص نص الكتاب وموضوعه وما يتعلق به من مسائل علمية بحتة . عالية من ومثال ذلك كالآتي: ۱ وبه قال أبو الحسن الأشعري وعبد الله بن سعيد. انظر: مجرد مقالات، ٤٨ . ٢ ك: وجوده. ه سورة البقرة، ۱۸۹/۲. ٤ م + عليه السلام. وقد سبق التعريف به ص ۳۷ . ٦ ر - (عليه السلام) صح هـ . OV م - فاذا جاز أن يقول.
And we have sometimes used in the text quotation marks of double separators, between which is the relevant phrase in the text ..... ; it is a sign that separates the speech quoted from others and that by the author in the text, and it is also used to highlight some words in the text and display them. Here, and we observed the occurrence of the phrase 'said the Sheikh, may God have mercy on him', or 'said Sheikh Abu Mansur, may God have mercy on him', or 'said the Faqih, may God have mercy on him' in the body of the book, and we saw that this statement is not reasonably attributable to the author even if he was dictating, but rather it is either the work of the one being dictated to or the work of the copyist afterwards. And it is our opinion to put this phrase or that between curly brackets {} with a note to that effect here. As for the footnotes that were found at the bottom of each page during the editing, we arranged them in the form of double footnotes; we indicated in them the differences, and we placed in them the necessary comments to explain some of what occurred in the text and document it. However, we did not differentiate in the type of the number of this double footnote regarding the distinction between differences and comments, and we left it to the reader to be aware of them to facilitate him in the general appearance of the footnotes. And we indicated in the margin the missing word or phrase in one of the two manuscripts or the printed copy with a sign, and the additional word or phrase with a sign + ; then we placed after each the word or phrase. For we used to prefer that all that be at the bottom of each page to facilitate the studying reader who should be critical, not influenced by someone else's opinion or viewpoint; for it is assumed in most edited books that the reader is at a level of insight, and at a high level of free thought, so that he himself participates during reading most of the scientific steps that the editor went through. And likewise we hope that this method has placed both of them in one council, competing in establishing the text, and sharing from one level concerning the text of the book and its subject and what relates to it of purely scientific issues. High from And an example of that is as follows: 1. And with it said Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari and Abd Allah ibn Sa'id. See: Mujarrad Maqalat, 48. 2. K: wujuduhu. H Surah al-Baqarah, 2/189. 4 M + 'alayhi al-salam. And it has been previously introduced on p. 37. 6 R - ('alayhi al-salam) ṣaḥḥa H. OV M - fa-idha jaza an yaqul.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage explains the editor's typographical conventions and the structure of footnotes. First, the editor mentions using double quotation marks to set off quoted material and to highlight certain words in the text. He then addresses a specific issue: phrases like 'said the Sheikh' appear in the text, which the editor believes could not have originated from the author (even if dictating), so they are enclosed in curly brackets {} to indicate editorial suspicion. Next, he describes the arrangement of footnotes as 'double footnotes' (هوامش مزدوجة): one set for textual variants (فروق) and another for explanatory comments (تعليقات). However, he did not use different numbering systems for these two types; instead, he leaves it to the reader to discern them. He also uses signs: to indicate a missing word and + for an extra word in the margin. The editor expresses his ideal that the reader should be an active critic, engaging with the editorial process. The passage ends with an example of such footnotes, though the example contains unclear symbols and numbers likely from the manuscript. The editor hopes that his method places the editor and reader in a collaborative relationship.

Key Terms
علامات التنصيص
قوسين معقوفين
هوامش مزدوجة
فروق
تعليقات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Curly brackets are used for general editorial insertions or clarifications.
Correction: In this edition, curly brackets specifically mark phrases the editor considers not original to the author, such as 'said the Sheikh'.

Checkpoint
How does the editor indicate a phrase he believes is not original to the author?
A) He deletes it without comment.
B) He places it between curly brackets {} with a note.
C) He moves it to the margin.
D) He writes it in red ink.

Key Takeaway
The editor uses clear typographical signals—quotation marks, curly brackets, and double footnotes—to guide the reader through textual variants and editorial judgments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you open an edited book, do you trust the editor's choices without question, or do you actively examine the footnotes and textual signs as the editor intended? What would it take for you to become that 'critical reader' he describes?
?
The editor hopes that the reader 'participates himself during reading most of the scientific steps that the editor went through.' Have you ever approached a text with such an investigative mindset? How might your understanding of this book change if you engaged with its variants and comments as partners in establishing the truth?
?
The editor removed phrases like 'said the Sheikh' because he judged them inauthentic. In your own learning, do you accept inherited phrases or traditions at face value, or do you scrutinize their origins? What is at stake if you fail to question?

Knowledge Over Taqlid: Maturidi's Rational Foundation
Maturidi argues against taqlid in favor of rational proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Maturidi argues that religious knowledge must be based on rational proof rather than blind imitation of tradition.

Core Idea
Truth in religion demands proof, not inherited custom.

This is the opening argument of Imam Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid. He confronts the reality of sectarian disagreement and asks: if every group claims truth, how can one know which is correct? His answer: only rational proof (hujja 'aqliyya) can provide certainty. Blind imitation (taqlid) is not a valid path to religious knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage (even though it is heavily corrupted in the manuscript) and its literal translation. Notice Maturidi's opening style: praise to Allah followed by a direct statement of the issue.
The Arabic text here is from a corrupt manuscript; the meaning is reconstructed from context and commentary.
2
Identify the problem of sectarianism
Maturidi describes the existence of many sects, each claiming to follow the truth while considering others false. This contradictory state shows that truth cannot be determined by mere affiliation.
He highlights that their agreement on disagreement indicates the need for a criterion beyond tradition.
3
Grasp his solution: rational proof
The passage asserts that one must have a rational proof (hujja 'aqliyya) to verify the truth of a claim. Taqlid, which lacks certainty, is invalid in foundational matters of religion.
Rational proof is necessary for certitude, which is required for true iman.

In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. O Lord, complete with good. Praise be to Allah, to whom every good is good without reference to reality? This brings blessings and success in what follows, and we ask Him for grace upon those with whom He seals the message, and upon the brothers of the messengers, and upon all His saints. And we seek refuge with Him from the evil of approaching Him? Then: the first statement of al-Shaykh Abu Mansur, may Allah have mercy on him: 'Now then, ... (unclear)... and action follows? (unclear)... that taqlid (blind imitation) is not permissible, because it is clear that... (unclear)... And there is no power except with Allah, the Exalted. Then the foundation by which religion is known: indeed, there must be for these people a religion that they agree upon, and its foundation is the branches which have two aspects: one is benefit and the other is reason. ... (unclear)...
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is a methodological introduction by Imam Maturidi (Abu Mansur al-Maturidi) laying out the necessity of knowledge based on proof rather than unthinking acceptance (taqlid) in matters of religion. He begins with the basmala and hamdalah, then states that the first point (al-qawl al-awwal) is that despite the existence of many differing sects, each claims to follow the truth while considering others false. However, their very disagreement and mutual exclusion indicate that truth cannot be attained simply by following authority (taqlid). Rather, one must have a rational proof (hujja 'aqliyya) to ascertain the truthfulness of a claim. He emphasizes that religion has foundations (usul) which are known by reason and revelation, and that blind imitation is invalid because it does not provide certainty. The passage thus establishes that the correct approach to religion is to seek demonstrative proof, not to rely on inherited customs or sectarian affiliation.

Key Terms
التقليد
الحجة العقلية
الأصول
الفروع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Maturidi rejects all forms of following scholars or tradition.
Correction: Maturidi only rejects taqlid that lacks proof and contradicts reason. Following a scholar who provides rational proofs is acceptable, as long as one understands the basis.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi in this passage, what is the basis for knowing the truth in religion?
A) Following the majority of scholars
B) Blind imitation (taqlid) of ancestors
C) Rational proof (hujja 'aqliyya) and certainty
D) Emotional inspiration

Key Takeaway
Maturidi establishes that religious truth must be verified by rational proof, not accepted by blind imitation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever truly examined why you believe what you believe, or are you simply comfortable because your parents and community taught it to you? What would it cost you to seek rational proof for your deepest convictions, and are you willing to pay that price?
?
Maturidi says that taqlid is invalid because it lacks certainty. Can you honestly name one core belief of yours that you hold based on reason and evidence rather than upbringing or fear of doubt? If you cannot, what does that mean for the strength of your faith?
?
The sects in Maturidi's argument all claim truth yet reject each other. Do you ever dismiss the beliefs of others without first understanding and rationally evaluating their proofs? How would you feel if someone did that to you? What will you do differently after today?

The Opening: The Problem of Sectarian Claims and Taqlid
Maturidi opens with praise, then states the problem of sectarian claims and taqlid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the problem that motivated Imam Maturidi to write his book: the universal claim of each sect to exclusive truth and their reliance on blind imitation (taqlid) rather than evidence.

Core Idea
Every sect claims truth for itself and falsehood for others—but all rely on imitation, not evidence.

Imam Maturidi opens his Kitab al-Tawhid with a paradox: despite their conflicting claims, all religious sects share two things—each believes its own doctrine is true, and each follows a predecessor without proof. This observation sets the stage for his entire project: to establish that true knowledge of religion must come from evidence, not blind taqlid.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening doxology and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Notice the formal tone and the praise of Allah, followed by the prayer on the Prophet and other messengers.
This is the author's customary opening, but it also serves to frame the work as a religious endeavor.
2
Identify the observation about sects
After the doxology, Maturidi states his observation: people are divided into many sects, yet they all agree on one thing—each considers its own way true and others false. Reflect on why this is a problem.
3
Note the introduction of taqlid
Maturidi then points out that each sect has a predecessor (salaf) whom they imitate (taqlid). This sets up the core issue: reliance on authority without evidence leads to conflict and uncertainty.
The argument is cut off, but the direction is clear: taqlid is invalid as a basis for religious truth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And we ask Him to bless the one with whom He sealed the message, and his brothers among the messengers, and all His saints. And we seek protection with Him from slipping, and we desire from Him that which honors us of speech and action. [The obligation of knowing religion through evidence] Shaykh Abu Mansur, may Allah have mercy on him, said: 'Now then, we have found people differing in sects in religion, agreeing, despite their disagreement in religion, on one word: that what they themselves follow is true and what others follow is false; with the agreement of all of them that each of them has a predecessor whom they imitate.' Commentary
This passage is the opening of Kitab al-Tawhid. It begins with a doxology praising Allah and sending blessings on the Prophet and other messengers. Then the author, Imam Maturidi, states the problem: people are divided into various religious sects, yet they all agree on one thing—each believes its own doctrine is true and others' are false. They also agree that each has a predecessor (salaf) whom they blindly follow (taqlid). The author then begins to argue that this kind of taqlid is invalid, though the argument is cut off at the page break. The passage sets the stage for the need to derive religious knowledge from evidence rather than mere imitation.

Imam Maturidi opens his book by highlighting that all sects claim truth for themselves and falsehood for others, yet all rely on blind imitation (taqlid) rather than evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What would it cost you to honestly examine just one of them today?
?
Maturidi points out that every group has a predecessor they follow without question. Who are the 'predecessors' in your life—teachers, scholars, or family—whose views you have never critically evaluated? Will you take the risk of asking 'why' about one of their teachings this week?
?
The passage reveals a universal human tendency: we assume we are right and others wrong, yet we rarely verify our own foundations. Are you willing to subject your most comfortable belief to the same standard of evidence you expect from others? What specific step can you take to move from taqlid to conviction rooted in proof?

The Invalidity of Taqlid and the Necessity of Rational Proof
Maturidi argues that taqlid is inexcusable and only rational proof establishes truth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that blind imitation (taqlid) is not a valid excuse in matters of religious truth and why only rational proof establishes certainty.

Core Idea
Blind imitation cannot justify religious truth—only rational proof compels the intellect.

In this passage, Imam al-Maturidi confronts a common human tendency: following the religion of one's ancestors without question. He observes that people of different faiths all claim truth, yet each imitates predecessors. He argues that this cannot be a valid excuse, because the opposite could also claim the same. Instead, true knowledge of religion must come through rational proof that compels the intellect. This lesson unpacks his powerful reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage thoughtfully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Let Maturidi's own words and argument flow without interruption. This will give you a sense of his style and the logical progression.
Notice the structure: observation, problem, solution.
2
Identify Maturidi's observation
Maturidi starts by noting that people differ in religion yet each believes his own is true. They also agree that each follows a predecessor. What does this imply?
3
Understand the argument against taqlid
He deduces that taqlid cannot be an excuse because if it were, then the opposite side would also be excused, leading to contradiction. Taqlid only gives numbers, not truth.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
4
See the exception: rational proof
The only valid justification is when the original authority (prophet) has a rational proof. This proof compels the intellect and distinguishes truth from falsehood.
Maturidi insists that such proof is available and accessible.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that it is impossible for similar proofs to exist for opposing religions, and each person is obligated to seek knowledge of truth through evidence.
Think about how this applies to your own learning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ أبو منصور رحمه الله : أما بعد فإنا وجدنا الناس مختلفي المذاهب في النحل، في الدين، متفقين على اختلافهم في الدين على كلمة واحدة: أن الذي هو عليه حق والذي عليه غيره باطل؛ على اتفاق جملتهم في أن كلا منهم له سلف يُقلد. فثبت أن التقليد ليس مما يُعذر صاحبه لإصابة مثله ضده. على أنه ليس فيه سوى كثرة العدد؛ اللهم إلا أن يكون لأحد ممن ينتهي القول إليه حجة عقل يُعلم بها صدقه فيما يدعى، وبرهان يُقهم المنصفين على إصابته الحق. فمن إليه مرجعه في الدين بما يوجب تحقيقه عنه فهو المحق؛ وعلى كل واحد منهم معرفة الحق فيما يدين هو به كأن الذي دان به هو مع أدلة صدقه وشهادة الحق له قد حصرهم، إذ منتهى حجج كل منهم ما يضطر العقول إلى التسليم له لو ظفر بها؛ وقد ظهرت لمن ذكرت. ولا يجوز ظهور مثلها لضده في الدين، لما تتناقض حجج العقل بعد ما غلبت حججه وأظهر تموية أسباب الشبه في غيره. ولا قوة إلا بالله العظيم.
The Sheikh Abu Mansur, may God have mercy on him, said: To proceed: We have found people differing in schools of thought concerning religious communities, in religion, agreeing despite their differences in religion on one word: that what they themselves follow is true and what others follow is false; with the agreement of all of them that each of them has a predecessor whom they imitate. Thus it is established that imitation (taqlid) is not something for which its practitioner is excused for attaining the like of its opposite. Moreover, it contains nothing but multiplicity of numbers. Unless there is for one of those to whom the statement ultimately goes a rational proof (hujja 'aqliyya) by which his truthfulness in what he claims is known, and a demonstration (burhan) that compels the fair-minded to his correctness of the truth. So whoever is the one to whom reference in religion is made by that which necessitates its verification from him is the correct one; and it is incumbent upon each one of them to know the truth in what he himself practices, as if the one who practices it, along with the proofs of his truthfulness and the testimony of the truth for him, has encompassed them, since the utmost of the proofs of each of them is that which compels the intellects to submission if they were to attain it; and it has appeared to those I mentioned. It is not permissible for its like to appear for its opposite in religion, because the proofs of the intellect are contradictory after his proofs have prevailed and he has made apparent the dissimulation of the causes of doubts in others. There is no power except through God, the Mighty.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage is an argument rejecting blind imitation (taqlid) and establishing the necessity of rational proof in religion. Maturidi begins by observing that people have different religions, yet each claims his own is true and others false. They also agree that each has a predecessor they imitate. From this, he deduces that taqlid is not a valid excuse because if it were, then the opposite could also be excused, leading to contradiction. Taqlid only provides quantity, not truth. The only exception is when the person to whom the statement ultimately goes (the prophet or the original source) possesses a rational proof and a compelling demonstration. That person is the one who is correct, and all others must seek knowledge of truth through proof. Maturidi emphasizes that the proofs of each religion ultimately compel the intellect if obtained, and these proofs have appeared to the one he mentioned (the prophet). It is impossible for similar proofs to appear for the opposite religion because that would lead to contradiction between intellectual proofs. He concludes with a phrase of reliance on God.

Key Terms
تقليد (taqlid)
حجة عقل (hujja 'aqliyya)
برهان (burhan)
عقل (aql)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That taqlid is acceptable as long as one follows a reputable scholar.
Correction: Maturidi argues that taqlid is inexcusable because if it were valid, then the opposite could also claim the same excuse, leading to contradiction. The only valid basis is rational proof.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi conclude about those who rely solely on taqlid (imitation) in religion?
A) They are excused because they follow their forefathers.
B) They are not excused because their opposite could also claim the same excuse.
C) They are correct if they follow a majority.
D) They are correct only if they follow a prophet.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi establishes that religious truth cannot be based on imitation; only compelling rational proof validates belief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever sincerely examined the rational evidence for your belief in God and the truth of Islam, or are you simply following what you were raised on? If you haven't, what is stopping you from doing so today?
?
If you rely on taqlid, consider this: if you had been born into a different religion, you would likely imitate its scholars and consider your current beliefs false. Does that possibility shake your certainty? How will you move from imitation to conviction based on proof?
?
You hold your religion to be true and others false. But if you have never verified the rational proof behind your own beliefs, how do you know you are not just like those you consider misguided? Will you commit today to learning at least one rational proof for your faith from the sources?

The Two Foundations of Religion: Revelation and Reason
Maturidi identifies hearing (sam') and intellect (aql) as the two sources for knowing religion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Maturidi identifies two fundamental sources for knowing religion: hearing (revelation) and intellect (reason), and that both are necessary.

Core Idea
Religion rests on two pillars: revelation and reason.

Before diving into specific doctrines, Maturidi establishes the epistemological foundations of religion itself. He asserts that every human being must have a basis for their religious beliefs, and that basis comes from two sources: the transmitted message (hearing) and rational reflection (intellect). In this passage, he introduces these two foundations and begins by discussing the universal reliance on hearing.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two sources
The passage begins by stating that religion requires a foundation. Find where Maturidi lists the two aspects.
Look for the words 'وجهان'.
2
Understand the argument about hearing
Maturidi argues that no human being is free from relying on hearing, even skeptics. Reflect on why this is significant.
He shows that transmitted knowledge is unavoidable.
3
Recognize the implication for intellect
Although the passage cuts off, note that intellect is named as the second source. Expect that Maturidi will argue that reason is also necessary.
This sets up the discussion of the relationship between revelation and reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم أصل ما يُعرف به الدين - إذ لا بد أن يكون لهذا الخلق دين يلزمهم الاجتماع عليه وأصل يلزمهم الفزع إليه - وجهان؛ أحدهما السمع، والآخر العقل. أما السمع فمما لا يخلو بشر من انتحاله مذهبًا يعتمد عليه ويدعو غيره إليه، حتى شاركهم في ذلك أصحاب الشكوك والتجاهل فضلاً عن الذي يُقر بوجود الأشياء وتحقيقها،
Then the foundation by which religion is known - since it is necessary for these creatures to have a religion that obliges them to agree upon it and a foundation to which they must resort - are two aspects: one is hearing (sam'), and the other is intellect (aql). As for hearing, no human being is free from adopting it as a school of thought to rely upon and to call others to, to the extent that the people of skepticism and ignorance share in that, let alone one who affirms the existence of things and their reality.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage is a classification. Maturidi states that religion is known through two fundamental sources: hearing (sam', meaning revelation transmitted through scripture and prophets) and intellect (aql, rational reasoning). He argues that both are necessary. Then he discusses hearing, noting that no human being can avoid relying on some transmitted tradition, even skeptics and the ignorant, let alone those who accept the reality of things. This shows the universality of reliance on transmitted knowledge. (The text cuts off but likely continues with intellect.)

Key Terms
أصل (asl)
السمع (al-sam')
العقل (al-aql)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That religion can be known exclusively through reason without revelation, or exclusively through revelation without reason; Maturidi affirms both as necessary foundations.
Correction: Maturidi shows that both hearing (revealed knowledge) and intellect (rational reasoning) are essential foundations for religion.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what are the two foundations for knowing religion?
A) Prophethood and miracles
B) Hearing (sam') and intellect (aql)
C) Consensus and analogy
D) The Qur'an and Sunnah

Key Takeaway
Maturidi establishes that religion is founded on both transmitted revelation (hearing) and rational intellect, and begins by showing that no one can escape reliance on transmitted knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily practice of Islam, do you rely more on what you have heard from others or on your own reasoning? Identify one specific teaching you follow without understanding its rational basis, and commit to studying its wisdom using both revelation and intellect.
?
If even skeptics depend on transmitted knowledge, what does that reveal about your own need for teachers and scholars? How will you ensure that your learning combines humble acceptance of revelation with active intellectual engagement?
?
Maturidi says no one is free from relying on hearing. What is one belief you hold about your faith that you have only accepted because you heard it, and what step will you take this week to examine it with your intellect while staying rooted in revelation?

Truth is Known by Rational Proof, Not by Numbers
Maturidi argues that truth is determined by decisive rational proof, not by numerical majority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Maturidi considers rational proof, rather than majority opinion, as the decisive criterion for truth in religious matters.

Core Idea
Truth is determined by compelling rational proof, not by the number of followers.

Maturidi confronts a common assumption: that truth can be judged by the number of people who hold a belief. In this passage, he sets a different standard—one grounded in reason. He argues that the decisive factor is a rational proof (ḥujja ʿaqliyya) that compels any fair-minded person to accept it. Let's explore his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice Maturidi's confident and decisive tone.
2
Translate and consider the meaning
Read the English translation and ensure you understand the main claim: truth is not about numbers, but about proof.
3
Identify the criterion for truth
Find where Maturidi states what makes someone correct: 'a rational proof by which his truthfulness is known' and 'a proof that compels minds to submission.'
This is the core of the argument.
4
Reflect on why numbers are insufficient
Consider why Maturidi rejects numerical majority as a criterion. The passage implies that truth is not a popularity contest.
Think about how this applies to contemporary debates.

ليس مما يُعذر صاحبه لإصابة مثله ضده . على أنه ليس فيه سوى كثرة العدد؛ اللهم إلا أن يكون لأحد ممن ينتهي القول إليه حجة عقل يُعلم بها صدقه فيما يدعى، وبرهان يُقهم المنصفين على إصابته الحق. فمَن إليه مرجعه في الدين بما يوجب تحقيقه عنه فهو المحق؛ وعلى كل واحد منهم معرفة الحق فيما يدين هو به كأن الذي دان به هو مع أدلة صدقه وشهادة الحق له قد حصرهم ، إذ منتهى حجج كل منهم ما يضطر العقول إلى التسليم له لو ظفر بها؛ وقد ظهرت لمن ذكرت . ولا يجوز ظهور مثلها لضده في الدين، لما تتناقض حجج العقل بعد ما غلبت حججه وأظهر تموية أسباب الشُّبه في غيره. ولا قوة إلا بالله العظيم.
It is not something for which its adherent is excused for hitting its opposite. Rather, it contains only multiplicity of number; unless there is for one of those to whom the statement is traced a rational proof by which his truthfulness in what he claims is known, and a proof that compels the impartial to the correctness of the truth. So the one to whom reference is made in religion, with what necessitates its verification from him, he is the truthful one; and upon every one of them is the knowledge of the truth in what he himself practices as religion, as if the one who practices it is, with the proofs of his truthfulness and the witness of the truth for him, has encompassed them, for the ultimate of the proofs of each of them is that which compels minds to submission if they were to obtain it; and it has appeared to those whom I mentioned. And it is not permissible for its like to appear to its opposite in religion, because the proofs of reason contradict after his proofs have prevailed and he has manifested the confusing of the causes of doubts in others. And there is no power except with Allah the Almighty.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage presents an argument about the criterion for truth in religion. Maturidi states that truth is not determined by the mere multiplicity of followers. Rather, the one who has a rational proof (ḥujja 'aqliyya) that demonstrates his truthfulness is the one who is correct. The proof must compel rational minds (al-ʿuqūl) to submission. Such a proof cannot appear to the opposite position because that would lead to contradiction among rational proofs. The passage emphasizes that the decisive factor is the strength of the proof, not the number of adherents.

Key Terms
حجة
عقل
برهان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That truth in religion is determined by majority opinion or numbers.
Correction: Maturidi explicitly states that truth is known by a rational proof that compels minds, not by the number of adherents.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the criterion for truth in religion?
A) Majority opinion
B) Rational proof that compels minds
C) Consensus of scholars
D) Following one's ancestors

Key Takeaway
Truth in religion is established by rational proof that compels minds, not by the number of adherents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted a belief about Islam simply because it was popular or widely held, without demanding the rational proof that Maturidi insists upon? Which belief is that, and will you examine it today?
?
Maturidi says the truthful person has a proof that compels impartial minds. If you were to present your core religious beliefs to a neutral, rational judge, would your proof hold up? If not, what is the one belief you are least able to defend rationally?
?
The passage warns that rational proofs for falsehood cannot truly exist because they contradict the truth. Are you willing to accept that your own doubts or objections might stem from a lack of understanding, not from a valid counter-proof? Will you commit to seeking that understanding starting now?

The Two Foundations of Religion: Hearing and Reason
Maturidi identifies hearing (revelation) and reason as the two origins of religious knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Maturidi identifies hearing (revelation) and reason as the two essential foundations for all religious knowledge.

Core Idea
Revelation and reason together form the twin pillars of religious knowledge.

Maturidi begins his theological work by establishing that religion requires a foundation. He argues that this foundation is twofold: transmitted revelation (samʿ) and rational inquiry (ʿaql). This passage sets the stage for his entire method of theology, grounding it in both divine communication and human intellect.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: Maturidi first states a necessity, then gives the two aspects, then elaborates on hearing.
The passage is short but packed with meaning.
2
Identify the two foundations
The passage explicitly says there are two 'wajhan' (aspects): al-samʿ and al-ʿaql. Pinpoint where each is mentioned and how Maturidi describes them.
3
Reflect on the universal practice argument
Maturidi supports the foundation of hearing by pointing out that all humans, even skeptics, rely on some transmitted school. Consider how kings, prophets, and craftsmen all depend on received teachings.
This shows that hearing is not just for religion but is universal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كون السمع والعقل أصلين يُعرف بهما الدين. ثم أصل ما يُعرف به الدين - إذ لا بد أن يكون لهذا الخلق دين يلزمهم الاجتماع عليه وأصل يلزمهم الفزع إليه - وجهان ؛ أحدهما السمع، والآخر العقل. أما السمع فمما لا يخلو بشر من انتحاله مذهبًا يعتمد عليه ويدعو غيره إليه، حتى شاركهم في ذلك أصحاب الشكوك والتجاهل فضلاً عن الذي يُقر بوجود الأشياء وتحقيقها، على ذلك جرت سياسة ملوك الأرض من سيرة كل منهم على ما راموا تسوية أمورهم عليه وتأليف ما بين قلوب رعيتهم به ؛ وكذلك أمر الذين ادعوا الرسالة والحكمة، ومن قام بتدبير أنواع الصناعة. وبالله المعونة والنجاة.
Hearing (al-samʿ) and reason (al-ʿaql) are two foundations by which religion is known. Then the foundation by which religion is known – since there must necessarily be for this creation a religion that obligates them to unite upon it and a foundation that obligates them to have recourse to it – is two aspects: one is hearing (al-samʿ), and the other is reason (al-ʿaql). As for hearing, no human being is free from adopting a madhhab (school) that he relies upon and calls others to, such that even the people of skepticism and ignorance share in that, let alone the one who affirms the existence of things and their verification. Upon that the policy of the kings of the earth proceeded, from the conduct of each of them upon what they aimed to settle their affairs and to unite the hearts of their subjects; and likewise the matter of those who claimed prophethood and wisdom, and those who undertook the management of the various types of crafts. And with Allah is assistance and salvation.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage classifies the two foundational sources of religious knowledge: hearing (al-samʿ, i.e., transmitted revelation) and reason (al-ʿaql). Maturidi states that every human adopts some school (madhhab) based on hearing, even skeptics, and this is illustrated by the universal practice of kings, prophets, and craftsmen who rely on received teachings. The passage establishes that religion must have both a revealed source and rational foundation.

Key Terms
سمع
عقل
أصل
مذهب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Revelation (samʿ) is sufficient on its own and does not need reason.
Correction: Maturidi places both hearing and reason as foundational, showing that religion requires both transmitted revelation and rational thought.

Checkpoint
What are the two foundations of religious knowledge according to this passage?
A) Hearing (samʿ) and reason (ʿaql)
B) Intuition and revelation
C) Philosophy and science
D) Scripture and tradition

Key Takeaway
Maturidi establishes that religion cannot be known through revelation alone or reason alone; both are necessary foundations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you rely solely on your own reason without seeking guidance from revelation? When has that led you astray? What step will you take today to submit your intellect to what Allah has revealed?
?
You follow a madhhab in your daily life—your habits, your sources of knowledge. Is the madhhab of your life truly based on the two foundations Maturidi describes, or are you unknowingly following the skeptics and kings? Reflect on one area where your practical beliefs contradict this lesson.
?
If hearing (revelation) is universal, even among skeptics, what does that say about your own reliance on transmitted knowledge? Do you accept religious teachings simply because of tradition, or have you engaged your reason to affirm them? What will you do to ensure both foundations are active in your understanding of religion?

The Failure of Subjectivism and Inspiration as Sources of Knowledge
Maturidi refutes two false epistemologies and explains why they fail.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Maturidi rejects subjective feelings and inspiration as reliable sources of knowledge.

Core Idea
Subjective feelings and inspiration cannot be sources of truth because they lead to contradictions.

Imam Maturidi begins by identifying two common but flawed approaches to knowing what is true and good. The first relies on personal sentiment, the second on claimed inspiration. He shows that both fail the test of consistency, and thus cannot be trusted as paths to knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage identifying the false views
Locate the two opinions Maturidi reports: one based on what occurs in the heart, the other based on inspiration. Note how each is presented.
These are the views Maturidi will refute.
2
Understand the refutation's core argument
Maturidi argues that these views lead to endless contradictions because different people claim contradictory things. If falsehood can appear in the same form as truth, you cannot trust such experiences.
The key phrase: 'falsehood is pictured in the same form as truth.'
3
Examine the analogies to lottery and tracking
Maturidi compares these false methods to drawing lots (qur‘a) and the skill of a tracker (qā’if). Both produce varying results and thus cannot be reliable causes of truth.
This reinforces the argument that these methods are not objective.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that these two views cannot be causes of knowledge. This establishes the need for a more objective epistemology.
The phrase 'فلم يجز أن يكونا سببي الحق' is the key conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : ثم اختلف في الأسباب التي بها يُعلم المصالح والحق والمحاسن من أضدادها. فمنهم من يقول : ما يقع في قلب كل منهم حسنه لزمه التمسك به. ومنهم من يقول : يعجز البشر عن الإحاطة بالسبب، ولكن يتمسك بما ألهم، لما يكون ذلك ممن له تدبير العالم. قال الشيخ رحمه الله : وهما بعيدان من أن يكونا من أسباب المعرفة، لأن وجوه التضاد والتناقض في الأديان بين، ثم عند كل واحد منهم أنه المحق. ومحال أن يكون سبب الحق يعمل هذا العمل، لما تصوّر الباطل بنفس صورة الحق؛ فمحال الثقة بمن ظهر كَذِبُه كلَّ ما كان معتقداً لمذهب باعتقاد الحق بما ذكرت عند ضده، وفي إلهامه أنه هذا الظهور. مع مبطل . ولم يكن لواحد منهما دليل غير الذي لآخر في خطابه ؛ وذلك نوع ما لا يدفع الاختلاف والتضادَّ اللذين بهما التفاني. وعلى ذلك يبطل إعلام القرعة فيما يعجز عنه ذو العقل، ولم يُجْعَل في الحكم الجَبْرُ على الرّضا، إذ هي تخرج مختلفاً ؛ وكذلك أمر القائف. فلم يجز أن يكونا سببي الحق. ولا قوة إلا بالله. قال الشيخ أبو منصور رحمه الله : ثم السبيل التي يُوصل بها إلى العلم بحقائق الأشياء العيان والأخبار والنظر .
Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: Then they differed concerning the causes (asbāb) by which the benefits, truth, and good things are known in contrast to their opposites. Some of them say: What occurs in the heart of each of them as good, it is incumbent upon him to adhere to it. Others say: Humans are incapable of encompassing the cause, but they adhere to what they are inspired, because that is from the One who has the governance of the world. The Shaykh (may God have mercy on him) said: Both are far from being among the causes of knowledge, because the aspects of contradiction and opposition in religions are evident, yet each of them claims that he is the truth. It is impossible that the cause of truth should produce this effect, when falsehood is pictured in the same form as truth; therefore, it is impossible to trust one whose falsehood has appeared, whenever he believes in a school of thought by believing in the truth as I mentioned concerning his opposite, and in his inspiration that it is this appearance. And neither of them has a proof different from the other in his discourse; that is a type that does not repel the difference and opposition which lead to mutual destruction. And upon that, the information of the lottery (qur‘a) is invalidated in what the intelligent person is incapable of, and compulsion (jabr) to satisfaction is not made in the judgment, since it produces differing results; and likewise the matter of the tracker (qā’if). So it is not permissible for them to be causes of truth. And there is no power except with God. Then Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: Then the paths by which knowledge of the realities of things is attained are: direct observation (al-‘iyān), reports (al-akhbār), and reasoning (al-naẓar).
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of two false epistemologies and a classification of the true sources of knowledge. Abu Mansur (Imam Maturidi) first reports two incorrect views: (1) that knowledge of good and truth comes from what occurs in one's heart, and (2) that humans cannot attain the cause but must rely on inspiration as it comes from the Divine. Maturidi refutes both, arguing that contradictions among different claimants show that these cannot be reliable causes of truth. If falsehood can appear in the same form as truth, then trust cannot be placed in such subjective experiences. He further compares these to practices like casting lots (qur‘a) and tracking (qā’if), which produce divergent results and thus cannot be valid means to truth. Therefore, these two views cannot be causes of knowledge. Finally, he states the three correct paths to knowledge: direct observation (al-‘iyān, encompassing all senses), transmitted reports (al-akhbār), and reasoning (al-naẓar).

Key Terms
أسباب المعرفة (asbāb al-ma‘rifa)
إلهام (ilhām)
قرعة (qur‘a)
قائف (qā’if)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Personal intuition or inspiration can be a valid source of religious knowledge.
Correction: Maturidi shows that such subjective sources lead to contradictions and cannot be trusted.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi reject the view that what occurs in the heart is a cause of knowledge?
A) Because it is too subjective and varies between people
B) Because it leads to contradictions and falsehood can appear as truth
C) Because it is not mentioned in the Quran
D) Because it relies on the senses

Key Takeaway
Subjective feelings and inspiration cannot be reliable sources of knowledge because they lead to irresolvable contradictions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel strongly that something is true in your heart, have you ever considered that someone else feels just as strongly about the opposite? How can you know your feeling is not merely a product of your desires or environment? Will you commit to testing your convictions against the objective proofs of revelation and reason rather than trusting your own certainty?
?
If you claim that God inspires you in a matter, how do you distinguish that from your own thoughts or from what others also claim as inspiration? Are you willing to subject your inspiration to the criteria of the scholars like Maturidi, who show that inspiration without proof leads to confusion and destruction? What step will you take today to ground your spiritual experiences in sound knowledge?
?
This lesson shows that subjective feelings and inspirations can be deceptive. Think about a time when you were absolutely sure of something based on a spiritual experience, but later realized you were mistaken. What does that tell you about the reliability of your own heart? What will you now rely on to find the truth?

The Three Valid Paths to Knowledge: Observation, Reports, and Reasoning
Maturidi enumerates the three valid sources of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three sources of knowledge according to Imam Maturidi: direct observation, reliable reports, and sound reasoning.

Core Idea
True knowledge comes from observation, testimony, and reasoning — not from inner feelings or inspiration.

Having refuted subjective epistemologies, Maturidi now states the positive foundation. He identifies three and only three paths by which we can attain certain knowledge of realities. This classification is central to Sunni epistemology and frames all subsequent theological inquiry.

Guided Path
1
Read the final sentence carefully
Maturidi says: 'Then the paths by which knowledge of the realities of things is attained are: direct observation, reports, and reasoning.' Memorize these three.
The Arabic terms: al-‘iyān, al-akhbār, al-naẓar.
2
Understand 'direct observation' (al-‘iyān)
This refers to sense perception — seeing, hearing, etc. It is the most immediate form of knowledge.
It includes all five senses, but primarily sight as the strongest.
3
Understand 'reports' (al-akhbār)
These are transmitted statements, especially from trustworthy sources. Maturidi elsewhere distinguishes between mutawatir (mass-transmitted) and ahad (single) reports.
This is the basis for accepting prophetic traditions.
4
Understand 'reasoning' (al-naẓar)
This is rational inference — using the intellect to deduce conclusions from premises. It includes analogical reasoning and logical proofs.
Reasoning is necessary for knowledge of God and abstract truths.
5
Reflect on why these three are sufficient
Every claim to knowledge must be reducible to one of these three. They are foundational and comprehensive.
Note that Maturidi does not include intuition or inspiration here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : ثم اختلف في الأسباب التي بها يُعلم المصالح والحق والمحاسن من أضدادها. فمنهم من يقول : ما يقع في قلب كل منهم حسنه لزمه التمسك به. ومنهم من يقول : يعجز البشر عن الإحاطة بالسبب، ولكن يتمسك بما ألهم، لما يكون ذلك ممن له تدبير العالم. قال الشيخ رحمه الله : وهما بعيدان من أن يكونا من أسباب المعرفة، لأن وجوه التضاد والتناقض في الأديان بين، ثم عند كل واحد منهم أنه المحق. ومحال أن يكون سبب الحق يعمل هذا العمل، لما تصوّر الباطل بنفس صورة الحق؛ فمحال الثقة بمن ظهر كَذِبُه كلَّ ما كان معتقداً لمذهب باعتقاد الحق بما ذكرت عند ضده، وفي إلهامه أنه هذا الظهور. مع مبطل . ولم يكن لواحد منهما دليل غير الذي لآخر في خطابه ؛ وذلك نوع ما لا يدفع الاختلاف والتضادَّ اللذين بهما التفاني. وعلى ذلك يبطل إعلام القرعة فيما يعجز عنه ذو العقل، ولم يُجْعَل في الحكم الجَبْرُ على الرّضا، إذ هي تخرج مختلفاً ؛ وكذلك أمر القائف. فلم يجز أن يكونا سببي الحق. ولا قوة إلا بالله. قال الشيخ أبو منصور رحمه الله : ثم السبيل التي يُوصل بها إلى العلم بحقائق الأشياء العيان والأخبار والنظر .
Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: Then they differed concerning the causes (asbāb) by which the benefits, truth, and good things are known in contrast to their opposites. Some of them say: What occurs in the heart of each of them as good, it is incumbent upon him to adhere to it. Others say: Humans are incapable of encompassing the cause, but they adhere to what they are inspired, because that is from the One who has the governance of the world. The Shaykh (may God have mercy on him) said: Both are far from being among the causes of knowledge, because the aspects of contradiction and opposition in religions are evident, yet each of them claims that he is the truth. It is impossible that the cause of truth should produce this effect, when falsehood is pictured in the same form as truth; therefore, it is impossible to trust one whose falsehood has appeared, whenever he believes in a school of thought by believing in the truth as I mentioned concerning his opposite, and in his inspiration that it is this appearance. And upon that, the information of the lottery (qur‘a) is invalidated in what the intelligent person is incapable of, and compulsion (jabr) to satisfaction is not made in the judgment, since it produces differing results; and likewise the matter of the tracker (qā’if). So it is not permissible for them to be causes of truth. And there is no power except with God. Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of two false epistemologies and a classification of the true sources of knowledge. Abu Mansur (Imam Maturidi) first reports two incorrect views: (1) that knowledge of good and truth comes from what occurs in one's heart, and (2) that humans cannot attain the cause but must rely on inspiration as it comes from the Divine. Key Takeaway
The three paths to knowledge are direct observation, reliable reports, and sound reasoning — each playing a distinct role in acquiring certainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own beliefs, which of these three paths do you rely on most? Are you neglecting one of them? How might that imbalance affect the certainty of your faith?
?
Consider a conviction you hold about God that you have not personally observed, has not been reliably reported, and you have not reasoned through carefully. Is it truly knowledge? Would you be willing to examine it against Maturidi’s criteria?
?
If you were to be honest with yourself, do you sometimes accept religious claims simply because they feel inspiring or come from a respected figure, without verifying whether they meet the standard of reliable reports? How can you change that?

Why Inner Feelings and Inspiration Are Not Sources of Knowledge
Maturidi refutes the claim that inner feelings or inspiration are valid sources of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Maturidi argues that inner feelings and inspiration cannot be valid sources of knowledge, and how he extends this argument to practices like casting lots.

Core Idea
Truth cannot be based on subjective feelings that contradict each other.

Imam Maturidi addresses a fundamental methodological question: how do we know what is true and beneficial? Some claimed that whatever occurs in the heart or is received through inspiration is sufficient. In this passage, the Imam demonstrates why such claims collapse under logical scrutiny, using the undeniable fact of religious contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Let the author's argument unfold naturally.
2
Identify the two views criticized
The passage mentions two groups: those who rely on what occurs in the heart, and those who rely on inspiration. Note their claims.
3
Understand Maturidi's main refutation
Focus on the contradiction argument: if each person follows their own heart or inspiration, they will disagree, so it cannot be a universal criterion for truth.
4
See the extension to other practices
Maturidi extends the same logic to casting lots and tracking, showing they also lead to contradictory results.
5
Reflect on the underlying principle
Consider why subjective and contradictory methods cannot be reliable sources of knowledge. This reinforces the need for objective criteria.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اختلف في الأسباب التي بها يُعلم المصالح والحق والمحاسن من أضدادها. فمنهم من يقول : ما يقع في قلب كل منهم حسنه لزمه التمسك به. ومنهم من يقول : يعجز البشر عن الإحاطة بالسبب، ولكن يتمسك بما ألهم، لما يكون ذلك ممن له تدبير العالم. قال الشيخ رحمه الله : وهما بعيدان من أن يكونا من أسباب المعرفة، لأن وجوه التضاد والتناقض في الأديان بين، ثم عند كل واحد منهم أنه المحق. ومحال أن يكون سبب الحق يعمل هذا العمل، لما تصوّر الباطل بنفس صورة الحق؛ فمحال الثقة بمن ظهر كَذِبُه كلَّ ما كان معتقداً لمذهب باعتقاد الحق بما ذكرت عند ضده، وفي إلهامه أنه هذا الظهور. مع مبطل . ولم يكن لواحد منهما دليل غير الذي لآخر في خطابه ؛ وذلك نوع ما لا يدفع الاختلاف والتضادَّ اللذين بهما التفاني. وعلى ذلك يبطل إعلام القرعة فيما يعجز عنه ذو العقل، ولم يُجْعَل في الحكم الجَبْرُ على الرّضا، إذ هي تخرج مختلفاً ؛ وكذلك أمر القائف. فلم يجز أن يكونا . الحق. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Then they differed regarding the means by which benefits, truth, and good things are known from their opposites. Among them are those who say: what occurs in the heart of each of them as good, it is incumbent upon him to adhere to it. And among them are those who say: human beings are incapable of encompassing the cause, but they adhere to what they are inspired with, because that is from the One who manages the world. The shaykh said: Both are far from being means of knowledge, because the aspects of opposition and contradiction in religions are clear, and then each one of them considers himself the one who is correct. And it is impossible for the cause of truth to work this way, since falsehood takes the same form as truth; so it is impossible to trust someone whose lying has become apparent, whenever he is a follower of a doctrine believing it to be truth, as I mentioned regarding his opponent—and in his inspiration is that this appears—with a falsehood. And neither of them had a proof other than what the other had in his speech; that is a type that does not repel the difference and contradiction which lead to mutual destruction. And likewise, the indication of the lot (qur‘a) is invalidated in matters that the intelligent person cannot grasp, and compulsion to satisfaction is not made in judgment, since it comes out differently; and likewise the matter of the tracker (qa’if). So it is not permissible for them to be the truth. There is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of two false views regarding the sources of knowledge. Imam Maturidi first reports two opinions: some say that whatever occurs in one's heart as good must be followed; others say that humans cannot grasp the cause but should adhere to inspiration from the manager of the world. The shaykh rebuts both: (1) If each religion claims its own inner feeling or inspiration, they contradict each other, so they cannot all be true. (2) Falsehood can appear in the same form as truth, so one cannot trust a person whose lies have become manifest. (3) Neither has a proof superior to the other. Then he extends the argument to other methods like casting lots or following tracks, which also lead to contradictory results. Therefore, these are not valid means of knowledge.

Key Terms
أسباب المعرفة
إلهام
وقوع في القلب
قرعة
قائف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi rejects all subjective experiences as sources of knowledge.
Correction: He rejects them only as independent criteria for truth, not as personal experiences.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi reject the idea that what occurs in the heart can be a source of knowledge?
A) Because it contradicts revelation
B) Because people differ in what occurs in their hearts, leading to contradictions
C) Because the heart is not reliable
D) Because it is not mentioned in the Quran

Key Takeaway
Inner feelings and inspiration cannot serve as universal criteria for truth because they lead to contradictions and cannot distinguish truth from falsehood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel strongly that something is right, do you ever consider that the same feeling could be experienced by someone with a conflicting belief? How does this passage challenge the way you have relied on your own feelings to justify actions?
?
Is there any belief you hold that you have never subjected to external scrutiny, relying instead on an inner conviction? After reading this, what would it take for you to honestly test that belief?
?
Maturidi says falsehood can take the same form as truth. In your life, where might you be mistaking a sincere feeling for the truth? What proof do you have beyond your own heart?

The Three Valid Means of Knowledge According to Maturidi
Maturidi enumerates the three valid means of attaining knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Imam Maturidi identifies exactly three valid means of attaining knowledge of realities: sensory perception, reports, and reasoning.

Core Idea
Knowledge of reality comes only through sensory perception, reports, and reasoning.

Imam Maturidi begins his discussion of epistemology by stating the fundamental paths to knowledge. This concise statement establishes the framework for all subsequent theological argumentation. Before we can discuss matters of creed, we must agree on how we know what we know.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage slowly. Notice that Maturidi uses the word 'then' (thumma) to indicate that this follows from his earlier discussion of knowledge.
The passage is a single sentence, so read it carefully.
2
Identify the three categories
List the three Arabic terms: al-‘iyan, al-akhbar, al-nazar. Think about what each means in English.
These are the foundational categories.
3
Reflect on the completeness
Consider why Maturidi does not include intuition (dhawq) or mystical unveiling. The commentary will explain.
This is key to understanding his rationalist approach.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ أبو منصور رحمه الله : ثم السبيل التي يُوصل بها إلى العلم بحقائق الأشياء العيان والأخبار والنظر .
Then the paths by which one arrives at knowledge of the realities of things are sensory perception, reports, and reasoning.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage is a concise statement by Imam Maturidi enumerating the three valid means of attaining knowledge. He lists them as al-‘iyan (sensory perception), al-akhbar (reports), and al-nazar (reasoning). These are the foundational epistemological categories in his system.

Key Terms
عيان
أخبار
نظر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that intuition or mystic enlightenment is a fourth valid source of knowledge.
Correction: Maturidi explicitly limits valid sources to three: sensory perception, reports, and reasoning. Intuition is not a reliable means of certain knowledge.

Checkpoint

A) Sensory perception
B) Reports
C) Reasoning
D) Intuition

Key Takeaway
Maturidi establishes a rational epistemology: only sensory perception, reliable reports, and sound reasoning can yield certain knowledge of realities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you accepted a piece of knowledge based on intuition rather than one of these three paths? How did that affect your understanding, and what will you do differently now?
?
Which of these three paths—sensory perception, reports, or reasoning—do you tend to neglect in your daily pursuit of truth? Describe one concrete step you will take this week to strengthen that path in your life.
?
Think of a belief you hold that you have never examined against the three paths Maturidi outlines. What is the belief, and how will you verify it through sensory evidence, reliable reports, or sound reasoning starting today?
