The Certainty of Sensory Perception
Maturidi defines sensory perception and argues its necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that sensory perception is a foundational and undeniable source of knowledge, recognized even by animals.

Core Idea
Sensory perception is the bedrock of knowledge, known even by animals.

Before we can argue about deeper theological matters, we must establish the reliability of our basic means of knowledge. Maturidi begins by defining sensory perception as the foundation of knowledge that has no opposite of ignorance. He then appeals to the universal testimony of animal nature to show that denying the senses is unnatural and unreasonable.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of al-‘iyan
Read the passage where Maturidi defines sensory perception as what the senses perceive and calls it the foundation of knowledge. Notice that he states it has no opposite of ignorance.
This sets up the absolute certainty of sensory knowledge.
2
Examine the argument from animals
Maturidi points out that even animals rely on their senses for survival, pleasure, and pain. Reflect on why this strengthens the case for sensory certainty.
If animals, which lack reason, use senses, then denying them is beneath even animals.
3
Understand the agreement not to argue
The passage concludes that scholars agree not to debate such a denier because he cannot even affirm his own denial or his own existence. Consider why this makes argument futile.
This sets the stage for alternative refutation methods in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالعيان ما يقع عليه الحواس، وهو الأصل الذي لديه العلم الذي لا ضدّ له من الجهل. فمن قال بضده من الجهل فهو الذي يُسمَّي مُنكِرَه كل سامع مكابِرًا؛ تأبى طبيعة البهائم أن يكون ذلك رتبتها؛ إذ كل منها يعلم ما به بقاؤها وفناؤها وما يتلذذ به ويتألّم، وصاحب هذا يُنكر ذلك. وأجْمِعَ أن لا يُناظر مع مَنْ كان ذلك قوله، إذ لا يُثبت إنكاره ولا حضوره بنفسه ؛ والمناظرة في مائية الشيء أو هستيته ، وهو لهما وللدفع جميعا دافع . لكنه يُمازح ، فيقال له : «تعلم بأنك تنفي؟». فإن قال : «لا»، بطل نفيه، وإن قال: «نعم»، أثبت نفيه، فيصير بما يدفع دافعا لدفعه. ويُؤلَم بالألم الشديد من قطع الجوارح ليدع تعنّته، إذ نحن نعلم أنه يعلم العيان إذ هو علم الضرورة، ولكنه يقوله متعنتا ، وحق مثله ما ذكرت ليجزع ويضجر، فيُقابل بتعنّت مثله فينهتك لديه ستره. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Sensory perception is what is perceived by the senses, and it is the foundation that possesses knowledge that has no opposite of ignorance. Whoever says its opposite of ignorance is called by every hearer a stubborn denier; the nature of animals refuses that to be their rank; since each of them knows what is its survival and destruction and what it enjoys and suffers, and the proponent of this denies that. And it is agreed that one should not argue with someone who holds that view, because he does not affirm his denial nor his own presence himself; and argumentation about the quiddity of a thing or its existence—he is a repeller of both and of repulsion. But one may joke with him and say: 'Do you know that you are denying?' If he says 'No,' his denial is void; if he says 'Yes,' he has affirmed his denial, thus he becomes by what he repels a repeller of his repulsion. And he is caused severe pain by the cutting of limbs to leave his stubbornness, for we know that he knows sensory perception because it is necessary knowledge, but he says it stubbornly. The treatment for someone like him is what I mentioned so that he may be distressed and annoyed, and he is confronted with stubbornness like his so that his veil is torn. There is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage is a detailed defense of sensory perception as a source of certain knowledge. Maturidi defines al-‘iyan as what the senses perceive, calling it the foundation of knowledge that has no opposite of ignorance. He argues that to deny the senses is to be a stubborn denier, since even animals rely on their senses for survival. The one who denies sensory perception is so unreasonable that scholars agree not to debate him, because he cannot affirm his own denial or his own existence. Maturidi then proposes a logical trick: ask him if he knows he is denying. If he says no, his denial is invalid; if he says yes, he has affirmed his denial, which is self-contradictory. Furthermore, such a denier should be subjected to physical pain to force him to abandon his stubbornness, since we know he has necessary sensory knowledge but is obstinate. The goal is to expose his absurdity.

Key Terms
عيان
علم الضرورة
مكابرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the foundation that possesses knowledge with no opposite of ignorance?
A) Reason
B) Revelation
C) Sensory perception
D) Intuition

Key Takeaway
Sensory perception is a necessary and undeniable source of knowledge, recognized even by animals.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly rely on your senses as a gift from Allah, or do you sometimes doubt the clear truths they reveal about His creation? How does that doubt weaken your certainty in the signs of Allah around you? What step will you take today to strengthen your trust in the evident knowledge Allah has given you?
?
When you meet someone who denies the obvious—like the existence of the Creator through the order of the universe—are you quick to engage in heated debate? Maturidi teaches that such denial is stubbornness and that debate is futile. Can you recognize when your efforts are wasted, and instead turn to Allah in prayer for guidance?
?
If even animals know what preserves and harms them through their senses, how much more accountable are you for using your senses to recognize your Lord's commands? Are you using your sight, hearing, and touch to draw closer to Allah or to disobey Him? What will you change today?

Refuting the Denier of Sensory Perception
Maturidi provides two refutation methods for the denier of sensory perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's two methods for refuting a stubborn denier of sensory perception: a logical trick and the use of physical pain to expose his contradiction.

Core Idea
The denier of senses is refuted by logical self-contradiction and practical exposure.

Having established that sensory perception is certain, Maturidi now turns to the specific case of someone who stubbornly denies it. He presents two approaches: a clever logical question that forces a self-contradiction, and a physical method that exploits the denier's own necessary knowledge. The goal is to strip away his pretense and make his stubbornness collapse.

Guided Path
1
Read the logical trick
Maturidi proposes a joking question: 'Do you know that you are denying?' Analyze how both possible answers lead to the denier's defeat.
If he says no, his denial is invalid; if he says yes, he affirms his own denial, contradicting himself.
2
Examine the physical method
Maturidi then suggests inflicting physical pain (cutting limbs) to force the denier to abandon stubbornness. Notice that he justifies this by saying we know the denier has necessary sensory knowledge but is obstinate.
This is not punishment but a method to expose his inherent knowledge.
3
Understand the purpose
The aim is to distress the denier and tear his veil of stubbornness. Reflect on how this aligns with the earlier agreement not to debate him.
The denier is treated with a similar stubbornness to break his pretense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالعيان ما يقع عليه الحواس، وهو الأصل الذي لديه العلم الذي لا ضدّ له من الجهل. فمن قال بضده من الجهل فهو الذي يُسمَّي مُنكِرَه كل سامع مكابِرًا؛ تأبى طبيعة البهائم أن يكون ذلك رتبتها؛ إذ كل منها يعلم ما به بقاؤها وفناؤها وما يتلذذ به ويتألّم، وصاحب هذا يُنكر ذلك. وأجْمِعَ أن لا يُناظر مع مَنْ كان ذلك قوله، إذ لا يُثبت إنكاره ولا حضوره بنفسه ؛ والمناظرة في مائية الشيء أو هستيته ، وهو لهما وللدفع جميعا دافع . لكنه يُمازح ، فيقال له : «تعلم بأنك تنفي؟». فإن قال : «لا»، بطل نفيه، وإن قال: «نعم»، أثبت نفيه، فيصير بما يدفع دافعا لدفعه. ويُؤلَم بالألم الشديد من قطع الجوارح ليدع تعنّته، إذ نحن نعلم أنه يعلم العيان إذ هو علم الضرورة، ولكنه يقوله متعنتا ، وحق مثله ما ذكرت ليجزع ويضجر، فيُقابل بتعنّت مثله فينهتك لديه ستره. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Sensory perception is what is perceived by the senses, and it is the foundation that possesses knowledge that has no opposite of ignorance. And it is agreed that one should not argue with someone who holds that view, because he does not affirm his denial nor his own presence himself; and argumentation about the quiddity of a thing or its existence—he is a repeller of both and of repulsion. But one may joke with him and say: 'Do you know that you are denying?' And he is caused severe pain by the cutting of limbs to leave his stubbornness, for we know that he knows sensory perception because it is necessary knowledge, but he says it stubbornly. The treatment for someone like him is what I mentioned so that he may be distressed and annoyed, and he is confronted with stubbornness like his so that his veil is torn. There is no power except through Allah.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed defense of sensory perception as a source of certain knowledge. Maturidi defines al-‘iyan as what the senses perceive, calling it the foundation of knowledge that has no opposite of ignorance. The Self-Defeating Nature of Denying Reports
Maturidi argues that denying reports is self-contradictory and leads to ignorance and ingratitude.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why denying all reports is self-contradictory and leads to ignorance and ingratitude.

Core Idea
Denying reports is like sawing off the branch you're sitting on.

Maturidi's argument for the necessity of reports is not just a theoretical point — it strikes at the very possibility of communication and knowledge. In this passage, he shows that anyone who tries to deny all reports ends up denying their own denial, because the denial itself is a report. This sets off a chain of absurd consequences, from ignorance of one's own identity to ingratitude for the blessings of speech and hearing. Let's work through his logic step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of reports
Read the first sentence. Maturidi says reports are of two types. What are they? He doesn't specify here, but his argument targets those who deny both types entirely.
Later in the book he may specify, but here the focus is on total denial.
2
Trace the self-contradiction argument
Notice how Maturidi shows that denying reports is self-defeating: the denial itself is a report. Then consider the consequences: ignorance of lineage, name, essence, and the inability to inform others of what one has seen.
Why is this important? It shows that reports are foundational to human knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ رحمه الله : والأخبار نوعان؛ من أنكر جملته لحق بالفريق الأول، لأنه أنكر إنكاره، إذ إنكاره خبر ، فيصير مُنكِرًا عند إنكاره إنكاره . مع ما فيه جهلُ نَسبه واسمه ومائيته واسم جوهره واسم كل شيء، فيجب به جهل محسوس وعجزه عن أن يُخبر عن شيء عاينه إذا خبر به. فكيف يبلغ هو إلى العلم بما يبلغه مما غاب عنه، أو متى يعلم ما به معاشه وغذاؤه، وكل ذلك يصل إليه بالخبر . مع ما فيه الكفران بعظيم نعم الله عليه، وبأصل ما حُمِد هو به وبما فُضَلَ به على البهائم من النطق والتميز بالسمع، وذلك نهاية المكابرة.
Reports are of two types; whoever denies them entirely joins the first group, because he denies his own denial, for his denial is a report. Thus he becomes a denier at the time of his denial of his denial. Along with what is in it of ignorance of his lineage, his name, his quiddity, the name of his substance, and the name of everything. So by it is necessitated ignorance of the sensible and his inability to inform about anything he has witnessed if he reports it. How then can he attain knowledge of what reaches him from what is absent from him? Or when does he know his livelihood and nourishment, all of which reach him through reports? Along with what is in it of ingratitude for the great blessings of Allah upon him, and for the basis by which he is praised and by which he was favored over animals—speech and distinction through hearing. That is the utmost stubbornness.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage argues for the necessity of reports (akhbar) as a source of knowledge. Maturidi states that reports are of two types (implying perhaps prophetic and non-prophetic, but he does not elaborate here). He then refutes the one who denies all reports: such a person joins the first group (of those who deny sensory perception) because his own denial is itself a report (a statement). So he contradicts himself. Moreover, without reports, one would be ignorant of his lineage, name, essence, and everything; even sensory knowledge cannot be communicated without reports. One cannot know absent matters or even how to sustain himself without reports. Denying reports also amounts to ingratitude to Allah for the blessings of speech and hearing that distinguish humans. Thus, denial of reports is extreme stubbornness.

Key Terms
أخبار
النطق
السمع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One can rationally deny the validity of all reports as sources of knowledge.
Correction: Maturidi shows that such denial is self-refuting because the denial itself is a report, and also leads to absurd consequences like ignorance of one's own identity.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi consider denying reports to be an act of ingratitude?
A) Because it rejects the messenger
B) Because it denies the blessing of speech and hearing by which reports are transmitted
C) Because it leads to ignorance
D) Because it is mentioned in the Quran

Key Takeaway
Denying reports is self-defeating and undermines all human knowledge and gratitude.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you rejected a report (e.g., a news story, a testimony, or even a piece of Islamic knowledge) simply because it didn't align with your desires. How does Maturidi's argument that denial is itself a report expose the inconsistency in your heart? What step will you take this week to humbly accept a report you have been resisting?
?
You rely on reports every day: your name, your family history, the food you eat, the news you hear. If you deny the value of reports, you deny the means by which you know your very identity. What does this reveal about your attitude toward the countless reports from Allah—such as the Quran and the teachings of the Prophet? Are you truly grateful for the blessing of hearing and speech, or have you taken them for granted? Pledge one concrete way you will honor these blessings today.
?
Consider the phrase 'utmost stubbornness' (mukabara). When you reject a truth that reaches you through reliable reports—whether about your own self or about Allah—you are not just being skeptical; you are being stubborn against the very structure of knowledge and gratitude. Is there an area of your life where you are stubbornly refusing to accept what has been clearly conveyed to you through trustworthy sources? What would it cost you to surrender that stubbornness, and what would you gain?

Dialectical Method for Deniers of Reports and Senses
Maturidi provides a dialectical method to refute deniers of reports and sensory perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's dialectical trick to expose the self-contradiction of those who deny reports or sensory perception.

Core Idea
Even obstinate deniers reveal their reliance on reports when they speak.

Maturidi presents a clever method for dealing with those who reject the validity of reports (khabar) or direct sensory observation (ʿiyān). Since these deniers often engage in obstinate refusal (taʿannut), serious debate may be futile. Instead, a simple question—'What do you say?'—uncovers their self-contradiction. This lesson explores the method and its rationale.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's style come through.
Notice the practical, almost playful tone—Maturidi frames this as a jest.
2
Identify the two categories of deniers
The passage addresses two types: the denier of reports (al-khabar) and the denier of sensory perception (al-ʿiyān). Find where each is discussed.
The method for each is similar but with a distinct final option for the denier of senses.
3
Understand the dialectical trick
The trick is to ask the denier 'What do you say?' If he repeats his denial, he has implicitly accepted the medium (a report or hearing). If he does not, you are free from his harm.
The repetition shows that he engaged in reporting or hearing, which contradicts his denial.
4
Reflect on the deeper point
Consider why this method works: even deniers must use the very tools they deny. The method exposes their inconsistency.
This reinforces that reports and sensory perception are fundamental to human discourse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله: ثم لا يُوصل بهما إلى إدراك المحاسن والمساوي التي يعمل العقول على الإحاطة بها إلا باستعمال الألسن بالتكلم بها وإدناء السمع إليها. وحق مناظرة منكر هذا - وإن كانت مناظرته سفها - أن يُمازح أيضاً، فتقول له عند إنكاره الخبر: ما تقول؟ فإن عاد إليه فاعلم أنه قَبِلَ خبرك حيث عاد إلى قوله وهو استعادتك الخبر، وإن لم يعد إليه كُفيت شره وحمدت الله وضحكت منه. ومثله لمن ينكر العيان، تقول له: «ما تقول؟» فإن عاد إليه ظهر لك أنه يعلمه، ولكنه يتعنّت، وإن لم يعد إليه كفيت شره وشكرت الله تعالى على ما ألهمك؛ أو تَضْرِبُه وتُؤلمه، فإنه لا يقدر أن يَضجَرَ أو يقابلك بالعتاب، لما لا يحتمل ذلك إلا بتسمية فعلك، وذلك يُعرف بالخبر، وقد أنكره. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Abu Mansur, may Allah have mercy on him, said: 'Then one cannot arrive by them to the perception of the good and the evil that minds encompass except by using tongues to speak with them and drawing hearing close to them. The right way to debate the denier of this — even if debating him is foolishness — is to jest with him as well. So you say to him upon his denial of the report: “What do you say?” If he repeats it, then know that he has accepted your report, since he returned to his statement and that is your repetition of the report. If he does not repeat it, you are spared his evil, praise Allah, and laugh at him. Similarly for the denier of sensory perception: you say to him, “What do you say?” If he repeats it, it becomes clear to you that he knows it but is obstinate. If he does not repeat it, you are spared his evil, thank Allah Almighty for what He inspired you, or you strike him and pain him, for he cannot become annoyed or confront you with reproach, because he cannot bear that except by naming your action, and that is known through report, which he has denied. And there is no power except through Allah.'
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage gives a practical dialectical method for dealing with the denier of reports (al-khabar) and the denier of sensory perception (al-ʿiyān). The point is that such deniers are obstinate, so serious debate is futile; instead, use a trick: ask them to repeat their statement. If they repeat it, they have implicitly accepted the medium of communication (a report in the first case, and the fact that they heard and responded in the second). If they refuse to repeat, they can be ignored or, as a last resort, physically punished for the denier of senses, since his pain would make him acknowledge through a report that he is in pain, which contradicts his denial of reports. The method reveals self-contradiction.

Key Terms
الخبر (al-khabar)
العيان (al-ʿiyān)
التعنت (al-taʿannut)
السمع (al-samʿ)
النطق (al-nuṭq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The method endorses violence as a primary response against deniers.
Correction: Physical punishment is only mentioned as a last resort for the denier of senses if the trick fails, and it serves to expose further contradiction.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does the denier of reports implicitly accept when he repeats his denial after being asked 'What do you say?'
A) He accepts the truth of reports
B) He accepts that his statement is a report
C) He admits he is lying
D) He accepts the existence of sensory perception

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's dialectical trick reveals that even obstinate deniers implicitly rely on the very means of knowledge they reject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you stubbornly clung to a position even though your own words or actions betrayed your acceptance of the truth? What would it take for you to admit that contradiction today?
?
This method reveals that even deniers rely on reports and senses to deny. In what area of your life are you denying a truth while unconsciously using that very truth? Will you let that awareness change you?
?
Maturidi calls debating such a denier 'foolishness.' Where in your life are you engaging in futile arguments that only feed your ego? Can you walk away and thank Allah for sparing you from that evil?

Definition and Certainty of Mutawatir Report
Maturidi defines mutawatir report and its epistemic certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidian definition of mutawatir report and how it yields certain knowledge through collective infallibility and divine grace.

Core Idea
Mutawatir knowledge arises from the impossibility of collective falsehood.

When reports from prophets reach us, they come through narrators who could err or lie. How can we be certain of their truth? Imam Maturidi answers by defining a special category of report—mutawatir—whose cumulative testimony makes falsehood impossible. This lesson unpacks his reasoning, showing how individual fallibility is overcome by collective agreement and divine facilitation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic original and its translation. Notice the problem Maturidi starts with: narrators are not infallible. Then follow how he moves from that problem to a solution.
2
Identify the condition for mutawatir
Maturidi says: 'if such a report is never found to be false.' That is the key condition. Underline it. It is not just many narrators, but the impossibility of falsehood.
3
Understand collective vs. individual fallibility
Each narrator could be wrong individually, but when they all agree, falsehood becomes impossible. This is the core insight of mutawatir.
See how he uses 'عصمة' (infallibility) collectively, not individually.
4
Reflect on the role of divine grace
Maturidi argues that such agreement cannot happen by chance. It is God who grants success and manifests truth. Think about why human desires and opinions would normally disagree.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
:[الخبر المتواتر]: ثم الأخبار التي تنتهي إلينا من الرسل تنتهي على ألسن من يُحتمل منهم الغلط والكذب، إذ ليس معهم دليل الصدق ولا برهان العصمة ، فحق مثله النظر فيه . فإن كان مثله مما لا يُوجد كَذِبًا قط فهو الخبر الذي من انتهى إليه مثله لزمه حق شهود القول لمن اتضح البرهان على عصمته ، وذلك وصف خبر المتواتر : من أن كلاً منهم - وإن لم يقم دليل على عصمته - فإن الخبر منهم إذا بلغ ذلك الحد ظهر صدقه، وثبتت عصمة مثله عن الكذب، وإن أمكن خلاف ذلك في كلّ على الإشارة . وهكذا القول فيها طريقه الاجتهاد، وإن احتمل خطأ كل على الانفراد والغلط، فإنهم لم يتفقوا إلا بمن يوفقهم لذلك ليظهر حقه؛ إذ الآراء لا تُؤدّي إليه بعد اختلاف الأهواء وتفرّق الهِمَم لذات ذي الرأي دون لطف العزيز الحميد الذي يملك إظهار حقه وعصمة خلقه فيما شاء. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
[The concurrent report (al-khabar al-mutawātir)]: Then the reports that reach us from the messengers reach us through tongues of those from whom error and falsehood are possible, for they do not have with them a proof of truthfulness nor a proof of infallibility. So it is proper to investigate such a report. If it is such that falsehood is never found in it, then it is the report that, when it reaches that level, one is obliged to accept it as the truth of the witness from whom the proof of his infallibility has become clear. That is the description of the concurrent report (khabar mutawātir): that each of them—even if no proof of his infallibility stands—when the report from them reaches that limit, its truth becomes evident, and the collective infallibility of such a group concerning falsehood is established, even if the opposite is possible in each individually, as an indication. And thus the ruling in it is that its path is ijtihād (juridical reasoning), even if error is possible for each individually and isolation, they do not agree except through one who grants them success for that so that His truth may appear; for opinions do not lead to that after the divergence of desires and the dispersal of motives for the sake of the opinion-holder, without the grace of the Mighty, the Praiseworthy, who is able to manifest His truth and preserve His creation in what He wills. There is no power except through God.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage defines mutawātir (concurrent) report. It begins by noting that reports from prophets come through narrators who are not infallible and could err or lie. Therefore, such reports require investigation. However, if a report reaches a level where it is never found to be false—meaning it is impossible for it to be false—then it is a mutawātir report. The description: even if each narrator individually lacks a proof of infallibility, when the report reaches mass transmission (that limit), its truth becomes clear, and the collectivity is established as infallible regarding falsehood, even though individually error is possible. The passage then explains that such reports ultimately rely on ijtihād (reasoning), but the agreement of so many diverse individuals cannot occur without divine grace. This is because personal opinions, given differing desires and motives, would not converge to produce a unanimous true report unless God facilitates it. Thus, mutawātir reports produce certainty, and their acceptance is based on rational necessity combined with divine assistance.

Key Terms
خبر متواتر (khabar mutawātir)
عصمة (infallibility)
اجتهاد (ijtihād)
صدق (truthfulness)
علم (certain knowledge)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mutawātir is defined simply by a large number of narrators, regardless of the possibility of collusion.
Correction: Maturidi emphasizes that the defining characteristic is the impossibility of falsehood, which arises from the nature of the report, not just the count. A large number alone does not guarantee impossibility if collusion is possible.

Checkpoint
What is the defining characteristic of a mutawātir report according to Maturidi?
A) It is narrated by many people
B) It is impossible for it to be false
C) It is transmitted by prophets
D) It is supported by reason

Key Takeaway
Mutawatir reports yield certain knowledge because the collective agreement of diverse, independent narrators on a falsehood is impossible, and this impossibility is ultimately guaranteed by divine grace.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read or hear a report about Islam—whether a hadith or a story about the Prophet—do you ever question its authenticity? How does the concept of mutawātir challenge you to reconsider the basis of your certainty in such reports?
?
Maturidi argues that the agreement of narrators on a truth is not merely a product of human reasoning but of divine grace. In your own life, when have you seen people with differing desires come together on a truth that you know could only be from God? Does that realization deepen your trust in His guidance?
?
If you treat some transmitted religious knowledge as 'certain' without verifying its chain, what does that say about your intellectual responsibility? Are you willing to investigate the sources of your beliefs as Maturidi suggests, even when it is uncomfortable?

Understanding Khabar al-Wahid: Probability and Ijtihad
Maturidi explains the epistemic status and ruling of khabar al-wahid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why khabar al-wahid does not provide certainty and how ijtihad is used to determine action based on it.

Core Idea
Khabar al-wahid leads to probability, not certainty; action requires ijtihad.

Imam Maturidi addresses the status of reports transmitted by a single narrator (khabar al-wahid). Unlike mutawatir reports, these do not yield certain knowledge. This lesson explains how scholars must use ijtihad and examine narrators to decide whether to act on such reports, recognizing that even sensory knowledge sometimes involves probability.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the key terms: khabar al-wahid, ijtihad, sam' (revealed law).
2
Identify Maturidi's main claim
Maturidi states that khabar al-wahid does not reach the level of necessitating certain knowledge (ilm) and testimony. Find that sentence and note the consequence.
The phrase 'لا يبلغ هذا القدر في إيجاب العلم' is central.
3
Understand the analogy with sensory knowledge
Maturidi compares khabar al-wahid to sensory knowledge, which sometimes relies on probability due to weak senses or distant objects. This shows that even the highest path of knowledge (sensory) can involve probability, so it is not surprising that khabar al-wahid does as well.
This strengthens the argument that ijtihad is necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خبر الواحد] وخبر آخر لا يبلغ هذا القدر في إيجاب العلم والشهادة بأنه الحق عن نبي الرحمة، فيجب العمل به والترك، بالاجتهاد والنظر في أحوال الرواة، والظاهر مِمّا ظهر حكمه، وجوازه في السمع الذي قد أحيط. ثم يُعمل بما يغلب عليه الوجه وإن احتمل الغلط، إذ ربما يُعمَل به في علم الحس الذي هو أرفع طرق العلم بضعف الحواس أو ببعد المحسوس ولطفه على أن ترك العمل به والعمل جميعا لا يرجع المرء فيه إلى الإحاطة. وإلى أيهما مال كان في ذلك إعراض عن حق الخبر . فلذلك لزم القول فيه بالاجتهاد بالوجهين ولا قوة إلا بالله .
[The single report (khabar al-wāḥid)]: And another report that does not reach this degree in necessitating knowledge and testimony that it is the truth from the Prophet of mercy, so acting upon it and abandoning it are obligatory, through ijtihād (juridical reasoning) and examination of the conditions of the narrators, and the apparent from what has appeared of its ruling, and its permission in the transmitted law (al-sam`) which has been encompassed. Then one acts upon the preponderant probability, even if error is possible, for sometimes one acts upon it in sensory knowledge which is the highest of the paths of knowledge, due to weakness of the senses or distance of the sensed object and its subtlety, such that abandoning acting upon it and acting upon it both do not return a person to certainty. Whichever he inclines to, in that there is turning away from the truth of the report. Therefore, ruling concerning it through ijtihād in both ways (acting or abandoning) is necessary. There is no power except through God.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage discusses khabar al-wāḥid (single report). It is defined as a report that does not reach the level of producing certain knowledge (ʿilm) and testimony that it is the truth from the Prophet. Therefore, one is obliged to either act upon it or abandon it, based on ijtihād (juridical reasoning) and examination of the narrators' conditions, and the apparent (zāhir) from what has appeared of its legal ruling, and its permissibility in the revealed law (al-samʿ) that has been definitively established. Then one acts according to the preponderant opinion (ghālib al-raʿy), even though error is possible. Maturidi justifies this by noting that even in sensory knowledge, which is the highest form of knowledge, one sometimes acts on probable grounds due to weakness of the senses or the subtlety/distance of the object. Neither acting nor abandoning yields certainty in such cases, so whichever choice one makes involves some turning away from the full truth of the report. Therefore, ijtihād is necessary in both options. The point is that khabar al-wāḥid does not give certainty but requires probabilistic reasoning.

Key Terms
خبر واحد (khabar al-wāḥid)
اجتهاد (ijtihād)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think khabar al-wahid gives certain knowledge like mutawatir reports.
Correction: Maturidi explicitly states it does not reach that degree; it yields only probability, requiring ijtihad.

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi say we should treat khabar al-wāḥid?
A) Accept it without question
B) Reject it because it is uncertain
C) Act upon it through ijtihād
D) Require it to be mutawātir

Key Takeaway
Khabar al-wahid does not yield certainty; scholars must use ijtihad to determine action based on preponderant probability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a religious ruling based on a single hadith, do you instinctively treat it as certain or probable? How does this lesson challenge your default reaction, and what concrete step can you take to humble yourself before probabilistic knowledge?
?
The passage compares acting on probability in religious matters to sensory knowledge, which we rely on despite its fallibility. Where in your life do you demand absolute certainty before acting, even though you accept probability in other areas? What is the spiritual cost of that double standard?
?
If you were faced with a khabar al-wahid that seemed to contradict your preferences, would you honestly submit to ijtihād or would you find excuses to dismiss it? How might this test reveal your true reliance on God's guidance versus your own desires?

The Divine Command to Use Rational Reflection
Maturidi cites Qur'anic verses commanding reflection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Qur'an itself commands and encourages rational reflection (nazar) as a means to reach truth.

Core Idea
God commands reflection; denial of it contradicts divine guidance.

Maturidi begins his argument for the necessity of nazar by grounding it in divine revelation. He cites multiple Qur'anic verses that explicitly command reflection on creation, showing that intellectual inquiry is not optional but a religious obligation. This lesson explores those verses and their implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Identify the Qur'anic verses Maturidi cites.
Notice that he starts with the Qur'an's miraculous nature itself.
2
Identify the commands
List the verses he quotes. Each contains a command to look, reflect, or ponder.
For example, 'Do they not look at the camels?'
3
Understand the claim
Maturidi says these verses 'encourage reflection and necessitate consideration.' Why does he see them as proof of obligation?
Divine commands imply duty.
4
Reflect on the implication
If God commands nazar, then it is obligatory. But is that enough? This prepares for lesson 2.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذلك دَلَّ الله بالذي ثبت بالأدلة المعجزة أنه منه ، من نحو القرآن الذي عجز الإنس والجن أن يأتوا بمثله. مع الأمر به بقوله : سنريهم آياتنا في الآفاق إلى آخر السورة ، وقوله : أفلا ينظرون إلى الإبل - الآية ، وقوله : إن في خلق السموات والأرض - الآية ، وقوله : وفي أنفسكم أفلا تبصرون ، وغير ذلك مما رغب في النظر وألزم الاعتبار وأمر بالتفكر والتدبر، وأخبر أن ذلك يوقفهم على الحق ويبين لهم الطريق. ولا قوة إلا بالله . مع ما ليس لمن ينكر النظر على دفعه دليل سوى النظر ، فدل ذلك على لزوم النظر بما به دفعه. مع ما لا بد من معرفة ما في الخلق من الحكمة، إذ لا يجوز فعل مثله عبئًا، وما فيه من الدلالة على من أنشأه أو على كونه بنفسه أو حَدَثٍ أو قِدَم ، وكل ذلك مما لا سبيل إلى العلم به إلا بالنظر . على أن البشر خُص بملك تدبير الخلائق والمحنة فيها وطلب الأصلح لهم في العقول واختيار المحاسن في ذلك واتقاء مضادّة ذلك ؛ ولا سبيل إلى معرفة ذلك إلا باستعمال العقول بالنظر في الأشياء على أن مفزع الكُلِّ . عند النوائب واعتراض الشُّبه إلى النظر في ذلك والتأمل. فدلّ أنه يدلّ على الحقائق ويوصل به إليها ، على نحو الفزع عند اشتباه اللون إلى البصر، والصوت إلى السمع، وكذا كل شيء إلى الحاسة التي بها دَرْكُها ، فَمِثْلُه النظر. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
God indicated through that which is established by miraculous proofs to be from Him, such as the Qur'an which humans and jinn are incapable of bringing its like. Along with His command: 'We will show them Our signs in the horizons' to the end of the surah, and His saying: 'Do they not look at the camels' – the verse, and His saying: 'Indeed in the creation of the heavens and the earth' – the verse, and His saying: 'And in yourselves, do you not see?' and other such verses that encourage reflection (nazar) and necessitate consideration, and command pondering and deliberation, and inform that that will make them stand upon the truth and clarify for them the path. And there is no power except with God. Along with the fact that for the one who denies reflection (nazar) there is no proof to repel it except reflection (nazar) itself, so that indicates the necessity of reflection (nazar) by that by which it is repelled. Along with the necessity of knowing the wisdom in creation, since it is not permissible to do something like it in vain, and what is in it of indication of the one who originated it or of its being by itself or its being originated or eternal, and all that there is no way to knowledge of it except through reflection (nazar). Moreover, humans are distinguished by the power of managing created beings and trials in them, and seeking what is best for them in intellects, and choosing the good in that, and avoiding what is contrary to that; and there is no way to knowledge of that except by using intellects through reflection (nazar) on things, since the recourse of all, at calamities and the intrusion of doubts, is to reflection (nazar) and deliberation. So it shows that it (nazar) indicates realities and leads to them, like the recourse at confusion of color to sight, and sound to hearing, and likewise everything to the sense by which it is perceived, so reflection (nazar) is like that. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage is a multi-faceted argument for the necessity of rational reflection (nazar). Maturidi begins by citing Qur'anic commands and examples that encourage reflection, showing that God Himself has mandated nazar through miraculous proofs like the Qur'an's inimitability. He then points out that even those who deny nazar must use nazar to argue against it, which self-refutes their position and proves nazar's necessity. Furthermore, he argues that knowledge of the wisdom and purpose in creation—whether it is originated or eternal, and who originated it—can only be attained through nazar. He adds that humans are uniquely endowed with the capacity to manage affairs, seek benefit, and avoid harm, and this too requires nazar. Finally, he draws an analogy: just as we rely on our senses when confused about colors or sounds, we must rely on nazar to discern truth from falsehood. The structure is a cumulative proof: divine command, logical self-refutation, epistemological necessity, practical human need, and analogical reasoning all converge on the same conclusion.

Key Terms
نظر (nazar)
أدلة معجزة (miraculous proofs)
قرآن (Qur'an)
اعتبار (consideration)
تفكر (pondering)
تدبر (deliberation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi cite as the primary evidence for the necessity of nazar in this passage?
A) Logical self-refutation
B) Qur'anic verses commanding reflection
C) The inimitability of the Qur'an only
D) The need to manage worldly affairs

Key Takeaway
The Qur'an commands nazar, making it a divinely mandated duty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You read that God commands you to look at the camels, the heavens, and yourself. When was the last time you actually did that with the intention of recognizing His power? What would it cost you to take five minutes today to do it?
?
The verse 'Do you not see?' is directed at you. If you are honest, do you avoid this kind of reflection because it might demand a change in your life? What is the one sign you have been ignoring, and are you willing to face it right now?

The Self-Refutation of Denying Nazar
Maturidi presents self-refutation and epistemological arguments for nazar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the very act of denying nazar requires nazar, and that all knowledge depends on it.

Core Idea
Denying reflection forces one to reflect, proving its necessity.

Maturidi presents a powerful logical argument: anyone who denies rational reflection must use reflection to make their case. This self-refutation demonstrates that nazar is unavoidable. He then expands to show that all knowledge of creation's origin, purpose, and human affairs depends on nazar, and illustrates with an analogy to the senses.

Guided Path
1
Read the self-refutation argument
Read the first part of the passage: 'For the one who denies nazar, there is no proof to repel it except nazar.'
This is the core of the self-refutation.
2
Understand the logic
If someone argues against nazar, they are using reasoning (nazar) itself. So they cannot deny nazar without using it.
It's a self-defeating position.
3
Examine the epistemological necessity
Maturidi then argues that knowledge of creation's wisdom, origin, and eternity can only be gained through nazar.
This shows nazar is indispensable.
4
Consider the human need and analogy
Humans manage affairs and seek benefit using intellect; this requires nazar. Just as senses are used for their objects, nazar is used for truth.
The analogy reinforces the argument.
5
Synthesize the conclusion
All these points converge to prove that nazar is necessary and unavoidable.
Lesson 1's divine command is now supported by reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذلك دَلَّ الله بالذي ثبت بالأدلة المعجزة أنه منه ، من نحو القرآن الذي عجز الإنس والجن أن يأتوا بمثله. مع الأمر به بقوله : سنريهم آياتنا في الآفاق إلى آخر السورة ، وقوله : أفلا ينظرون إلى الإبل - الآية ، وقوله : إن في خلق السموات والأرض - الآية ، وقوله : وفي أنفسكم أفلا تبصرون ، وغير ذلك مما رغب في النظر وألزم الاعتبار وأمر بالتفكر والتدبر، وأخبر أن ذلك يوقفهم على الحق ويبين لهم الطريق. ولا قوة إلا بالله . مع ما ليس لمن ينكر النظر على دفعه دليل سوى النظر ، فدل ذلك على لزوم النظر بما به دفعه. مع ما لا بد من معرفة ما في الخلق من الحكمة، إذ لا يجوز فعل مثله عبئًا، وما فيه من الدلالة على من أنشأه أو على كونه بنفسه أو حَدَثٍ أو قِدَم ، وكل ذلك مما لا سبيل إلى العلم به إلا بالنظر . على أن البشر خُص بملك تدبير الخلائق والمحنة فيها وطلب الأصلح لهم في العقول واختيار المحاسن في ذلك واتقاء مضادّة ذلك ؛ ولا سبيل إلى معرفة ذلك إلا باستعمال العقول بالنظر في الأشياء على أن مفزع الكُلِّ . عند النوائب واعتراض الشُّبه إلى النظر في ذلك والتأمل. فدلّ أنه يدلّ على الحقائق ويوصل به إليها ، على نحو الفزع عند اشتباه اللون إلى البصر، والصوت إلى السمع، وكذا كل شيء إلى الحاسة التي بها دَرْكُها ، فَمِثْلُه النظر. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
God indicated through that which is established by miraculous proofs to be from Him, such as the Qur'an which humans and jinn are incapable of bringing its like. Along with His command: 'We will show them Our signs in the horizons' to the end of the surah, and His saying: 'Do they not look at the camels' – the verse, and His saying: 'Indeed in the creation of the heavens and the earth' – the verse, and His saying: 'And in yourselves, do you not see?' and other such verses that encourage reflection (nazar) and necessitate consideration, and command pondering and deliberation, and inform that that will make them stand upon the truth and clarify for them the path. And there is no power except with God. Along with the fact that for the one who denies reflection (nazar) there is no proof to repel it except reflection (nazar) itself, so that indicates the necessity of reflection (nazar) by that by which it is repelled. Along with the necessity of knowing the wisdom in creation, since it is not permissible to do something like it in vain, and what is in it of indication of the one who originated it or of its being by itself or its being originated or eternal, and all that there is no way to knowledge of it except through reflection (nazar). Moreover, humans are distinguished by the power of managing created beings and trials in them, and seeking what is best for them in intellects, and choosing the good in that, and avoiding what is contrary to that; and there is no way to knowledge of that except by using intellects through reflection (nazar) on things, since the recourse of all, at calamities and the intrusion of doubts, is to reflection (nazar) and deliberation. So it shows that it (nazar) indicates realities and leads to them, like the recourse at confusion of color to sight, and sound to hearing, and likewise everything to the sense by which it is perceived, so reflection (nazar) is like that. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage is a multi-faceted argument for the necessity of rational reflection (nazar). Maturidi begins by citing Qur'anic commands and examples that encourage reflection, showing that God Himself has mandated nazar through miraculous proofs like the Qur'an's inimitability. He then points out that even those who deny nazar must use nazar to argue against it, which self-refutes their position and proves nazar's necessity. Furthermore, he argues that knowledge of the wisdom and purpose in creation—whether it is originated or eternal, and who originated it—can only be attained through nazar. He adds that humans are uniquely endowed with the capacity to manage affairs, seek benefit, and avoid harm, and this too requires nazar. Finally, he draws an analogy: just as we rely on our senses when confused about colors or sounds, we must rely on nazar to discern truth from falsehood. The structure is a cumulative proof: divine command, logical self-refutation, epistemological necessity, practical human need, and analogical reasoning all converge on the same conclusion.

Key Terms
نظر (nazar)
عجز (incapacity)
حكمة (wisdom)
حَدَث (origination)
قِدَم (eternity)
اعتبار (consideration)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Faith does not require rational reflection; reason is separate from religion.
Correction: Maturidi shows that even denying nazar uses nazar, and that all knowledge of religious truths depends on it.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what happens when someone tries to deny nazar?
A) They can use tradition as proof
B) They must use nazar to argue against it
C) They can remain silent
D) They can rely on intuition

Key Takeaway
Nazar is unavoidable; all knowledge and even denial of nazar presuppose it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When doubts about your faith arise, do you immediately engage in careful reflection (nazar) or do you try to push them away? What does your instinctive reaction reveal about your trust in the intellect Allah gave you?
?
The passage says we use nazar to distinguish truth from falsehood, just as we use sight for colors. Is there an area of your life where you avoid thinking deeply because it might be uncomfortable? Will you commit to using nazar there?
?
Denying nazar requires nazar—so you cannot escape it. Are there beliefs you hold simply because others hold them, without personal reflection? How can you begin to use your God-given intellect to verify them?

Refuting Skeptics on Sensory Knowledge
Maturidi responds to skeptics who deny sense knowledge due to rare errors, attributing it to Satanic deception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that occasional sensory errors do not invalidate sense perception; rather, such errors are exceptions exploited by Satan to mislead.

Core Idea
Rare sensory errors are Satanic tricks, not reasons to reject all knowledge.

Maturidi confronts skeptics who use occasional sensory mistakes to deny all sense-based knowledge. He frames their denial as a deception by Satan, not a rational conclusion. In this passage, he begins to expose these doubts and provides examples of how perception can seem to err.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the translation. Notice how Maturidi introduces the topic and the examples he gives.
The passage ends abruptly with 'or' (أو), suggesting more examples follow.
2
Identify the central argument
Maturidi claims that skeptics are deluded by Satan, not that God's proof is deficient. Understand this as his key move: he shifts the blame from God to Satanic deception.
This is a common theological response to skeptical arguments.
3
Examine the examples
Maturidi offers two cases where perception seems to err: defective sight and dreams. Ask yourself: do these rare cases justify rejecting all sensory knowledge? The commentary will explain why they don't.
Defective sight is an exception due to a physical flaw; dreams are not waking perception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

A – So we say: Satan deludes the denier of direct perception (al-ʿiyān) by that which may turn out to be other than what the deliberator supposed, in order to turn him away from worship of the Merciful, such as the one whose sight is afflicted (al-muʾawwaf baṣar), or the one whose self contends with him in sleep (al-manām), or

Commentary
This passage introduces a rebuttal against those who deny the validity of sensory knowledge (al-ʿiyān) because of occasional errors. Maturidi explains that such deniers are overcome by luxury (rafah) and seduced by Satan, not because God's proof is deficient. He then gives an example: Satan deludes the denier by presenting cases where direct perception seems to err, such as a person with defective vision (probably meant as 'afflicted in sight') or someone in a dream-state experiencing nightmares. The implication is that these exceptional cases do not invalidate the general reliability of sense perception; rather, they are used by Satan to lead people astray. The text ends abruptly, but the argument likely continues that the existence of such errors does not negate the overall validity of sensory knowledge as a means of knowing.

The Necessity of Rational Inference for Knowledge
Maturidi argues that knowledge of report truthfulness and identity depends on rational inference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that rational inference (istidlal) is necessary for all knowledge, including recognizing people and evaluating reports.

Core Idea
Without rational inference, no interaction with the world is possible.

Imam Maturidi begins by showing that even simple recognition—like knowing a person or fire after seeing it once—depends on rational inference. He then extends this to evaluating the truth of reports. This passage establishes that rational inference is not optional but necessary for any meaningful knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English carefully to get the flow of Maturidi's argument.
Notice the strong language of necessity.
2
Identify the reductio ad absurdum
Maturidi says if a person does not use inference, he cannot interact with anyone or learn from anyone. This is absurd, so inference is necessary.
This is a key logical move.
3
Connect to the conclusion about reports
In the final sentence, Maturidi explicitly states that truth or falsehood of reports depends on rational inference.
This shows the scope of inference includes both sense-based and testimonial knowledge.
4
Reflect on why this matters for theology
This argument grounds the need for reasoned theology (kalam) against those who claim to rely only on reports or sensory experience.
Maturidi is laying the foundation for his whole project.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد، فإن معرفة إنسان أو نار أو شيء بالذي شوهد مرة لا تخرج إلا على الاستدلال بالذي عُرف، ولو لم يدلّه للزمه أن لا يعامل أحداً قط؛ ثم لا يقبل تعليم أحد، لأنه لا دليل عنده يُعلم أنه من [هو]، ويجوز أن يوجد [دليل] بخبره أولاً. ثبت أن كل ذلك استدلال، وهو لازم. ولا قوة إلا بالله. ويعني ذلك أنه لا يمكن صدق الإخبار أو كذبه إلا باستدلال العقل.
Then, the knowledge of a person, or fire, or something by that which was observed once does not come except through inference from that which was known. And if he did not infer, it would be necessary that he never deal with anyone; then he would not accept anyone's teaching, because he has no evidence by which he knows who he is, and it is possible that evidence exists from his report first. It is established that all of that is inference, and it is necessary. There is no strength except through God. And this means that truthfulness or falsehood of reports can only be known through rational inference.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage is an argument demonstrating the necessity of rational inference (istidlal). Maturidi uses a reductio: If a person refrains from inference, he cannot recognize a person or object after a single observation, and thus cannot interact with anyone or accept teaching. This leads to an absurdity, so inference is required. He then concludes that distinguishing true from false reports also depends on rational inference, not just sense or report.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi's argument rejects the validity of sense perception and reports.
Correction: The argument does not reject sense or report; it shows that rational inference is required to make sense of them and to evaluate their truthfulness.

Key Takeaway
Rational inference is necessary for all knowledge, including recognition and the evaluation of reports.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Think of a religious teacher or scholar you follow: have you ever rationally inferred the truth of what they teach, or do you rely solely on their authority? What would it mean for your faith if you never used your own reason?

The passage concludes that distinguishing truth from falsehood in reports depends on rational inference. The most important report you rely on is the Qur'an. Have you ever personally inferred its truth, or have you simply accepted it because others do? What would change if you actively applied inference to confirm it?

Affirming Attributes Without Likening to Creation
Maturidi states his thesis and the shahid-gha'ib principle with example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why affirming God's names and attributes does not entail likening Him to creation, based on the principle that the unseen is known through the witnessed.

Core Idea
Affirming attributes does not necessitate tashbih; language from creation is a necessary tool, not a cause of likeness.

Maturidi begins by asserting a fundamental thesis: that affirming God's names and attributes is not an act of tashbih (likening). He then provides the epistemological reason: we cannot know the unseen (gha'ib) except through the witnessed (shahid). This passage establishes the foundation for a sound theological method.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic once without analyzing, then read the translation. Notice the strong opening thesis.
The author uses a very precise style.
2
Identify the shahid-gha'ib principle
Find where Maturidi states that knowledge of the unseen comes only through the witnessed. This is the key to his argument.
3
Reflect on the example
Look at the phrase 'Knowing, not like the knowers'. Understand how it removes any notion of similarity.
This pattern applies to all names and attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ أبو منصور رحمه الله : وليس في إثبات الأسماء وتحقيق الصفات تشابه لنفي حقائق ما في الخلق عنه كالهستية والثبات ولكن الأسماء لما لم يُحتمل التعريف ولا تحقيق الذات بحق الربوبية إلا بذلك، إذ لا وجه لمعرفة غائب إلا بدلالة الشاهد، ثم إذا أريد الوصف بالعلو والجلال. فذلك طريق المعرفة في الشاهد وإمكانُ القول، إذ لا يحتمل وسعنا العرفان بالتسمية بغير الذي شاهدنا ، ولا الإشارة إلى ما لا نأخذ من الحس وحق العيان. لو احتمل وسعنا ذلك لقلنا ذلك، لكنا [أردنا] به ما يُسقط الشبه من قولنا : «عالم لا كالعلماء»، وهذا النوع في كل ما نسميه به ونصفه والله الموفق. مع ما كان التشابه الذي تُقدّره أوهامنا ليس عن قول اللسان تُقدّره، بل بما كنا نعرف الشبه بين الذاتين والفعلين، فإلى ذلك يرجع وهمنا عند التسمية؛ وذلك يُحققه لو لم يكن لهما اسم عُرِفا به ووصف وصفا به. فإذا كان الله سبحانه، فيما اعتقدنا وحدانيته، اعتقدنا[ه] غير شبيه بالمعروفين في تسمية الآحاد لم يلزمنا التسمية بما تعرَّفنا ما لولا الاسم لم يجب التشبيه بذلك الاسم . ولا قوة إلا بالله. على أن من نفي الأسماء والصفات من الفلاسفة لم يقل بالتعطيل، وكل مثبت معناه في
Shaykh Abu Mansur, may Allah have mercy on him, said: There is no likeness in affirming names and verifying attributes that negate the realities of what is in creation from Him, like 'that-ness' (huwiyya) and stability (thabat). But the names are not capable of definition nor verification of the essence by the right of lordship except through that, since there is no way to know the unseen (gha'ib) except by indication of the witnessed (shahid). Then when one intends description with transcendence and majesty, that is the way of knowledge in the witnessed and the possibility of speech, since our capacity does not allow knowledge by naming with other than what we have witnessed, nor pointing to what we do not take from sense and the truth of sight. If our capacity allowed that, we would say that, but we intend by it what removes likeness from our saying: 'Knowing, not like the knowers,' and this type applies to everything we name Him with and describe Him with, and Allah is the granter of success. Moreover, the likeness that our imaginations estimate is not estimated by the utterance of the tongue, but by our knowing the resemblance between two essences and two actions; so our imagination refers to that at the time of naming. And that is verified by the fact that if they had no name by which they are known and description by which they are described, then when Allah, exalted, in what we believe of His oneness, we believe Him unlike those known in naming individuals, naming does not oblige us with what we have come to know; if not for the name, resemblance would not be necessitated by that name. And there is no power except with Allah. Moreover, those among the philosophers who negate names and attributes did not speak of negating (ta'til), and every affirmer of His meaning is in...
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage is a methodological explanation of how to affirm God's names and attributes without falling into tashbih (likening God to creation). Maturidi begins by stating that affirming names and verifying attributes does not entail likeness to creation; rather, it negates the realities of created things from God, such as 'that-ness' (huwiyya) and stability (thabat). The reason we use these terms is that we have no way to define or verify God's essence except through language derived from the witnessed world (shahid), because the unseen (gha'ib) can only be known by analogy with the witnessed. However, when we describe God with transcendence and majesty, we use the same terms but with the understanding that they are not applied in the same way. Our capacity does not allow us to name God with something we have not witnessed or perceived by the senses; if it did, we would. Instead, we use qualifiers like 'Knowing, not like the knowers' (عالم لا كالعلماء) to remove any notion of similarity. This principle applies to all names and attributes. Maturidi then explains that the similarity we imagine is not a result of the utterance itself, but of our knowledge of resemblance between essences and actions. If two things had no names, no resemblance would be implied. Therefore, when we believe God is unlike any known individual in His oneness, the mere use of a common name does not necessitate similarity. Finally, he notes that even philosophers who negate names and attributes do not fully deny (ta'til), and every affirmer of God's meaning is in... (the text cuts off). The key point is that language from the witnessed world is necessary for divine discourse, but it does not imply likeness because we always negate any similarity through qualifiers and intention.

Key Terms
الإثبات
التشبيه
الشاهد
الغائب
الهستية
الثبات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Using the same words for God and creatures implies similarity (tashbih).
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that the intention and context remove any such implication, as shown by the qualifier 'not like the knowers'.

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi justify using terms from the witnessed world (shahid) to describe God without falling into tashbih?
A) By saying that God is unlike anything we know.
B) By adding qualifiers like 'not like the knowers' and intending to negate similarity.
C) By avoiding all names and attributes entirely.
D) By using philosophical terms not found in the Qur'an.

Key Takeaway
Affirming God's names and attributes does not entail likeness because we use terms from the witnessed world while negating any similarity through qualifiers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is All-Knowing', do you actively negate any likeness to creaturely knowledge in your heart, or do you unconsciously imagine a superhuman version of your own knowing? Will you commit to adding the silent qualifier 'not like the knowers' every time you invoke His names?
?
Maturidi says our capacity does not allow us to name God with something we have not witnessed. How often do you speak about Allah in ways that go beyond what He has revealed or what the Prophet taught? Will you discipline your tongue to only describe Him as He describes Himself?
?
If every word we use about Allah carries the risk of tashbih, how carefully do you examine your own descriptions in prayer, supplication, and daily speech? What is one specific phrase you commonly use that might imply unguarded similarity, and how will you correct it starting today?

The Imagination of Resemblance and the Role of Names
Maturidi discusses the source of imagined resemblance and concludes with God's uniqueness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the imagination of resemblance between God and creation arises not from words but from our knowledge of essences, and how this is resolved by affirming God's uniqueness.

Core Idea
The similarity we imagine is not in the words but in our understanding of essences; the use of common names does not imply actual likeness.

Maturidi continues his rebuttal of tashbih by examining the psychology of resemblance. He argues that when we hear a name applied to both God and creation, we tend to imagine similarity between essences. But this is a habit of mind, not a logical necessity. The passage clarifies that if two things had no names, no resemblance would be implied, and that God's oneness prevents any such comparison.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage focusing on the cause of imagined similarity
Pay attention to where Maturidi says the imagination of resemblance is not from the utterance but from our knowledge of essences and actions.
2
Understand the thought experiment about nameless things
Maturidi asks: if two things had no names, would any resemblance be implied? The answer is no, showing that names themselves do not create likeness.
This is a key logical step.
3
See the conclusion about God's uniqueness
Since we believe God is unique and unlike any individual, the use of common names does not necessitate any actual similarity.

قال الشيخ أبو منصور رحمه الله : وليس في إثبات الأسماء وتحقيق الصفات تشابه لنفي حقائق ما في الخلق عنه كالهستية والثبات ولكن الأسماء لما لم يُحتمل التعريف ولا تحقيق الذات بحق الربوبية إلا بذلك، إذ لا وجه لمعرفة غائب إلا بدلالة الشاهد، ثم إذا أريد الوصف بالعلو والجلال. فذلك طريق المعرفة في الشاهد وإمكانُ القول، إذ لا يحتمل وسعنا العرفان بالتسمية بغير الذي شاهدنا ، ولا الإشارة إلى ما لا نأخذ من الحس وحق العيان. لو احتمل وسعنا ذلك لقلنا ذلك، لكنا [أردنا] به ما يُسقط الشبه من قولنا : «عالم لا كالعلماء»، وهذا النوع في كل ما نسميه به ونصفه والله الموفق. مع ما كان التشابه الذي تُقدّره أوهامنا ليس عن قول اللسان تُقدّره، بل بما كنا نعرف الشبه بين الذاتين والفعلين، فإلى ذلك يرجع وهمنا عند التسمية؛ وذلك يُحققه لو لم يكن لهما اسم عُرِفا به ووصف وصفا به. فإذا كان الله سبحانه، فيما اعتقدنا وحدانيته، اعتقدنا[ه] غير شبيه بالمعروفين في تسمية الآحاد لم يلزمنا التسمية بما تعرَّفنا ما لولا الاسم لم يجب التشبيه بذلك الاسم . ولا قوة إلا بالله. على أن من نفي الأسماء والصفات من الفلاسفة لم يقل بالتعطيل، وكل مثبت معناه في
Shaykh Abu Mansur, may Allah have mercy on him, said: There is no likeness in affirming names and verifying attributes that negate the realities of what is in creation from Him, like 'that-ness' (huwiyya) and stability (thabat). But the names are not capable of definition nor verification of the essence by the right of lordship except through that, since there is no way to know the unseen (gha'ib) except by indication of the witnessed (shahid). Then when one intends description with transcendence and majesty, that is the way of knowledge in the witnessed and the possibility of speech, since our capacity does not allow knowledge by naming with other than what we have witnessed, nor pointing to what we do not take from sense and the truth of sight. If our capacity allowed that, we would say that, but we intend by it what removes likeness from our saying: 'Knowing, not like the knowers,' and this type applies to everything we name Him with and describe Him with, and Allah is the granter of success. Moreover, the likeness that our imaginations estimate is not estimated by the utterance of the tongue, but by our knowing the resemblance between two essences and two actions; so our imagination refers to that at the time of naming. And that is verified by the fact that if they had no name by which they are known and description by which they are described, then when Allah, exalted, in what we believe of His oneness, we believe Him unlike those known in naming individuals, naming does not oblige us with what we have come to know; if not for the name, resemblance would not be necessitated by that name. And there is no power except with Allah. Moreover, those among the philosophers who negate names and attributes did not speak of negating (ta'til), and every affirmer of His meaning is in...
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage is a methodological explanation of how to affirm God's names and attributes without falling into tashbih (likening God to creation). Maturidi begins by stating that affirming names and verifying attributes does not entail likeness to creation; rather, it negates the realities of created things from God, such as 'that-ness' (huwiyya) and stability (thabat). The reason we use these terms is that we have no way to define or verify God's essence except through language derived from the witnessed world (shahid), because the unseen (gha'ib) can only be known by analogy with the witnessed. However, when we describe God with transcendence and majesty, we use the same terms but with the understanding that they are not applied in the same way. Our capacity does not allow us to name God with something we have not witnessed or perceived by the senses; if it did, we would. Instead, we use qualifiers like 'Knowing, not like the knowers' (عالم لا كالعلماء) to remove any notion of similarity. This principle applies to all names and attributes. Maturidi then explains that the similarity we imagine is not a result of the utterance itself, but of our knowledge of resemblance between essences and actions. If two things had no names, no resemblance would be implied. Therefore, when we believe God is unlike any known individual in His oneness, the mere use of a common name does not necessitate similarity. Finally, he notes that even philosophers who negate names and attributes do not fully deny (ta'til), and every affirmer of God's meaning is in... (the text cuts off). The key point is that language from the witnessed world is necessary for divine discourse, but it does not imply likeness because we always negate any similarity through qualifiers and intention.

الذات
الفعل
الاسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mere use of the same word for God and creation creates resemblance.
Correction: Maturidi says it is not the word but our knowledge of resemblance between essences that causes the imagination of likeness.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does the imagination of resemblance at the time of naming depend on?
A) The utterance itself
B) Our knowledge of resemblance between essences
C) The grammatical structure
D) The intention of the speaker

Key Takeaway
The likeness we imagine is not due to the name itself but to our understanding of the essences; God's uniqueness ensures that common names do not imply actual similarity.

?
You use the same word 'exists' for God and for yourself. Have you ever paused to realize that His existence is absolutely unique, while yours is contingent? What specific change in your daily awareness of God will you make starting now to avoid any trace of tashbih in your heart?

Three Views on the Indication of the Witnessed upon the Unseen
Maturidi enumerates three opinions on the indication of the witnessed upon the unseen.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three different opinions about how the witnessed world points to the unseen (God), especially why indicating difference is clearer than indicating likeness.

Core Idea
The witnessed points to the unseen more by difference than by likeness.

This passage from Imam Maturidi presents a key methodological discussion: how do we move from what we see (the witnessed) to what is unseen (God)? Different thinkers have proposed different analogies. By examining their arguments, we can sharpen our theological reasoning and avoid errors like assuming the world is eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage in Arabic and English
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Notice that the passage lists three distinct opinions introduced by 'فمنهم من يقول'.
The passage ends with 'ثم' indicating continuation, but the three views are complete for this section.
2
Examine the first view: analogy of likeness
The first group says the witnessed indicates the unseen by likeness (مثل), arguing that the witnessed is the principle (أصل) for the unseen branch. This leads them to affirm the pre-eternity of the world.
This view is associated with those who believe in the eternity of the world based on analogical reasoning.
3
Understand the second view: no beginning
The second group argues that there is no time when the world's beginning is imagined without imagining a time before it, thus invalidating any endpoint or beginning for the world.
This view might be a form of argument from infinite regress.
4
Analyze the third view: indication of difference
The third group says the witnessed indicates both the similar and the different, but indication of difference is clearer. For example, witnessing something tells you about its temporal origination or pre-eternity, which are not like the thing itself.
This is likely Maturidi's own view, emphasizing God's transcendence.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider why favoring difference avoids theological pitfalls like anthropomorphism (tashbīh) and maintains divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : ثم اختلف في وجه دلالة الشاهد على الغائب. فمنهم من يقول : على مثله، إذ هو أصل للذي غاب عنه، ولا يخالف الأصل فرعه؛ مع ما كان طريق معرفة الغائب الشاهد، وقياس الشيء مع نظيره فيه أثبتوا قدم العالم، إذ الشاهد يدل على مثله، فصار الغائب به عالَمًا أيضاً. ثم هو يدل في كل وقت على مثله قبله، وفي ذلك إيجاب القدم للكل . ومنهم من يقول : ما من وقت يُتوهّم فيه ابتداء العالم إلا وقد يتوهم قبله، فيبطل له الغاية. ومنهم من يقول: يدل على المثل والخلاف، ودلالته على الخلاف أوضح؛ لأن من شيئًا من العالم يدله على حدثه أو قدمه، وقدمه أو حدثه ليس هو مثله ولا نظيره؛ ثم
Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: Then they differed regarding the manner of indication of the witnessed (shāhid) upon the unseen (ghā'ib). Among them are those who say: upon its likeness (mithl), since it is the principle (asl) for what has become absent from it, and the branch does not differ from the principle; moreover, the way of knowing the unseen is the witnessed, and analogizing a thing with its counterpart – by it they affirmed the pre-eternity (qidam) of the world, because the witnessed indicates its likeness, so the unseen becomes a world also. Then it indicates at every time its likeness before it, and in that is necessitating pre-eternity for the whole. And among them are those who say: there is no time in which the beginning of the world is imagined except that before it is imagined, so the endpoint is invalidated for it. And among them are those who say: it indicates both the similar and the different, and its indication of the different is clearer, because whoever witnesses something of the world, it indicates to him its temporal origination or its pre-eternity, and its pre-eternity or its temporal origination is not its likeness nor its counterpart; then...
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage presents different views on how the witnessed world points to the unseen (God). The first view (probably of the dahriyya or naturalists) argues by analogy of likeness, which leads to affirming pre-eternity of the world. The second view (of some theologians) argues that there is no beginning imagined. The third view (Maturidi's own) is that the indication is more clearly one of difference (khilāf) rather than likeness, because the attributes of temporal origination or pre-eternity are not like the objects themselves. The passage ends unfinished.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The witnessed can only indicate the unseen through exact likeness.
Correction: Maturidi shows that indication of difference is clearer, because the attributes of the unseen (e.g., pre-eternity) are not like the attributes of the witnessed.

Checkpoint
What is the third view on the indication of the witnessed upon the unseen according to this passage?
A) It indicates only likeness.
B) It indicates only difference.
C) It indicates both the similar and the different, but difference is clearer.
D) There is no indication at all.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi presents three views on how the witnessed indicates the unseen, showing that the indication of difference is more logically sound than simple analogy of likeness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the world around you, do you naturally assume God is like it or different from it? How does this assumption affect the way you worship and rely on Him? This lesson challenges you to see the indication of difference more clearly—will you actively train your mind to recognize God's uniqueness in every created thing starting today?
?
The first view leads to the dangerous error of affirming the pre-eternity of the world. Is there any created thing—your health, wealth, relationships, or status—that you treat as if it were eternal or ultimate? Identify one such thing and commit to giving it its proper place as a temporary creation, not an ultimate concern.
?
Maturidi emphasizes that the indication of difference is clearer than similarity. Yet your heart often imagines God in human terms—angry like a father, loving like a mother. How does this passage expose that tendency as a form of hidden shirk? What concrete step will you take today to purify your concept of God from any likeness to His creation?
