The Necessity of Tahqiq and the Consequences of Failure
Rhetorical challenge to those who fail in tahqiq and fall into ta'til or tashbih.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why proper investigation (tahqiq) is essential in theology and how its absence leads to either divesting God of attributes (ta'til) or likening Him to creation (tashbih).

Core Idea
Without tahqiq, theology collapses into error: either ta'til or tashbih.

Imam Maturidi presents a powerful rhetorical challenge to those who fail to achieve tahqiq—rigorous investigation—in matters of God's names and attributes. He argues that such failure inevitably leads to one of two extremes: the negation of divine attributes (ta'til) or anthropomorphism (tashbih). The passage forces us to confront fundamental questions about worship, religion, and the origin of the world.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Note the rhetorical style of Imam Maturidi.
2
Identify key terms
Find the terms 'tahqiq', 'ta'til', 'tashbih', and 'hadath al-alam' in the passage. Understand their meanings.
3
Analyze the rhetorical questions
The Imam presents a series of questions. Consider their purpose: to expose the theological emptiness of those who fail to investigate.
4
Consider the consequences
According to the passage, what are the two possible outcomes? Either return to a meaning close to understanding, or join the deniers of the world's origination.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends with a statement about 'choice of bewilderment'. What does this imply about the state of those who reject tahqiq?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التحقيق نفى التعطيل ثم لم يجب به التشابه، فمثله في الأسماء. وإذا لم يحققوا فما يقولون لو قيل لهم: «ما تعبدون، وإلى ماذا تدعون، وبأي دين تدينون، ومَنْ أَمَركم ونهاكم عما تُنْهَوْن وتُؤمرون، ومَنْ به بدء العالم العلوي والسفلي، وبمن كان أوليّة الأشياء؟» ليرجعوا إلى معنى يقرب إلى الفهم أو يلحقوا بمنكري حدث العالم، ويبطلوا قولهم في الأول: إنه العقل، أو الأصل، أو السابق، أو الروحاني الأول، أو ما قالوا في ذلك؛ وفي ذلك اختيار الحيرة والتمسك بالجهل ودفع ما يُعرف غير العالم به. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Al-tahqiq (investigation) negates ta'til (divesting God of attributes)? Then they did not respond to tashbih (anthropomorphism) accordingly, so it is similar in names. If they do not investigate, what would they say if it were said to them: 'What do you worship, and to what do you call, and with what religion do you profess, and who commanded you and forbade you from what you are forbidden and commanded, and by whom is the beginning of the upper and lower world, and by whom was the primacy of things?' So that they might return to a meaning close to understanding, or they might join the deniers of the world's origination, and they invalidate their statement concerning the First: that it is intellect, or the origin, or the prior, or the first spiritual being, or whatever they said about that. In that is the choice of bewilderment, adherence to ignorance, and repelling what is known other than the world by it. There is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is a rhetorical challenge directed at those who fail to achieve proper investigation (tahqiq) in matters of God's names and attributes. The author argues that such failure leads either to ta'til (negation of attributes) or tashbih (anthropomorphism), and then confronts them with questions about the object of their worship, the content of their religion, and the origin of the world. It ends with a threat that they either return to a rational meaning or join the deniers of the world's createdness.

Method of Knowledge and Response to Irrationality Objection
Maturidi answers the claim that something from nothing is irrational, explaining the proper method: rational evidence, not sensory representation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's critique of demanding sensory evidence for metaphysical truths and his two-fold answer to the objection that creation from nothing is irrational.

Core Idea
Demanding sensory proof for non-sensory matters is like trying to see sounds.

Maturidi responds to a common objection: how can we rationally accept creation from nothing? He first points out the category error of using sensory imagination to judge metaphysical realities. Then he offers two complementary answers, grounding the proof in the impossibility of life arising from a dead thing. This lesson unpacks his method.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objector claims that something from nothing is 'outside the rational' because it cannot be imagined in the mind. Maturidi begins by noting this has been addressed before.
The phrase 'خروج عن المعقول' is key.
2
See the methodological critique
Maturidi rebukes the misuse of sensory standards, comparing it to trying to distinguish sounds by sight or colors by hearing. Each domain has its proper method.
This analogy shows the absurdity of demanding sensory evidence for non-sensory matters.
3
Examine the two-fold answer
Maturidi gives two interpretations of 'conception in imagination': either it means proof-based existence (which we affirm) or it means a physical model (which God is beyond). This answers the objection.
This distinguishes between rational evidence and sensory representation.
4
Grasp the proof from life and death
The proof for the world's origination is that life cannot come from a dead thing. Since living things exist, their life is originated, and so is their death. This shows temporal origination.
The proof is concise: life must be created because it cannot arise from lifeless matter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
{ قال الشيخ رحمه الله : } وما ذُكِرَ من الخروج عن المعقول بما لا يتصوّر في الوهم فقد بيناه . وبعد : فان ذلك عقل خُص به مَن لا عقل له لأنه طلب معرفة ما ليس طريقه الحس بالحس، ، فهو كمن يريد أن يميز بين الأصوات بالبصر وبين الألوان بالسمع، وكذا كل معروف بحس أحب أن يعقل ذلك بغيره فيقصر عنه عقله، فمثله ما كان طريق العلم به غير الحواس، فأراد الوصول إليه بها [ف] لم يسعه عقله . وهذا الجواب جواب لقوله أيضاً : كون شيء من غير شيء خارج من المعقول. وللأمرين جواب آخر ، وهو أن يقال : [إن] تعني بالتصور في الوهم الوجود بالأدلة فهو لازم، ولا نقول بما ليس فيه ذلك. وإن أردت المِثَالَ جَلّ رَبُّنا عن ذلك، بل هو الجاعل لكل ذي المثال مثلاً وهو منشئ ذلك. ودليل حدث العالم إحالة كون حياة في ميت لأنه بها يحيى . ثبت أن حياة الأشياء حدث، فكذلك موتها، إذ قد يكون بعد الحياة .
{ The Shaykh, may God have mercy on him, said: } And what was mentioned of departing from the rational by what is not conceivable in imagination, we have already explained it. Moreover, that is a mind with which he who has no mind is distinguished, because he sought knowledge of that whose path is not sense by sense. So he is like one who wants to distinguish between sounds by sight and between colors by hearing. Likewise, everything known by a sense, if one loves to comprehend it by other than that, his mind falls short of it. Similarly, that whose path of knowledge is other than the senses, if he wants to arrive at it by them, his mind does not suffice him. And this answer is also an answer to his saying: 'The existence of a thing from nothing is outside the rational.' And for the two matters there is another answer, which is to say: If you mean by 'conception in imagination' existence by evidence, then that is necessary, and we do not say what does not have that. But if you want a model, our Lord is exalted above that; rather, He is the one who makes for every possessor of a model a likeness, and He is the originator of that. And the proof for the temporal origination of the world is the impossibility of life being in a dead thing, because by it it lives. It is established that the life of things is originated, and likewise their death, since it may occur after life.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage is a methodological response to the objection that creation ex nihilo is rationally inconceivable. Maturidi first notes that the issue of leaving the rational has been addressed. He then rebukes the objector: demanding sensory evidence for non-sensory matters is foolish—it is like trying to distinguish sounds by sight. Each domain has its proper method. The claim that something from nothing is irrational is answered in two ways. First, if by 'conception in imagination' you mean proof-based existence, we affirm it. Second, if you demand a physical model, God is beyond that; He creates all models. The proof of the world's origination is that life cannot come from a dead thing, yet things live, so life is originated, and death follows.

وهم
حس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi is rejecting rational thought by saying the objection is irrational.
Correction: Maturidi is correcting the misuse of sensory standards for metaphysical truths; he affirms rational evidence.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi compare someone who demands sensory evidence for a non-sensory matter to?
A) A blind person describing colors
B) Someone trying to distinguish sounds by sight
C) A deaf person listening to music
D) A person using taste to see

Key Takeaway
Maturidi defends rational theology by clarifying that each domain of knowledge has its proper method; metaphysical truths are known through evidence, not sensory models.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Refuting the Objection of the Imaginable
Maturidi refutes the claim that kalam arguments go beyond reason and the imaginable

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's defense of kalam against the charge that its topics go beyond rational imagination, and why demanding sensory evidence for unseen matters is misguided.

Core Idea
Each domain of knowledge has its own proper method; the unseen is known by proof, not imagination.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi responds to the objection that kalam arguments about God are invalid because they cannot be imagined or sensed. He provides two powerful answers: first, criticizing the very demand for sensory imagination in non-sensory matters, and second, clarifying what it means for something to be 'imaginable'. This lesson unpacks his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let Maturidi's argument flow through you.
Notice the two distinct answers introduced by 'this answer is also an answer' and 'another answer'.
2
Identify the two answers
Find Maturidi's first answer: criticizing the demand for sensory knowledge. Find the second: clarifying the meaning of 'imaginable'.
The first uses an analogy (sounds by sight). The second distinguishes proof-based existence from physical likeness.
3
Analyze the analogy
Maturidi compares the objector to someone who tries to distinguish sounds by sight. What point is he making about methods of knowledge?
Each sense has its proper object; similarly, each domain has its proper epistemic method.
4
Understand the second answer
If 'imaginable' means provable by evidence, we affirm it. If it means having a physical likeness, God is far above that.
This clarifies the ambiguity in the objection.
5
Reflect on the lesson's core idea
Why does Maturidi consider the objector's demand unreasonable? How does this defense uphold the legitimacy of kalam?
Connect back to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
{ قال الشيخ رحمه الله : } وما ذُكِرَ من الخروج عن المعقول بما لا يتصوّر في الوهم فقد بيناه . وبعد : فان ذلك عقل خُص به مَن لا عقل له لأنه طلب معرفة ما ليس طريقه الحس بالحس، فهو كمن يريد أن يميز بين الأصوات بالبصر وبين الألوان بالسمع، وكذا كل معروف بحس أحب أن يعقل ذلك بغيره فيقصر عنه عقله، فمثله ما كان طريق العلم به غير الحواس، فأراد الوصول إليه بها فلم يسعه عقله . وهذا الجواب جواب لقوله أيضاً : كون شيء من غير شيء خارج من المعقول. وللأمرين جواب آخر ، وهو أن يقال : إن تعني بالتصور في الوهم الوجود بالأدلة فهو لازم، ولا نقول بما ليس فيه ذلك. وإن أردت المِثَالَ جَلّ رَبُّنا عن ذلك، بل هو الجاعل لكل ذي المثال مثلاً وهو منشئ ذلك.
The Shaykh, may Allah have mercy on him, said: And what is mentioned of going beyond the rational by what is not conceivable in imagination, we have already explained it. And after that, that is a mind with which one without a mind is endowed, because he seeks knowledge of what is not known by sensation through sensation. So he is like one who wants to distinguish sounds by sight and colors by hearing. And likewise everything known by one sense, if someone wants to understand it by another, his intellect falls short. Similarly, what has a path of knowledge other than the senses, if one wants to reach it through the senses, his intellect cannot contain it. And this answer is also an answer to his statement: 'the existence of something from nothing is outside the rational.' And for both matters there is another answer, which is to say: If you mean by conception in imagination existence through proofs, then it is necessary, and we do not say what does not involve that. But if you intend a likeness (mithal), our Lord is exalted above that; rather, He is the one who makes for every owner of a likeness a likeness, and He is the originator of that.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection. The opponent argues that kalam arguments about God's attributes or creation go beyond reason because they are not imaginable (mutasawwar fi al-wahm). Maturidi responds in two ways. First, he says such an objection comes from a mind that lacks reason (aqll khussa bihi man la aqla lahu). The opponent is demanding sensory knowledge for non-sensory matters. He uses an analogy: it is like trying to distinguish sounds with sight or colors with hearing. Each domain has its own proper method of knowledge; the unseen (ghayb) is known through rational proofs, not senses. Second, he gives another answer: if the opponent means by 'imaginable' that something can be proven by evidence, then we affirm that for God's existence and attributes—they are proven. But if they demand a physical likeness or example (mithal), God is far above that; He creates examples but is not Himself comparable.

Key Terms
وهم
حس
عقل
مثال
دليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Theological arguments are invalid because they cannot be imagined or sensed.
Correction: Maturidi explains that unseen matters are known through rational proofs, not sensory imagination. Demanding sensory evidence is a category error.

Checkpoint
What analogy does Maturidi use to criticize those who demand sensory imagination for theological reasoning?
A) Trying to taste colors.
B) Trying to distinguish sounds by sight.
C) Trying to weigh ideas.
D) Trying to measure time.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that demanding sensory imagination for unseen matters is a category error; rather, rational proofs are the proper method for theological knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
You are like the one who wants to distinguish sounds by sight every time you try to understand Allah's commands through worldly benefits alone. What is one command you have ignored because it doesn't make sense to your desires, and how will you train your intellect to obey it?
?
Maturidi says that if by 'imaginable' you mean provable, then it is necessary. Have you ever lazily accepted a belief without seeking its proof? Write down one article of faith you hold weakly and resolve to learn its rational evidence from the Quran and kalam this week.

The Method of Tawhid: Starting with Tashbih and Ending with Tawhid
Maturidi explains that tawhid necessarily starts with tashbih and ends with tawhid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's methodological principle that tawhid necessarily begins with conceptual comparison (tashbih) and ends with affirmation of divine uniqueness (tawhid), and why this approach is necessary for both reason and revelation.

Core Idea
Tawhid starts with creation-based concepts and ends with God's uniqueness — the only way to speak about God without falling into ta'til or tashbih.

Maturidi presents a foundational principle of Islamic theology: we can only speak about God by beginning with familiar concepts from creation, but we must end by negating any similarity. This passage explains why this method is not a compromise but a necessity imposed by both reason and revelation. Let's explore how Maturidi justifies this approach.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify key terms
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the terms 'tashbih', 'tawhid', 'ta'til', and 'darurah' (necessity). Note how Maturidi frames his argument.
The passage is dense; read it slowly.
2
Understand the analogy of the afterlife
Maturidi uses the example of perceiving the reward and punishment of the Hereafter through worldly pleasures and pains. This illustrates how we must start with what we know (creation) to infer what we cannot perceive directly (God).
This is a key part of his method: the unseen is understood through the seen.
3
See how the method applies to God's attributes
Maturidi explains that we must affirm attributes like 'Knower' and 'Powerful' based on creation, but then negate likeness by adding 'not like the knowers'. This is how tawhid is achieved: affirmation with negation of similarity.
This avoids both ta'til (negation of attributes) and tashbih (crude anthropomorphism).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأصل في حرف التوحيد أن ابتداءه تشبيه وانتهاءه توحيد، دفعت إلى ذلك الضرورة؛ إذ بالمدرك المفهوم يُستدلّ على ما قصرت الأفهام من إدراك ما [جل] عن الأوهام، نحو ما يدرك ثواب الآخرة وعقابها بلذات الدنيا والأذيات التي فيها . وكذا وصف الله تعالى بالمدرك " من خلقه للدلالة والعبارة، فقيل: «عالم» و«قادر» ونحو ذلك؛ إذ في الإمساك عن ذلك تعطيل، وفي تحقيق المعنى الموجود في خلقه تشبيه، فوصل به «لا كالعلماء» " ونحوه ، ليُجعل نفي التشبيه ضمن الإثبات. فهذا فيما ألزمت ضرورة العقل القول به والسمع جميعا . فأما ما لاسمع فيه ولا في العقل احتماله فالتسمية به جرأة عظيمة. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
The foundation in the matter of tawhid is that its beginning is tashbih and its end is tawhid. Necessity compels to that: because by what is perceived and understood one infers what the intellects fall short of perceiving from what is too sublime for fancies, like how one perceives the reward of the Hereafter and its punishment through the pleasures and pains of this world. Likewise, describing Allah by what is perceived from His creation for indication and expression, so it was said: 'Knower' and 'Powerful' and the like; because refraining from that is ta'til, and actualizing the meaning found in His creation is tashbih. So it is connected with 'not like the knowers' and the like, so that the negation of tashbih is made a part of affirmation. This is that which the necessity of reason and revelation both have obligated to say. As for that which has no basis in revelation nor in reason, naming Him with it is a great audacity. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage explains the methodological principle of tawhid. Maturidi states that we must start with tashbih (using terms conceptually familiar from creation) and end with tawhid (affirming God's uniqueness). This is necessary because we can only conceive of the unseen through the seen. For example, we understand heavenly reward by analogy with worldly pleasures, and we describe God as 'Knower' and 'Powerful' because not doing so is ta'til, but doing so without qualification is tashbih. So we add 'not like the knowers' to negate similarity. This method is obligated by both reason and revelation. Naming God with terms unsupported by revelation or reason is a great audacity.

Key Terms
توحيد
تشبيه
ضرورة
مدرك
تعطيل
إثبات
سمع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that we can describe God directly without any analogy from creation, or that any term derived from creation implies similarity.
Correction: Maturidi shows that it is necessary to start with terms from creation (tashbih) because our minds can only grasp the unseen through the seen, but we must then negate similarity (tawhid) by adding qualifications like 'not like the knowers'.

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi describe the methodological process of tawhid?
A) It begins with tawhid and ends with tashbih
B) It begins with tashbih and ends with tawhid
C) It avoids all similarity
D) It only uses reason

Key Takeaway
Tawhid requires starting with tashbih (conceptual analogy) and ending with tawhid (affirming transcendence), a method necessitated by both reason and revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about Allah, do you ever catch yourself either picturing Him in human terms or avoiding any description altogether? Which tendency do you struggle with more, and what does this lesson demand you change right now?
?
You recite names like 'All-Knowing' or 'All-Powerful' regularly. Starting today, will you pause to add the implicit 'not like the knowers' or 'not like the powerful' in your heart? How will you train yourself to do this consistently?
?
Are you comfortable with the tension of saying Allah is 'Knowing' but not like any knowledge you know? Or do you secretly wish for a simpler way? What is one concrete step you will take this week to live out this method of affirmation-with-negation in your daily awareness of Allah?

Divine Attributes: Affirmation without Likeness
Maturidi states the principle for attributes like istiwa': affirm revelation, negate likeness, no decisive interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's method of affirming divine attributes revealed in scripture while negating any likeness to creation, and why he refrains from fixing a specific interpretation.

Core Idea
Affirm the revelation, negate similarity, and leave the how to Allah.

Imam Maturidi sets out a foundational principle for approaching verses that describe Allah in terms that could be misunderstood anthropomorphically. He anchors the entire discussion in the Quranic verse 'There is nothing like unto Him.' This lesson unpacks that principle and shows how it applies to attributes like istiwa' and the vision of Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the English translation. This helps you absorb Maturidi's original phrasing before interpretation.
2
Identify the foundational verse
Notice that Maturidi starts by quoting 'There is nothing like unto Him' (42:11). This verse is the pillar of his entire approach to divine attributes.
This verse negates any similarity between Allah and His creation.
3
Trace the three-step method
Maturidi applies a consistent method to the verse about istiwa': 1) Affirm the revelation as it came, 2) Negate any likeness to creation, 3) Do not settle on a single interpretation because other meanings are possible and unknown.
This is called the method of tafwid — consigning the meaning to Allah.
4
Extend the principle to other attributes
Maturidi explicitly states that this same method applies to all revealed attributes, such as the vision of Allah in the afterlife. The rule is always the same: affirm, negate likeness, believe without specifying.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله: وأما الأصل عندنا في ذلك أن الله تعالى قال: ليس كمثله شيء، فنفي عن نفسه شبه خلقه، وقد بينا أنه في فعله وصفته متعال عن الأشباه. فيجب القول بالرحمن على العرش استوى على ما جاء به التنزيل ونفى عنه شبه الخلق بما أضاف إليه؛ إذ جاء به التنزيل وثبت ذلك في العقل. ثم لا نقطع في تأويله على شيء لاحتماله غيره مما ذكرنا، واحتماله أيضا ما لم يبلغنا مما يعلم أنه غير محتمل شبه الخلق؛ ونؤمن بما أراد الله به. وكذلك في كل أمر ثبت التنزيل فيه نحو الرؤية وغير ذلك، يجب نفي الشبه عنه والإيمان بما أراده من غير تحقيق على شيء دون شيء. والله الموفق.
Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: And the principle with us regarding that is that Allah the Exalted said: 'There is nothing like unto Him.' So He negated from Himself similarity to His creation. And we have explained that He is in His action and attribute exalted above likenesses. So it is necessary to affirm 'The Most Merciful established Himself upon the Throne' as it came in the revelation, and He negated from Himself similarity to creation by what He added to it, since it came in the revelation and that is established in the intellect. Then we do not definitively decide its interpretation to one thing, because it may admit other [meanings] than what we mentioned, and also it may admit what has not reached us, which is known that it is not similar to creation. And we believe in what Allah intended by it. And similarly, in every matter in which the revelation is established, such as the vision [of Allah] and other than that, it is necessary to negate similarity from Him and to have faith in what He intended, without specifying one thing over another. And Allah is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage is a methodological principle in kalam regarding divine attributes. Maturidi begins by grounding the principle in the Quranic verse 'There is nothing like Him' (al-Shura 42:11), which negates any similarity between Allah and His creation. He states that Allah is transcendent in essence, actions, and attributes. From this, the correct approach to the verse 'The Most Merciful established Himself upon the Throne' (Ta-Ha 20:5) is to affirm it as revealed while negating any anthropomorphic likeness. The revelation is decisive, and reason also supports transcendence. However, Maturidi emphasizes that we do not fix a specific interpretation (ta'wil) for the verse, because it may have multiple valid meanings that are not known to us, all of which are free from resemblance to creation. We simply believe in what Allah intended. This principle applies to all attributes reported in revelation, such as the vision of Allah (ru'ya) in the afterlife: we affirm it, negate likeness, and refrain from specifying how. The key is tanzih (transcendence) and tafwid (consigning the ultimate meaning to Allah).

Key Terms
الأصل
ليس كمثله شيء
شبه
تنزيه
استوى
تأويل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Maturidi denies the literal meaning of 'istawa' and interprets it metaphorically.
Correction: Maturidi affirms the literal revelation of the verse but negates any corporeal similarity, and does not fix any specific interpretation (ta'wil) because the true meaning is known only to Allah.

Checkpoint

B) We affirm it without specifying a meaning
C) We negate it entirely
D) We only accept the literal anthropomorphic meaning

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's principle is to affirm the revealed wording, negate any similarity to creation, and entrust the true meaning to Allah without fixing a single interpretation.

?

The Constraint of Created Concepts on Understanding Divine Attributes
Maturidi states the principle that understanding is constrained by created concepts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that human concepts from creation cannot be applied to Allah's attributes.

Core Idea
Our prior knowledge of Allah's transcendence prevents understanding His attributes through created analogies.

Maturidi begins by laying the epistemological foundation for tafwid: the listener's understanding is inherently shaped by concepts derived from the created world. Since we must affirm Allah's transcendence, we cannot interpret divine attributes using the same framework as for creatures. This lesson explores that fundamental constraint.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let Maturidi's own argument come through before interpreting.
Notice that Maturidi uses the term 'yadiqu' (becomes narrow) to describe the constraint.
2
Identify the key claim
Identify where Maturidi states the principle: the matter becomes narrow for the listener because of his understanding from creation. Ask yourself: why is that a problem?
3
Reflect on the role of prior knowledge
Maturidi emphasizes that prior knowledge of Allah—that He is unlike creation—prevents us from interpreting His attributes in a creaturely manner. Consider how this applies to specific attributes like istiwa'.
This is the epistemological basis for tafwid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل في هذا أن الأمر يضيق على السامع بما يقدره من المفهوم عن الخلق في الوجود. إذ لزم القول في الله بالتعالي عن الأشباه ذاتا وفعلا لم يجز أن يفهم من الإضافة إليه المفهوم من غيره في الوجود. مع ما كان الوقوف على المعنى الذي يصرف إليه الكلام في الخلق بما هو علمه به قبل سمع ذلك الكلام. والله سبحانه عرف قبل سمع ذلك الكلام على غير الذي عليه الخلق، فلم يجز صرف التأويل إلى ما فهمه من الخلق. إذ سببه العلم المتقدم منه. على احتمال ذلك المعنى معنى قد يفهم من الشاهد من «على» ومن «العرش» ومن «الاستواء» من معان مختلفة، لم يجز صرف ذلك إلى أوحش وجه وثمة لأحسن ذلك مساغ. مع ما كان الله يمتحن المكلفين بالوقوف في أشياء، كما جاء من نعوت الوعد والوعيد، وما جاء من الحروف المقطعة، وغير ذلك مما يؤمن المرء أن يكون ذا مما المحنة فيه الوقف لا القطع. والله أعلم.
The principle in this is that the matter becomes narrow for the listener due to what he estimates from the understanding of creation in existence. For when it is necessary to affirm in Allah the transcendence above likenesses in essence and action, it is not permissible to understand from the addition to Him what is understood from other than Him in existence. Moreover, the stopping at the meaning to which the speech is directed regarding creation is based on [prior] knowledge of it before hearing that speech. And Allah the Exalted is known before hearing that speech in a way different from what creation is upon. So it is not permissible to divert interpretation to what he understood from creation. Because its cause is the prior knowledge of that meaning, [and] the meaning that may be understood from the witnessed from 'upon', from 'the Throne', and from 'establishment' are various meanings. So it is not permissible to divert that to the ugliest face when there is scope for the most handsome. Moreover, Allah tests the obligated ones by stopping at things, as has come regarding the descriptions of promise and threat, and what has come of the disconnected letters, and other than that, which cause a person to believe that the test in it is stopping, not cutting. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage provides the epistemological justification for the principle of tafwid (consigning the meaning to Allah) in interpreting divine attributes. Maturidi explains that the listener's understanding is inherently limited by his concepts derived from the created world. Since we must affirm Allah's transcendence in essence and action, we cannot interpret the preposition 'upon' (ala) in 'istawa 'ala al-arsh' in the same way as we do for creatures. Moreover, our prior knowledge of Allah—that He is completely unlike creation—prevents us from imposing created meanings. The terms 'upon', 'the Throne', and 'establishment' have multiple meanings in the witnessed world (e.g., physical elevation vs. authority). Thus, we should not seize upon the ugliest (most anthropomorphic) possible meaning when there exists a more fitting (handsome) interpretation. Finally, Maturidi points out that Allah tests believers by commanding them to stop (waqf) at certain texts, such as the descriptions of paradise and hell or the disconnected letters at the beginnings of surahs. The test lies in not cutting (i.e., not definitively interpreting) but in stopping with faith. This reinforces the methodological stance of accepting revelation as it is without delving into speculative interpretation.

Key Terms
يضيق
المفهوم
الإضافة
التعالي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that tafwid means denying any meaning to the attributes.
Correction: Maturidi does not deny meaning; he argues that the meaning must be divested of created analogies because our prior knowledge of Allah's transcendence prevents us from interpreting them in a human framework.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why can't we understand divine attributes using concepts from creation?
A) Because we lack the intellectual capacity
B) Because our prior knowledge of Allah's transcendence prevents it
C) Because the Quran discourages interpretation
D) Because the attributes are metaphorical

Key Takeaway
Our prior knowledge of Allah's transcendence constrains how we understand His attributes: we cannot apply created concepts to the Divine.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse about Allah's 'hand' or 'face', do you catch your mind automatically forming an image? What concrete step will you take to train yourself to stop that and submit to the text without interpretation?
?

?
Maturidi says our understanding is 'narrowed' by created concepts. Where in your own heart do you feel the pull to explain Allah in human terms? Will you embrace tafwid and let the text stand, even if it leaves you uncomfortable?

Choosing the Handsome Meaning and the Trial of Stopping
Maturidi explains the method for interpreting polysemous terms and the trial of waqf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's method for handling multiple meanings of scriptural terms and his view that Allah tests believers by commanding them to stop at certain texts.

Core Idea
When terms have multiple meanings, choose the one befitting Allah (the handsome), and accept that some texts require stopping, not interpreting.

After establishing the epistemological constraint, Maturidi addresses how to handle terms like 'ala, al-arsh, and istiwa' which have multiple meanings in the witnessed world. He advocates choosing the most appropriate meaning (the handsome) rather than the worst (the ugliest). He then introduces the concept of divine testing through texts where the proper response is to stop (waqf) rather than speculate. This lesson examines these practical hermeneutical guidelines.

Guided Path
1
Examine the multiple meanings
Maturidi lists terms like 'upon', 'the Throne', and 'establishment' that have various meanings in our experience. Identify these terms and note the range of possible meanings.
In Arabic, these are 'ala, al-arsh, and al-istiwa'.
2
Understand the choice between ugly and handsome
Maturidi says we should not divert to the ugliest (orhat) face when there is scope for the most handsome (ahsan). Ask yourself: what makes a meaning 'handsome' in this context?
The handsome meaning is the one that best preserves tanzih.
3
Grasp the test of stopping
Maturidi mentions that Allah tests believers by commanding them to stop at certain texts, like descriptions of paradise and hell or the disconnected letters. Why is stopping a test? Reflect on the idea of trusting Allah without demanding full explanation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل في هذا أن الأمر يضيق على السامع بما يقدره من المفهوم عن الخلق في الوجود. إذ لزم القول في الله بالتعالي عن الأشباه ذاتا وفعلا لم يجز أن يفهم من الإضافة إليه المفهوم من غيره في الوجود. مع ما كان الوقوف على المعنى الذي يصرف إليه الكلام في الخلق بما هو علمه به قبل سمع ذلك الكلام. والله سبحانه عرف قبل سمع ذلك الكلام على غير الذي عليه الخلق، فلم يجز صرف التأويل إلى ما فهمه من الخلق. إذ سببه العلم المتقدم منه. على احتمال ذلك المعنى معنى قد يفهم من الشاهد من «على» ومن «العرش» ومن «الاستواء» من معان مختلفة، لم يجز صرف ذلك إلى أوحش وجه وثمة لأحسن ذلك مساغ. مع ما كان الله يمتحن المكلفين بالوقوف في أشياء، كما جاء من نعوت الوعد والوعيد، وما جاء من الحروف المقطعة، وغير ذلك مما يؤمن المرء أن يكون ذا مما المحنة فيه الوقف لا القطع. والله أعلم.
The principle in this is that the matter becomes narrow for the listener due to what he estimates from the understanding of creation in existence. For when it is necessary to affirm in Allah the transcendence above likenesses in essence and action, it is not permissible to understand from the addition to Him what is understood from other than Him in existence. Moreover, the stopping at the meaning to which the speech is directed regarding creation is based on [prior] knowledge of it before hearing that speech. And Allah the Exalted is known before hearing that speech in a way different from what creation is upon. So it is not permissible to divert interpretation to what he understood from creation. Because its cause is the prior knowledge of that meaning, [and] the meaning that may be understood from the witnessed from 'upon', from 'the Throne', and from 'establishment' are various meanings. So it is not permissible to divert that to the ugliest face when there is scope for the most handsome. Moreover, Allah tests the obligated ones by stopping at things, as has come regarding the descriptions of promise and threat, and what has come of the disconnected letters, and other than that, which cause a person to believe that the test in it is stopping, not cutting. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage provides the epistemological justification for the principle of tafwid (consigning the meaning to Allah) in interpreting divine attributes. Maturidi explains that the listener's understanding is inherently limited by his concepts derived from the created world. Since we must affirm Allah's transcendence in essence and action, we cannot interpret the preposition 'upon' (ala) in 'istawa 'ala al-arsh' in the same way as we do for creatures. Moreover, our prior knowledge of Allah—that He is completely unlike creation—prevents us from imposing created meanings. The terms 'upon', 'the Throne', and 'establishment' have multiple meanings in the witnessed world (e.g., physical elevation vs. authority). Thus, we should not seize upon the ugliest (most anthropomorphic) possible meaning when there exists a more fitting (handsome) interpretation. Finally, Maturidi points out that Allah tests believers by commanding them to stop (waqf) at certain texts, such as the descriptions of paradise and hell or the disconnected letters at the beginnings of surahs. The test lies in not cutting (i.e., not definitively interpreting) but in stopping with faith. This reinforces the methodological stance of accepting revelation as it is without delving into speculative interpretation.

Key Terms
الشاهد
أوحش
مساغ
الوقف
المحنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if a term has multiple meanings, we must choose the literal meaning.
Correction: Maturidi argues we should choose the most suitable meaning (the handsome) that aligns with Allah's transcendence, not automatically the literal.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi mean by 'stopping' (waqf) in the context of divine attributes?
A) Stopping reading the Quran
B) Refraining from definitive interpretation
C) Stopping to reflect
D) Stopping prayer

Key Takeaway
When terms have multiple meanings, choose the one that befits Allah's transcendence; and accept that some texts are a test to stop rather than interpret.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a verse about Allah that you cannot fully understand, does your heart feel restless until you find an explanation, or can you humbly stop and trust that Allah knows best? Which response reflects true submission?
?
Do you ever find yourself preferring an interpretation of a divine attribute simply because it feels comfortable or intellectually satisfying, without checking if it truly befits Allah's majesty? What would it cost you to set that aside and choose the meaning that upholds tanzih instead?
?
If Allah tested you by placing a text before you that demands waqf—a stop in your understanding—would your faith be strong enough to say 'I believe in it, all of it is from my Lord' without needing to dissect it? Or would your reliance on your own reasoning become an idol?

Maturidi's Linguistic Principle for 'Ru'ya'
Maturidi states the rule for when 'ru'ya' is figurative, citing Quranic examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's rule that the word 'ru'ya' is taken figuratively when it is used in a customary context or accompanied by an intended meaning, otherwise literally.

Core Idea
In the Quran, 'seeing' can be figurative when the context involves a customary action.

Maturidi begins by referencing the Quranic verse about faces looking at their Lord. He then lays down a crucial linguistic principle: the word 'ru'ya' (seeing) is used literally unless the context points to a customary or intended meaning. This principle helps interpret verses like 'Do you not see your Lord, how He extends the shade?' where 'seeing' is not literal vision.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the principle
Read the Arabic and translation. Locate the sentence starting with 'والأصل في الكلام' – this is the core principle. Notice the condition: 'otherwise not'.
The principle states when to turn from literal meaning.
2
Examine the Quranic examples
Maturidi cites two Quranic examples: 'ألم تر إلى ربك كيف مد الظل' and 'ألم تر كيف فعل ربك'. Consider what these verses mean – is 'seeing' literal?
These verses use 'ra'a' to mean 'consider' or 'ponder'.
3
Understand the linguistic basis in ordinary speech
Maturidi explains that if someone says 'I saw Zayd', it means seeing his person, but if they say 'I saw him doing something', it does not imply seeing his essence. This distinction grounds the principle.
This clarifies figurative versus literal.
4
Apply the principle to the verse about faces
The passage concludes that the verse about faces looking at their Lord follows the same rule. Discuss what this means for interpreting that verse.
Maturidi's view is that the 'looking' is not literal vision of Allah's essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد بينا ما قال في قوله: وجوه يومئذ ناضرة إلى ربها ناظرة. قال الفقيه رحمه الله: والأصل في الكلام أنه إذا كان على أمر معهود أو يقرن به المقصود إليه صرف عن حقيقته، وإلا لا. وذلك نحو قوله: ألم تر إلى ربك كيف مد الظل، وألم تر كيف فعل ربك... وأصله أن من قال: رأيت فلانا أو نظرت إلى فلان لم يحتمل غير ذاته؛ وإذا قال: رأيته يقول كذا ويفعل كذا إنه لا يريد به رؤية ذاته، فمثله أمر قصة موسى عليه السلام وهذه الآية.
And we have explained what he said in His saying: 'Faces on that day will be radiant, looking at their Lord.' Al-Faqih (may Allah have mercy on him) said: The principle in speech is that if it is about a customary matter or the intended meaning is attached to it, it is turned from its literal meaning, otherwise not. That is like His saying: 'Do you not see your Lord, how He extends the shade?' and 'Do you not see how your Lord did...' And its origin is that if someone says 'I saw Zayd' or 'I looked at Zayd', it cannot mean other than his essence; and if he says 'I saw him saying such and such and doing such and such', he does not mean thereby the vision of his essence. The same is the case of the story of Moses, peace be upon him, and this verse.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage establishes a linguistic principle for interpreting the word 'ru'ya' (seeing) in the Quran. Maturidi first references the verse 'Faces on that day will be radiant, looking at their Lord.' Then he states the principle: if the word is used in a context that is customary ('ala amr ma'hud) or accompanied by an intended meaning, it is taken figuratively, otherwise it is literal. He gives examples: 'Do you not see your Lord, how He extends the shade?' and 'Do you not see how your Lord did...' The rule is that if someone says 'I saw Zayd' or 'I looked at Zayd', it can only mean seeing his person. But if they say 'I saw him saying such-and-such', it does not mean seeing his essence. The same applies to the story of Musa and the verse about faces looking at their Lord.

Key Terms
الأصل في الكلام
صرف عن حقيقته
رأيته

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Every occurrence of 'ra'a' in the Quran implies literal vision.
Correction: Maturidi shows that 'ra'a' can be figurative when used in a customary context or with an attached intention, as in the examples he gives.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi's principle, when is the word 'ru'ya' taken figuratively?
A) When it refers to physical objects
B) When it is used in a customary context or accompanied by an intended meaning
C) When it appears in the Meccan surahs
D) When it is used in a command

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's principle: 'ru'ya' is figurative when the context involves a customary matter or attached intention.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read about Allah's attributes in the Quran, do you ever pause to consider whether a literal interpretation is always intended? Or do you assume that the obvious meaning is the correct one? This lesson calls you to examine your own hermeneutic. Will you commit to studying the principles of interpretation before forming conclusions?
?
Maturidi uses ordinary human language as a guide to understanding divine speech. In your own life, do you apply similar careful reasoning when reading religious texts, or do you rush to judgment based on first impressions? Take a moment to reflect: what would it look like for you to approach the Quran with the same precision that Maturidi applies?
?
The verse about faces looking at their Lord is often cited to prove literal vision of Allah. Yet Maturidi, a great scholar, says it is figurative. Are you willing to submit to scholarly reasoning even when it contradicts a popular belief you may have held? What would that cost you? Pray for the humility to accept truth wherever it lies.

The Hidden Self and Knowing God
Maturidi reports and comments on the saying about knowing the hidden self

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of the hidden self as defined by Maturidi and how he balances self-knowledge with external proofs for knowing God.

Core Idea
Your hidden self is a key to knowing God, but external proofs also suffice.

This passage from Imam Maturidi introduces a profound spiritual principle: the knowledge of one's inner, hidden self leads to knowledge of the Creator. He explains what this hidden self is and then offers a balanced evaluation, showing that while the saying is good, the rational arguments from creation are also sufficient. This lesson unpacks Maturidi's commentary and its implications for theological methodology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Maturidi first quotes a saying, then defines the term, then gives his own opinion.
2
Identify the definition of hidden self
Find the four characteristics of the hidden self: rectification of affairs, capacity for lofty things, management of creatures, and perception of hidden things through thought and causes.
3
Understand Maturidi's evaluation
Maturidi says the saying is good, but then points out that what he has previously mentioned (the rational arguments) is sufficient for knowing the Creator, without needing to fully perceive the hidden self.
4
Reflect on the relationship
Consider how both self-knowledge and external proofs lead to God. Maturidi does not dismiss the saying but shows that the path of reasoning is already enough.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال قوم من عرف نفسه الخفيّة عرف ربه ونفسه الخفيّة هي الكيان المجعول لصلاح الأمور، واحتمال المعالي، ومُلْكِ تدبير الخلائق، ودَرْكِ الخفيات من الأمور بالفكر والنظر في الأسباب. وما قاله حسن. وقد يقع بما ذكرت في معرفة الصانع كفاية عن دَرْك الخفي به، بما خفى من أحواله، ووصل إلى العلم بما استتر وظهر بالأسباب. وبه يَعْرِف ما خفى منه، سُمّي نفسًا أوّلاً وظهر [بعد]. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And some people said: Whoever knows his hidden self knows his Lord. And his hidden self is the entity made for the rectification of affairs, the capacity for lofty things, the kingdom of management of creatures, and the perception of hidden things through thought and consideration of causes. And what he said is good. And what I mentioned may suffice in knowing the Maker, without the perception of the hidden by it, because of what is hidden of his states, and he arrives at knowledge of what is concealed and manifest through causes. And by it he knows what is hidden from him; it is called a soul first and then appears [afterwards]. And there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage reports a saying and gives a comment. Some people claim that knowing one's hidden self (النفس الخفية) leads to knowing God. Maturidi explains that this hidden self is the entity that manages affairs, grasps lofty matters, and perceives hidden things through thought. He then comments that this saying is good, but what he has already mentioned (the arguments from design and capacity) is sufficient for knowing the Creator, without needing to perceive the hidden self entirely. Through causes, one arrives at knowledge of what is hidden and manifest. The hidden self is called a soul first and then later appears.

كيان
فكر
نظر
أسباب

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowing one's hidden self is the only way to know God.
Correction: Maturidi affirms that external proofs from creation are also sufficient for knowing the Creator, so self-knowledge is not the exclusive path.

Checkpoint

A) The soul that is sinful
B) The entity that manages and perceives hidden things
C) The physical body
D) The intellect only

Key Takeaway
Maturidi acknowledges the value of self-knowledge but emphasizes that rational reflection on creation is enough to know the Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Your hidden self is capable of lofty things and managing affairs, yet you often waste your intellect on trivial matters. How can you begin to use your thought and consideration of causes to perceive what is hidden, starting this week?
?
The phrase 'There is no power except through Allah' ends this passage. Do you live as if you truly believe that? Where in your life are you acting as if your own power suffices, ignoring that all ability comes from Him?

Three Bases for Separation and Union
Maturidi states the principle that separation and union must be by nature, choice, or another cause; then begins examining the first possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's principle that the states of separation and union can only arise from three possible causes: nature, choice, or an external cause, and the beginning of his examination of the natural basis.

Core Idea
Separation and union arise from only three causes: nature, choice, or an external cause.

Imam Maturidi begins his analysis of how things become separate or united. He establishes a foundational principle: the cause must be one of three possibilities. This lesson presents that principle and follows his initial reasoning into the first possibility — nature — though the argument continues beyond the available text.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully, noting the three possibilities listed. This passage is incomplete, but the principle is clear.
The text breaks off mid-sentence after 'أن'.
2
Identify the three causes
Find the three causes listed: nature (الطبع), choice (الاختيار), and another cause (آخر يجعلهما كذلك).
3
Understand the first possibility
Maturidi begins to explore what would follow if separation/union were by nature. Notice his conditional reasoning: 'if they were so by nature, then it would be necessary that they increase...' The argument is cut off.
The full argument likely continues on the next page.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله والأصل في ذلك أنهما عند التباين لا يعدو إما أن كانا كذلك بالطبع أو بالاختيار أو بآخر يجعلهما كذلك وكذلك المجتمع منه ثم التباين والامتزاج لا يعدوان ما ذكرنا فإن كانا كذلك بالطبع لوجب أن يزداد من ذلك فيما كان أصله التباين أو الاجتماع أن
Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: The principle in that is that they (the two states), when separation [occurs], cannot escape either being so by nature, or by choice, or by another that makes them so. And likewise, the united from it. Then separation and mixture do not exceed what we mentioned. If they were so by nature, then it would be necessary that they increase from that in what its origin was separation or union, that...
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of Maturidi's own analytical principle regarding the basis of separation and union. He states that when two things are in a state of separation, the cause of that separation must be one of three: (1) by nature (tab'), i.e., it is inherent to them, (2) by choice (ikhtiyar), i.e., they voluntarily separate, or (3) by another cause external to them that makes them separate. Similarly for the state of union. He then notes that separation and mixture cannot go beyond these three possibilities. He begins examining the first possibility (by nature), stating that if they were so by nature, then it would be necessary that they increase in that state — but the text cuts off, so the full argument is not present in this window.

Key Terms
الطبع
الاختيار
التباين
الامتزاج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that this is a complete argument or that the three causes are exhaustive in a different sense.
Correction: Maturidi is stating a methodological principle for analyzing physical phenomena; the passage is only the beginning of the argument, and the three causes are proposed as exhaustive logical possibilities for explaining separation/union.

Checkpoint

A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's principle for analyzing separation and union involves only three possible causes: nature, choice, or an external cause; the argument for the natural cause is initiated but incomplete.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience spiritual distance from Allah, do you honestly attribute it to a natural disposition (e.g., 'this is just how I am'), to your own choices, or to external circumstances beyond your control? ?
Consider a recent conflict or separation in a relationship. According to Maturidi's principle, was it caused by inherent nature, deliberate choice, or an external factor? If you could relive that moment, would you change your choice? How does this lesson confront you with the reality of your own agency?
?
The passage assumes that every separation and union falls into one of three causes. Think of one area of your life where you feel stuck or disconnected. What is the true cause? Are you blaming 'nature' or 'others' when it is actually your own choice to remain passive? Let this lesson push you to act.

The Only Path to Knowledge of God
Maturidi states that God is known only through the world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that according to Maturidi, knowledge of God is only attainable through the world's indication, not through direct sensory or rational access.

Core Idea
The world is the only window to know God.

Maturidi begins his epistemological foundation by asserting that there is no direct access to God. Our senses and even revelation (in terms of direct witnessing) cannot reach Him. Instead, the created world serves as the sole indicator. This lesson introduces that principle and the immediate conclusion that the world points to God's attributes, not His essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp Maturidi's claim.
Notice the emphasis on 'لا سبيل إلى العلم به إلا من طريق دلالة العالم'.
2
Identify the epistemological principle
Understand why Maturidi says that the avenues of the senses and hearing are cut off.
This establishes that we cannot know God directly.
3
Distinguish between essence and attributes
The passage states that the witness (world) indicates the attribute, not the essence. Reflect on why this distinction matters.
The example of existence after non-existence points to creation, an attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : والأصل أن الله تعالى إذ لا سبيل إلى العلم به إلا من طريق دلالة العالم عليه، بانقطاع وجوه الوصول إلى معرفته من طريق الحواس عليه أو شهادة السمع. ثم الشاهد يدل عليه من وجه الشهادة له بالصفة لا من وجه الشهادة بالذات؛ إذ الوجود بعد أن لم يكن هو دليل الإيجاد والإحداث الذي به يعلم الموجود المحدث. واختلاف أحوال الشاهد واجتماع المتضاد في الواحد هو دليل قدرته ونفاذ التدبير الذي بالمدبر القوى يكون، واتساق التدبير، وعدم التفاوت في الواقع تحت العقل على كثرته دليل علم العقل الذي به يُعلم العالم. ولا شيء في المحسوس يدل على ذات إذا نفي عنه الصفة، لم يجز القول بإثبات ذات غير تحقيق الصفات، إذ ذلك غير طريق شهادة العيان. وكذلك شهادة من ثبت صدقهم بالأدلة جاء بالعليم السميع البصير على ذكر العلم والقدرة ونحو ذلك، مع العلم أن هذه الأسماء من أسماء الصفات. ثم إذ لم يجز الوصف بالمكان وبالخروج أو الدخول أو الاتصال أو الانفصال أو البينونة أو نحو ذلك على نفي تلك الأحوال، من غير إثبات تحقيق الملفوظ ـ وكذلك شأن الاجتماع والافتراق والتحرك والسكون - لم يجز الذي قالوه، وبالله التوفيق.
Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: The foundation is that Allah the Exalted, since there is no way to knowledge of Him except through the way of the world's indication of Him, due to the cutting off of avenues of reaching knowledge of Him through the senses or testimony of hearing. Then the witness indicates Him from the aspect of bearing witness to the attribute, not from the aspect of bearing witness to the essence; for existence after non-existence is the proof of origination and invention through which the originated existent is known. And the differences of the states of the witness and the combination of opposites in one thing is the proof of His power and the penetration of governance which is by the powerful governor, and the coherence of governance, and the absence of discrepancy in what is subject to reason despite its abundance is the proof of the knowledge of the intellect by which the knower is known. And nothing in the sensible indicates an essence if the attribute is negated from it; it is not permissible to say: affirming an essence without verifying the attributes, for that is not the way of the testimony of direct observation. Likewise, the testimony of those whose truthfulness is established by proofs has come with the All-Knowing, the All-Hearing, the All-Seeing, mentioning knowledge and power and so on, with the knowledge that these names are among the names of attributes. Then when it is not permissible to describe with place and with exiting or entering or connection or separation or being distinct or similar [attributes] upon negation of those states, without affirming the verification of the uttered [term] – and likewise the matter of conjunction, disjunction, movement, and stillness – then that which they said is not permissible. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage is a methodological statement by Maturidi on how knowledge of God is attained. He begins by establishing the epistemological principle: there is no way to know God except through the creation (al-‘ālam), because sensory or direct access to His essence is impossible. The visible world (shāhid) indicates God by pointing to His attributes, not to His essence directly. For example, the fact that things come into existence after non-existence proves the act of creation (ījād, ihdāth), which in turn points to a Creator with power and will. Maturidi then explains that the variation of states in the world and the combination of opposites in a single being (like life and death in a human) are proofs of God's power and governance. The coherence and absence of discrepancy in the world, despite its vastness, is a proof of God's knowledge. He argues that sensory experience never reveals an essence stripped of attributes; therefore, it is invalid to affirm an essence without affirming its attributes when speaking of God. The prophets, whose truthfulness is proven, have come with names like 'al-‘Alim, al-Sami‘, al-Basir', which explicitly refer to attributes (knowledge, hearing, sight). Maturidi concludes that just as it is impermissible to describe God with terms like place or movement without negating them while affirming the meaning, similarly the opponents' denial of attributes is invalid. The proper method is to affirm the attributes as they are described in revelation and reason.

Key Terms
دلالة العالم (dilāla al-‘ālam)
الشاهد (al-shāhid)
الصفة (al-ṣifa)
الذات (al-dhāt)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: We can know God's essence directly through intellectual intuition or mystical experience.
Correction: Maturidi explicitly says the avenues through senses and hearing are cut off, and the world only indicates attributes, not essence.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi in this passage, what is the only way to knowledge of God?
A) Through direct sensory perception
B) Through the indication of the world
C) Through rational deduction alone
D) Through revealed scripture only

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of God is only possible through the world's indication, and the world reveals His attributes, not His essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the world—the sky, the trees, your own existence—do you truly see them as signs pointing to God's attributes, or do you just see them as random objects? What would it take for you to see each moment as a proof of His creative power?
?
Maturidi says even revelation (hearing) is not a direct avenue to knowing God's essence. How does this challenge your tendency to rely solely on religious texts or experiences? Are you willing to also reflect deeply on creation as a source of knowledge, or do you neglect that duty?
?
You claim to believe in God, but if you were asked to prove His existence using only this world, could you do it convincingly? What specific attribute of God can you point to right now from something you see or experience? If you struggle, what does that say about your active engagement with the world as a sign?

The World as Proof of Attributes: Power, Knowledge, and Rejection of Denial
Maturidi presents proofs from the world for God's attributes and refutes the denial of attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the world's varied states, coherence, and prophetic testimony to prove God's attributes of power and knowledge, and why denying attributes is invalid.

Core Idea
The world's diversity and harmony point to a powerful and knowing Creator.

Maturidi continues to explain how the world functions as a witness. He identifies specific observable phenomena that prove God's attributes: the combination of opposites in one being demonstrates power, the coherence and lack of discrepancy in the world demonstrates knowledge. He also addresses the issue of affirming attributes: since sensory experience never reveals an essence without attributes, it is invalid to speak of God's essence while negating His attributes. Prophetic testimony further confirms attribute names.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage fully
Identify the three main proofs Maturidi presents: proof of power, proof of knowledge, and the argument against denying attributes.
The passage is dense; read carefully.
2
Understand the proof of power
How does the combination of opposites and variation in states indicate God's power and governance?
Look for the phrase 'واجتماع المتضاد في الواحد هو دليل قدرته'.
3
Understand the proof of knowledge
How does the coherence and absence of discrepancy indicate knowledge?
Consider how a system without errors implies a knowing designer.
4
Analyze the argument on attributes
Note that nothing in the sensible indicates an essence without attributes, and prophetic testimony supports attribute names.
This refutes those who deny attributes.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Maturidi equates denying attributes to describing God with physical terms—both are impermissible.
The correct method is to affirm attributes as described.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : والأصل أن الله تعالى إذ لا سبيل إلى العلم به إلا من طريق دلالة العالم عليه، بانقطاع وجوه الوصول إلى معرفته من طريق الحواس عليه أو شهادة السمع. ثم الشاهد يدل عليه من وجه الشهادة له بالصفة لا من وجه الشهادة بالذات؛ إذ الوجود بعد أن لم يكن هو دليل الإيجاد والإحداث الذي به يعلم الموجود المحدث. واختلاف أحوال الشاهد واجتماع المتضاد في الواحد هو دليل قدرته ونفاذ التدبير الذي بالمدبر القوى يكون، واتساق التدبير، وعدم التفاوت في الواقع تحت العقل على كثرته دليل علم العقل الذي به يُعلم العالم. ولا شيء في المحسوس يدل على ذات إذا نفي عنه الصفة، لم يجز القول بإثبات ذات غير تحقيق الصفات، إذ ذلك غير طريق شهادة العيان. وكذلك شهادة من ثبت صدقهم بالأدلة جاء بالعليم السميع البصير على ذكر العلم والقدرة ونحو ذلك، مع العلم أن هذه الأسماء من أسماء الصفات. ثم إذ لم يجز الوصف بالمكان وبالخروج أو الدخول أو الاتصال أو الانفصال أو البينونة أو نحو ذلك على نفي تلك الأحوال، من غير إثبات تحقيق الملفوظ ـ وكذلك شأن الاجتماع والافتراق والتحرك والسكون - لم يجز الذي قالوه، وبالله التوفيق.
Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: The foundation is that Allah the Exalted, since there is no way to knowledge of Him except through the way of the world's indication of Him, due to the cutting off of avenues of reaching knowledge of Him through the senses or testimony of hearing. Then the witness indicates Him from the aspect of bearing witness to the attribute, not from the aspect of bearing witness to the essence; for existence after non-existence is the proof of origination and invention through which the originated existent is known. And the differences of the states of the witness and the combination of opposites in one thing is the proof of His power and the penetration of governance which is by the powerful governor, and the coherence of governance, and the absence of discrepancy in what is subject to reason despite its abundance is the proof of the knowledge of the intellect by which the knower is known. And nothing in the sensible indicates an essence if the attribute is negated from it; it is not permissible to say: affirming an essence without verifying the attributes, for that is not the way of the testimony of direct observation. Likewise, the testimony of those whose truthfulness is established by proofs has come with the All-Knowing, the All-Hearing, the All-Seeing, mentioning knowledge and power and so on, with the knowledge that these names are among the names of attributes. Then when it is not permissible to describe with place and with exiting or entering or connection or separation or being distinct or similar [attributes] upon negation of those states, without affirming the verification of the uttered [term] – and likewise the matter of conjunction, disjunction, movement, and stillness – then that which they said is not permissible. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage is a methodological statement by Maturidi on how knowledge of God is attained. He begins by establishing the epistemological principle: there is no way to know God except through the creation (al-‘ālam), because sensory or direct access to His essence is impossible. The visible world (shāhid) indicates God by pointing to His attributes, not to His essence directly. For example, the fact that things come into existence after non-existence proves the act of creation (ījād, ihdāth), which in turn points to a Creator with power and will. Maturidi then explains that the variation of states in the world and the combination of opposites in a single being (like life and death in a human) are proofs of God's power and governance. The coherence and absence of discrepancy in the world, despite its vastness, is a proof of God's knowledge. He argues that sensory experience never reveals an essence stripped of attributes; therefore, it is invalid to affirm an essence without affirming its attributes when speaking of God. The prophets, whose truthfulness is proven, have come with names like 'al-‘Alim, al-Sami‘, al-Basir', which explicitly refer to attributes (knowledge, hearing, sight). Maturidi concludes that just as it is impermissible to describe God with terms like place or movement without negating them while affirming the meaning, similarly the opponents' denial of attributes is invalid. The proper method is to affirm the attributes as they are described in revelation and reason.

Key Terms
الإيجاد (al-ījād)
الإحداث (al-iḥdāth)
القدرة (al-qudra)
العلم (al-‘ilm)
التدبير (al-tadbīr)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's essence can be known directly through the world without attributes.
Correction: Maturidi asserts that the world only indicates attributes, not essence, and that affirming essence without attributes is invalid.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does the combination of opposites in one thing prove about God?
A) His knowledge
B) His power
C) His essence
D) His oneness

Key Takeaway
The world's diversity and harmony prove God's power and knowledge, and denying attributes contradicts both reason and revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the vastness and order of the universe, do you truly see it as a witness to God's power and knowledge, or has familiarity dulled its impact? What intentional step will you take today to see the world as a constant proof?
?
You claim to believe that God is All-Knowing and All-Powerful, but do your private thoughts and actions reflect that belief? If you really believed He sees and controls everything, what is one habit you would change starting now?
?
This lesson argues that denying attributes is not even supported by sensory evidence. Is there any attribute of God that you find uncomfortable or that you subtly diminish in your heart? How can you affirm it fully in both belief and action?

Why Imam al-Maturidi Mentions Mu'tazila Views
Maturidi explains the purpose of citing Mu'tazila opinions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the methodological purpose behind citing heretical views in kalam works.

Core Idea
Citing false views is for refutation, not endorsement.

Imam al-Maturidi prefaces his discussion of tawhid by clarifying why he brings up the opinions of the Mu'tazila. He wants to prevent misunderstanding and show that their approach cannot prove tawhid. This brief passage sets the stage for the correct Sunni methodology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the phrase 'though I had no need'.
2
Identify the purpose statement
Find the clause that begins with 'so that the observer may know'. This is the central purpose: to show the futility of the Mu'tazila method in proving tawhid.
3
Reflect on the closing phrase
The passage ends with 'la hawla wa la quwwata illa billah'. Consider why a scholar of kalam would end a methodological statement with this expression of reliance on Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما أردت بما ذكرت من أقاويل المعتزلة - وإن لم يكن لى إلى ذكرها حاجة ـ ليعلم المتأمل أن لا سبيل إلى إثبات التوحيد ودفع معارضات الملحدة على مذهبهم، وأن الحق من القول في التوحيد قول غيرهم. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
I only intended by what I mentioned of the sayings of the Mu'tazila - though I had no need to mention them - so that the observer may know that there is no way to establish tawhid and repel the objections of the heretics according to their doctrine, and that the truth of the statement regarding tawhid is the statement of others. And there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage is a methodological preamble. Imam al-Maturidi states that he mentioned the opinions of the Mu'tazila not out of necessity but to demonstrate that their approach cannot successfully prove tawhid nor refute the objections of the atheists. The correct position on tawhid is that of other groups (i.e., Ahl al-Sunna). He ends with the phrase 'la hawla wa la quwwata illa billah' as an expression of reliance on Allah.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
التوحيد
الملحدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Citing heretical views implies endorsement or need to learn from them.
Correction: Imam al-Maturidi clarifies that he has no need to mention them; it is only to demonstrate the futility of their method.

Checkpoint
What is the purpose of mentioning the Mu'tazila views according to Imam al-Maturidi?
A) To endorse their doctrine
B) To show that their doctrine cannot establish tawhid
C) To learn from their arguments
D) To create confusion

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Maturidi mentions Mu'tazila views only to demonstrate that their approach cannot prove tawhid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt your faith shaken by arguments that claim to disprove God's existence? How does this lesson give you confidence that only the Sunni method can truly refute such objections?
?
When you hear someone attacking Islamic beliefs, do you feel compelled to engage every argument? What does Imam al-Maturidi's example teach you about the wisdom of choosing when and how to respond?
?
The author ends with 'there is no power except through Allah.' In your own efforts to defend your faith, are you relying on your own intellect or truly depending on Allah? Take a moment to repent for any arrogance and commit to seeking His help.

Defense of Rational Inquiry – The Objection of 'Abstaining is Safer'
Maturidi presents and begins to refute the claim that refraining from inquiry is safer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument of those who oppose rational inquiry and why Imam al-Maturidi insists that inquiry is necessary for discernment.

Core Idea
Refraining from inquiry does not guarantee safety; it leaves one without discernment.

Some argue that engaging in theological reasoning is risky because one might not attain truth and might open oneself to argument. Imam al-Maturidi begins his defense by presenting this objection and then countering that without inquiry, one cannot distinguish truth from falsehood. This lesson explores that exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English carefully. Notice that the passage first presents the opposing view, then begins to respond.
The passage is cut off, but the direction is clear.
2
Identify the opponents' claim
Find the sentence where the opponents say 'ترك النظر أسلم' (refraining is safer) and list their reasons.
They mention uncertainty and opening the door to argument.
3
See Maturidi's first counter
The text then says that through thought one intends to attain certain knowledge, amid the confusion between divine and satanic inspiration.
This shows that inquiry is needed to discern truth.
4
Reflect on the incomplete ending
The passage ends mid-sentence, but we can infer that Maturidi argues that refraining leaves one without any basis for discernment.
Consider how this fits into his broader defense of reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال قوم ترك النظر أسلم لما لا يأمن الناظر بالظفر بالحق. ثم فيه فتح باب الحجة على نفسه؛ مما لو امتنع ليأ من العَطَب، من حيث لولا هو لم يتخلله السبيل الذي يظنه أو الباطل ليلزمه حجة الله ؛ إذ بالفكر والبحث إرادة ما يضطر إلى العلم بأن الحق في ما انكشف له مع اشتباه خاطر الرحمن في الأمر والتحذير من خاطر الشيطان. وفي ترك النظر والبحث أمن ذلك، إذ لم ينكشف له ما يُلزمة التمييز ، ولا يخطر بذهنه ما يبعثه على
Some people said: Refraining from inquiry (al-naẓar) is safer, because the inquirer is not certain of attaining truth. Moreover, it opens the door of argument against oneself; such that if he refrains he would be safe from harm, because without it there would not interpose the path he supposes [to be truth] or falsehood to make the proof of Allah binding upon him. For through thought and investigation there is an intention that compels knowledge that the truth is what has been revealed to him, amidst the confusion of the thought of the Merciful concerning the matter and the warning against the thought of Satan. And in refraining from inquiry and investigation there is safety from that, for nothing is revealed to him that obliges discernment, nor does there occur to his mind what would impel him to...
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage is a defense of rational inquiry (al-naẓar) against those who claim it is safer to avoid it because one might not reach the truth or might fall into error. Imam al-Maturidi presents their argument: they say inquiry opens the door to argument against oneself, and if one refrains, one avoids the risk of being held accountable by God's proof. The text then begins to respond: through thought and investigation one aims to attain certain knowledge that the truth is what has been revealed, amid the confusion between divine inspiration and satanic whisper. The passage is cut off, but the gist is that refraining from inquiry does not guarantee safety; rather it leaves one without discernment. This is a characteristic Maturidi position defending the use of reason in theology.

فكر
بحث
حجة الله
خاطر الرحمن
خاطر الشيطان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Refraining from inquiry is safer because it avoids error and uncertainty.
Correction: Maturidi shows that without inquiry, one cannot even begin to discern truth from falsehood, leaving one without any basis for certainty and thus not safe from divine accountability.

Checkpoint

A) It is forbidden by scripture
B) It opens the door to argument and one may not reach truth
C) It leads to atheism
D) It is too difficult for ordinary people

Key Takeaway
Maturidi argues that rational inquiry is necessary for discernment, and refraining from it does not provide safety but rather leaves one without the means to distinguish truth from falsehood.
