Do you take this responsibility seriously, or do you prefer to remain passive? Commit to one specific way you will actively train your discernment this week.
?
The opponents claimed that refraining from inquiry is 'safer.' But Maturidi says it leaves you without discernment. In what area of your life have you chosen the 'safer' path of not asking difficult questions? What is the cost of that safety? Will you now take the risk of inquiry?

The Obligation of Rational Inquiry Grounded in the Intellect
Maturidi presents the objection against nazar and grounds its obligation in the intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the obligation of rational inquiry (nazar) is rooted in the intellect itself, not in a prior act of inquiry, and how this refutes the claim that refraining is safer.

Core Idea
Rational inquiry is not optional—your own intellect obliges you to seek truth.

Imam Maturidi opens this passage by addressing a common objection: some argue that refraining from theological inquiry is safer because one might fail to find the truth. In this lesson, we will see how he refutes this by demonstrating that the obligation to inquire stems directly from the intellect God has given us, not from any contingent act of inquiry.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial refutation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and translation, noting the objectors' claim that refraining is safer and Maturidi's counter-claim.
The objectors argue that inquiry opens the door to proof against oneself.
2
Examine the key claim: obligation from intellect
Focus on the phrase 'لأن لزوم النظر ليس عقيب نظر تقدمه بل عقيب الذي به يقع النظر والبحث وهو العقل'. This is the core of the argument.
The intellect is the source of the obligation, not a prior act of inquiry.
3
Reflect on why this makes inquiry inescapable
Consider the implications: if the intellect itself obliges inquiry, then refraining is not a neutral choice but a neglect of one's God-given faculty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة [ دفاع عن العلم بالنظر ] قال قوم ترك النظر أسلم لما لا يأمن الناظر بالظفر بالحق. ثم فيه فتح باب الحجة على نفسه؛ مما لو امتنع ليأ من العَطَب، من حيث لولا هو لم يتخلله السبيل الذي يظنه أو الباطل ليلزمه حجة الله؛ إذ بالفكر والبحث إرادة ما يضطر إلى العلم بأن الحق في ما انكشف له مع اشتباه خاطر الرحمن في الأمر والتحذير من خاطر الشيطان. وفي ترك النظر والبحث أمن ذلك، إذ لم ينكشف له ما يُلزمه التمييز ، ولا يخطر بذهنه ما يبعثه على الطلب. ولا قوة إلا بالله . و من ألزم النظر والبحث فيقول : في تركه عَطَبه لا محالة؛ لأن لزوم النظر ليس عقيب نظر تقدمه بل عقيب الذي به يقع النظر والبحث وهو العقل الذي به يعرف المحاسن والمساوى، وبه يعلم فضله على سائر الحيوان وبه يُعرف ملك تدبير أمر الأنام. مع ما يأتي على ذهنه وفطنته تبدده وفناؤه ، بما نال من لذة الحياة، وركبت فيه شهوة البقاء وعظيم ألم أسباب الفناء لو اعترضه. فلا بد من البحث في دَرك ما يلائمه ويُبقى له ألذ الأشياء وأشهاها عنده؛ مما يأبى عقله المخاطرة بروحه في الامتحان بالأشياء دون تكلف ما يُطلعه على الضار منها فيتقيه، والنافع من ذلك فيجتلبه : إما بالبحث عن تعرف من يثق بخبره ويأمن خيانته فيما يدله عليه فيصدر [التدبير ] في كل ذلك عن رأيه ، أو أن يجهد في الامتحان بنفسه بالقليل الذي يُريه عاقبته، مما يؤمن عن مثله الهلاك لقلته؛ فيكون في الأمرين جميعا لزوم البحث. مع ما يدفعه جهله - بما جبل عليه من الشهوات وما يبعثه عليه نفسه من الملاذ - عما يصيبه من المكروه وما يَحلّ به من الألم إلى النظر في حال نفسه : إنه بم صار كذلك، أو هل كان كذلك في الأبد، أو من أي وجه صار كذلك ؟ لا يسلم عن بعض الخواطر التي تمنعه عن ترك النظر في أحوال نفسه ليعرف به مبادئه، وليعلم أنه كذلك بنفسه أو بمن له في نفسه تدبير ؟ مع ما لا بد من أن يعرف ما به صلاحه وفساده، وما عليه من النعم وعنه من الدفاع ؛ وفي كل ذلك اضطرار إلى النظر ولزوم الحجة. وبالله التوفيق. مع ما يعلم بيقين أن هذا الذي سوّل له ترك النظر هو خاطر الشيطان؛ إذ ذلك عمله ليصده عن ثمرة عقله ويُفزعه لأمانته التي لديها ينال الفرصة ويظفر بالبغية دليل ذلك أن استعمال العقل بالفكر بالأشياء ليعرف ما استتر منها من المبادئ والنهايات ثم فيما يدله على حدثها ومحدثها يشغله عن شهوات النفس، ليعلم أن ذلك هو صنيع الشيطان على أنه إذ لم يجز إهمال شيء من الجوارح عن المنافع التي جعلت فيها، ولا كفها عن أعمالها بتة، بل يجب
Issue [Defense of Knowledge through Rational Inquiry]. Some people said that refraining from inquiry (nazar) is safer because the inquirer (nāẓir) is not assured of attaining the truth. Then in it there is opening the door of proof against himself; for if he refrained, he would be safe from destruction (ʿaṭab), in that without it (nazar) he would not be traversed by the path he thinks or falsehood that would oblige the proof of God; for through thought and research there is a will for what compels him to knowledge that the truth is in what is disclosed to him, along with confusion of the divine inspiration (khāṭir al-Raḥmān) in the matter and warning from the whisper of the devil (khāṭir al-Shayṭān). And in refraining from inquiry and research there is safety from that, since nothing is disclosed to him that obliges discrimination, and nothing occurs to his mind that impels him to seek. And there is no power except with God. And whoever obliges inquiry and research says: in leaving it is his destruction inevitably; because the obligation of inquiry is not consequent upon a preceding inquiry, but upon that by which inquiry and research occur, namely the intellect (ʿaql) by which good and bad are known, and by which he knows his superiority over other animals, and by which the management of the affairs of mankind is known. Along with what comes to his mind and intelligence of his dissipation and annihilation, due to what he has attained of the pleasure of life, and the desire for survival (shahwat al-baqāʾ) is instilled in him, and the great pain of causes of annihilation if he encounters it. So there is no avoiding research into attaining what suits him and preserves for him the most delicious and desirable things to him; since his intellect refuses to risk his soul in testing things without taking pains to learn what is harmful so he may guard against it, and the beneficial so he may attract it: either by research to know one whose report he trusts and whose treachery he is safe from in what he indicates to him, so that [management] (al-tadbīr) in all that issues from his opinion, or that he exert himself in testing by himself with a little that shows him its consequence, from the like of which safety from destruction is assured due to its smallness; so in both matters there is the necessity of research. Along with what his ignorance drives him—due to the desires with which he is constitutionally endowed and the pleasures his soul urges upon him—from what befalls him of disliked things and what pain befalls him, to look into his own state: why did he become so, or was he so in eternity, or from what aspect did he become so? He is not safe from some thoughts that prevent him from leaving inquiry into his own states to know thereby his beginnings, and to know whether he is so by himself or by one who has management of himself? Along with the necessity of knowing what his well-being and corruption consist of, and what blessings are upon him and what defense is from him; and in all that is compulsion to inquiry and obligation of the proof. And with God is success. Along with what he knows with certainty that this which made him consider leaving inquiry is the whisper of the devil (khāṭir al-Shayṭān); for that is his work to turn him away from the fruit of his intellect and frighten him from his trust by which he obtains the opportunity and attains the goal. The evidence of that is that the use of the intellect by thinking about things to know what is hidden of beginnings and ends, then what indicates to him their temporal origination and their Originator, occupies him from the desires of the soul, so that he knows that that is the work of the devil, such that when it is not permissible to neglect any of the faculties from the benefits for which they are created, nor to restrain them from their actions at all, but must
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage is a defense of rational inquiry (nazar). It begins by presenting the objection of those who argue that refraining from inquiry is safer because the seeker might not attain the truth. The objectors claim that inquiry exposes one to the proof of God and confusion between divine inspiration and satanic whispers. Then the author refutes this by stating that whoever insists on inquiry says that refraining leads inevitably to destruction. The obligation of inquiry stems not from a previous act of inquiry but from the intellect itself, which distinguishes good from bad, gives humans superiority over animals, and enables management of affairs. Then he elaborates on the existential pressures that compel inquiry: the love of life, fear of death, desire for beneficial things, and avoidance of harm. This forces one to investigate through reliable reports or personal experimentation. Additionally, one must reflect on his own origin and nature, and understand what brings him benefit or harm. All of this necessitates inquiry. Finally, he asserts that the suggestion to abandon inquiry is from the devil (shayṭān), because using the intellect diverts from desires, so the devil aims to deprive one of the intellect's fruit. The passage ends with 'بل يجب' (but must), indicating the obligation of inquiry.

Key Terms
النظر
العقل
خاطر الرحمن
خاطر الشيطان
التمييز
الحجة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think refraining from theological inquiry is safer and more pious because it avoids error.
Correction: Maturidi argues that the intellect itself obliges inquiry; refraining actually leads to destruction because it neglects the very tool God gave us to discern truth.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, the obligation of nazar stems from:
A) Fear of hell.
B) A command of a prophet.
C) The intellect itself.
D) The desire for knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The obligation of rational inquiry is inherent to the intellect, not contingent upon any prior act of inquiry.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you deliberately avoided thinking about a difficult truth because it felt safer to remain in ignorance? How does Maturidi's argument confront that choice?
?
Your intellect is a gift from Allah that obliges you to seek truth. What truth about your faith or life are you currently avoiding that your intellect is nudging you toward? What one step will you take this week to act on that obligation?
?
If you claim to follow Islam but never engage in rational inquiry to understand your beliefs, are you fulfilling the intellectual duty that Maturidi says is inherent? What specific question about your faith will you investigate before the end of this month?

7 min
The Inescapable Pressures and the Devil's Deception
Maturidi details the existential necessity of inquiry and exposes the devil's whisper.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how existential pressures (like love of life and fear of death) compel rational inquiry, and how the devil's whisper to abandon inquiry is exposed.

Core Idea
Life itself forces you to inquire, and the devil only wants to strip you of your intellect.

After establishing the intellectual basis for nazar, Imam Maturidi now deepens the argument by pointing to everyday human experiences. Our love of life, fear of pain, and desire for beneficial things naturally drive us to seek knowledge. In this lesson, we will see how these existential forces make inquiry inevitable, and how the urge to abandon it is actually a satanic plot.

Guided Path
1
Understand the existential pressures
Read the passage focusing on the desire for survival (shahwat al-baqāʾ) and fear of annihilation. How do these force inquiry?
The intellect refuses to risk the soul carelessly without investigation.
2
Examine the two methods of inquiry
Identify the two ways to gain beneficial knowledge: trusting a reliable authority or personal experimentation. Both lead to the necessity of research.
3
Recognize the devil's strategy
Note how Maturidi identifies the suggestion to abandon nazar as a satanic whisper. What evidence does he give?
Using the intellect to contemplate origins distracts from desires, so the devil aims to prevent that.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة [ دفاع عن العلم بالنظر ] قال قوم ترك النظر أسلم لما لا يأمن الناظر بالظفر بالحق. ثم فيه فتح باب الحجة على نفسه؛ مما لو امتنع ليأ من العَطَب، من حيث لولا هو لم يتخلله السبيل الذي يظنه أو الباطل ليلزمه حجة الله؛ إذ بالفكر والبحث إرادة ما يضطر إلى العلم بأن الحق في ما انكشف له مع اشتباه خاطر الرحمن في الأمر والتحذير من خاطر الشيطان. وفي ترك النظر والبحث أمن ذلك، إذ لم ينكشف له ما يُلزمه التمييز ، ولا يخطر بذهنه ما يبعثه على الطلب. ولا قوة إلا بالله . و من ألزم النظر والبحث فيقول : في تركه عَطَبه لا محالة؛ لأن لزوم النظر ليس عقيب نظر تقدمه بل عقيب الذي به يقع النظر والبحث وهو العقل الذي به يعرف المحاسن والمساوى، وبه يعلم فضله على سائر الحيوان وبه يُعرف ملك تدبير أمر الأنام. مع ما يأتي على ذهنه وفطنته تبدده وفناؤه ، بما نال من لذة الحياة، وركبت فيه شهوة البقاء وعظيم ألم أسباب الفناء لو اعترضه. فلا بد من البحث في دَرك ما يلائمه ويُبقى له ألذ الأشياء وأشهاها عنده؛ مما يأبى عقله المخاطرة بروحه في الامتحان بالأشياء دون تكلف ما يُطلعه على الضار منها فيتقيه، والنافع من ذلك فيجتلبه : إما بالبحث عن تعرف من يثق بخبره ويأمن خيانته فيما يدله عليه فيصدر [التدبير ] في كل ذلك عن رأيه ، أو أن يجهد في الامتحان بنفسه بالقليل الذي يُريه عاقبته، مما يؤمن عن مثله الهلاك لقلته؛ فيكون في الأمرين جميعا لزوم البحث. مع ما يدفعه جهله - بما جبل عليه من الشهوات وما يبعثه عليه نفسه من الملاذ - عما يصيبه من المكروه وما يَحلّ به من الألم إلى النظر في حال نفسه : إنه بم صار كذلك، أو هل كان كذلك في الأبد، أو من أي وجه صار كذلك ؟ لا يسلم عن بعض الخواطر التي تمنعه عن ترك النظر في أحوال نفسه ليعرف به مبادئه، وليعلم أنه كذلك بنفسه أو بمن له في نفسه تدبير ؟ مع ما لا بد من أن يعرف ما به صلاحه وفساده، وما عليه من النعم وعنه من الدفاع ؛ وفي كل ذلك اضطرار إلى النظر ولزوم الحجة. وبالله التوفيق. مع ما يعلم بيقين أن هذا الذي سوّل له ترك النظر هو خاطر الشيطان؛ إذ ذلك عمله ليصده عن ثمرة عقله ويُفزعه لأمانته التي لديها ينال الفرصة ويظفر بالبغية دليل ذلك أن استعمال العقل بالفكر بالأشياء ليعرف ما استتر منها من المبادئ والنهايات ثم فيما يدله على حدثها ومحدثها يشغله عن شهوات النفس، ليعلم أن ذلك هو صنيع الشيطان على أنه إذ لم يجز إهمال شيء من الجوارح عن المنافع التي جعلت فيها، ولا كفها عن أعمالها بتة، بل يجب
Issue [Defense of Knowledge through Rational Inquiry]. Some people said that refraining from inquiry (nazar) is safer because the inquirer (nāẓir) is not assured of attaining the truth. Then in it there is opening the door of proof against himself; for if he refrained, he would be safe from destruction (ʿaṭab), in that without it (nazar) he would not be traversed by the path he thinks or falsehood that would oblige the proof of God; for through thought and research there is a will for what compels him to knowledge that the truth is in what is disclosed to him, along with confusion of the divine inspiration (khāṭir al-Raḥmān) in the matter and warning from the whisper of the devil (khāṭir al-Shayṭān). And in refraining from inquiry and research there is safety from that, since nothing is disclosed to him that obliges discrimination, and nothing occurs to his mind that impels him to seek. And there is no power except with God. And whoever obliges inquiry and research says: in leaving it is his destruction inevitably; because the obligation of inquiry is not consequent upon a preceding inquiry, but upon that by which inquiry and research occur, namely the intellect (ʿaql) by which good and bad are known, and by which he knows his superiority over other animals, and by which the management of the affairs of mankind is known. Along with what comes to his mind and intelligence of his dissipation and annihilation, due to what he has attained of the pleasure of life, and the desire for survival (shahwat al-baqāʾ) is instilled in him, and the great pain of causes of annihilation if he encounters it. So there is no avoiding research into attaining what suits him and preserves for him the most delicious and desirable things to him; since his intellect refuses to risk his soul in testing things without taking pains to learn what is harmful so he may guard against it, and the beneficial so he may attract it: either by research to know one whose report he trusts and whose treachery he is safe from in what he indicates to him, so that [management] (al-tadbīr) in all that issues from his opinion, or that he exert himself in testing by himself with a little that shows him its consequence, from the like of which safety from destruction is assured due to its smallness; so in both matters there is the necessity of research. Along with what his ignorance drives him—due to the desires with which he is constitutionally endowed and the pleasures his soul urges upon him—from what befalls him of disliked things and what pain befalls him, to look into his own state: why did he become so, or was he so in eternity, or from what aspect did he become so? He is not safe from some thoughts that prevent him from leaving inquiry into his own states to know thereby his beginnings, and to know whether he is so by himself or by one who has management of himself? Along with the necessity of knowing what his well-being and corruption consist of, and what blessings are upon him and what defense is from him; and in all that is compulsion to inquiry and obligation of the proof. And with God is success. Along with what he knows with certainty that this which made him consider leaving inquiry is the whisper of the devil (khāṭir al-Shayṭān); for that is his work to turn him away from the fruit of his intellect and frighten him from his trust by which he obtains the opportunity and attains the goal. The evidence of that is that the use of the intellect by thinking about things to know what is hidden of beginnings and ends, then what indicates to him their temporal origination and their Originator, occupies him from the desires of the soul, so that he knows that that is the work of the devil, such that when it is not permissible to neglect any of the faculties from the benefits for which they are created, nor to restrain them from their actions at all, but must
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage is a defense of rational inquiry (nazar). It begins by presenting the objection of those who argue that refraining from inquiry is safer because the seeker might not attain the truth. The objectors claim that inquiry exposes one to the proof of God and confusion between divine inspiration and satanic whispers. Then the author refutes this by stating that whoever insists on inquiry says that refraining leads inevitably to destruction. The obligation of inquiry stems not from a previous act of inquiry but from the intellect itself, which distinguishes good from bad, gives humans superiority over animals, and enables management of affairs. Then he elaborates on the existential pressures that compel inquiry: the love of life, fear of death, desire for beneficial things, and avoidance of harm. This forces one to investigate through reliable reports or personal experimentation. Additionally, one must reflect on his own origin and nature, and understand what brings him benefit or harm. All of this necessitates inquiry. Finally, he asserts that the suggestion to abandon inquiry is from the devil (shayṭān), because using the intellect diverts from desires, so the devil aims to deprive one of the intellect's fruit. The passage ends with 'بل يجب' (but must), indicating the obligation of inquiry.

Key Terms
شهوة البقاء
الاضطرار إلى النظر
خاطر الشيطان
التمييز
العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The urge to abandon theological inquiry is just personal laziness or pragmatism.
Correction: Maturidi identifies this urge as a satanic whisper designed to deprive one of the intellect's fruit.

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the evidence that the suggestion to leave nazar is from the devil?
A) It causes pain.
B) The intellect occupies itself from desires when used.
C) It leads to destruction.
D) It is forbidden by revelation.

Key Takeaway
The desire for survival and fear of harm make inquiry inescapable, and the devil's whisper to abandon it is exposed by the fact that intellect diverts from desires.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you truly sat with the pain of life—your mortality, your suffering—and let it drive you to ask deep questions about your existence and your Creator? Or do you immediately distract yourself, numbing the very questions that could lead you to Jannah?
?
The devil's whisper to abandon inquiry is subtle: 'You already know enough, just focus on practicing.' But Maturidi says that whisper is the devil's work. What specific thoughts or rationalizations do you entertain that keep you from deeper study of Allah's signs and your own purpose?
?
Which do you rely on more: the trustworthy guidance of the Quran and the scholars, or your own flawed experiments in life? Maturidi says both are valid paths to inquiry, but neither is an excuse to stop. If you are honest, are you actively seeking to increase your knowledge of your Lord, or have you succumbed to the whisper that 'what you have is enough'?

The Excuse of Ignorance Is Not Valid Before God
Maturidi refutes the objection that ignorance is a valid excuse before God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that ignorance of God's commands is not a valid excuse, because God has provided sufficient evidence and means of knowledge.

Core Idea
Ignorance is no excuse when God has given you evidence and stirred your mind.

This passage presents a powerful objection: if a human master accepts a servant's excuse of ignorance, why wouldn't God? Maturidi's answer reveals a key principle: God has not left us without guidance. He has placed evidence, thoughts, and lessons for us. Therefore, failure to know is our own neglect.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: an objection is raised, then answered.
The objection uses an analogy from human interactions.
2
Identify the analogy and its limitation
Find the analogy of the master and servant. Note why Maturidi says it only works among humans: because the evidence is absent there.
3
Understand God's provisions
Maturidi lists three things God has given: evidence, stirring thoughts, and lessons. Reflect on how these make ignorance one's own fault.
The key term 'ترك النظر' (abandonment of reflection) is central.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال : إذ لا عذر في الشاهد للعبد أقبل من قوله لسيده: «لم أعلم أن فعلي يُسخطك فأتركه، ولو علمت ذلك لانزجرت مما فعلت»، لم لا كان ذلك في حكمة الله مقبولاً؟ قيل : ذلك إنما حسن بيننا لارتفاع ما به يُعرف الأمر من الدليل عليه. وأما الله سبحانه فقد جعل لعبده على الأمر بما أمره به دليلاً، وحرّك ذهنه بالخواطر ، ونبهه بصنوف العبر؛ فإنما أتى من قِبَل تركه النظر، وذلك فعله فيصير بما هو معتذر مَحْجُوجا؛ إذ بفعله أعرض عن ذلك. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
If he says: Since in the seen world there is no excuse for a servant when he says to his master, 'I did not know that my action displeases you, so I would leave it; had I known that, I would have refrained from what I did,' why is that not accepted in God's wisdom? He said: That is only considered good among us because the evidence by which the command is known is absent. But God Almighty has placed for His servant evidence for the command He has commanded him, and stirred his mind with thoughts, and alerted him with various lessons. So the failure comes only from his abandonment of reflection, and that is his own action. Thus he becomes, while excusing himself, confuted; because by his own action he turned away from that. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage is another objection-and-answer pair. The objector raises a scenario from this world: if a servant tells his master 'I did not know that my action displeases you, so had I known I would have refrained,' such an excuse is accepted among humans. So why would not God accept a similar excuse? Maturidi answers that in human interactions, the excuse is accepted only because there is no clear evidence that the action was wrong. But God has provided clear evidence for His commands, stirred the mind through thoughts (خواطر), and alerted through various signs (عبر). Therefore, the person's failure is due to abandoning reflection (ترك النظر), which is his own fault. Thus, when he offers ignorance as an excuse, he is confuted (محجوج) because his own turning away caused the ignorance.

Key Terms
الشاهد
الدليل
الخواطر
العبر
ترك النظر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That ignorance of God's commands is a valid excuse because it is accepted among humans.
Correction: Maturidi shows the human analogy fails because God has provided clear evidence, so ignorance is due to one's own failure to reflect.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi argue that the excuse of ignorance is not accepted by God?
A) Because God is more just than human masters.
B) Because God has provided evidence and stirred the mind, so ignorance is due to one's own neglect.
C) Because ignorance is never accepted in any legal system.
D) Because the person should have asked a scholar.

Key Takeaway
Ignorance is not an excuse before God because He has provided ample evidence and means of knowledge; failure to know stems from one's own abandonment of reflection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever excused yourself from seeking knowledge about a religious matter, thinking, 'I didn't know, so it's not my fault'? How does this passage confront that attitude right now?
?
God has placed evidence and stirred your mind through thoughts and lessons. When you ignore a prompting to learn or reflect, what does that reveal about your accountability before Him?
?
The passage says your own turning away makes you 'confuted.' In what area of your life today are you turning away from clear evidence, and what will you do to change that before you stand before God?

The Impossibility of Infinite Motion
Maturidi argues that motion must have a beginning because it ends.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's rational argument that motion must have a beginning because every observed motion ends.

Core Idea
Every motion that ends must have a beginning; infinite motion cannot end.

Maturidi presents a powerful rational argument against the possibility of an infinite series of motions. He bases his reasoning on the simple observation that any motion we observe terminates. Because termination is an end, what is terminated cannot be preceded by an infinite series, as an infinite series would have no end. Thus, each motion implies a starting point.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the literal translation to understand the surface meaning.
Notice the compressed style; Maturidi assumes prior refutation.
2
Identify the premises
Find two key premises: (1) every motion we observe passes away (ends), (2) what ends is finite. Maturidi states that no motion is in doubt except that it is an end of preceding motions.
The word 'nihaayah' (end) is crucial.
3
Understand why infinite cannot terminate
Maturidi argues that if a motion ends, its termination is the end of the series. An infinite series would have no end to terminate, so it cannot be that motion has an infinite past.
This is a version of the argument from the impossibility of an actual infinite.
4
Appreciate the conclusion
Since motion ends, it must have a beginning. Maturidi writes, 'thus the beginning is established for it.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ رحمه الله : أما القول بحركات لا نهاية لها فقد بينا فيما تقدم فسادها. ما لا يشك في حركة انقضت إلا أنها نهاية ما تقدم من الحركات، حتى لا يكون شيء مما تقدم هذه. وإذا ثبت نهاية الفناء لها والانقضاء لم يجز أن يتناهي الانقضاء لما لا يتناهي له الابتداء، ثبت لذلك الابتداء. وبعد، فإنا رأينا الجواهر كلها في رأى العين متفاوتة الحدود. لا يحتمل أن تكون هي كذلك على غير أن يكون كذلك إلا عن أقل متفاوت يظهر في أكثره ذلك؛ فثبت أن كان.
The Shaykh, may God have mercy on him, said: As for the statement about motions (ḥarakāt) that have no end, we have already shown its corruption earlier. There is no motion that has passed away that is doubted except that it is an end of the motions that preceded it, so that nothing of what preceded this one remains. And when the end of its passing away and termination is established, it is not permissible that the termination be ending for what has no beginning; thus the beginning is established for it. Moreover, we see that all substances (jawāhir), in the eye's view, have differing boundaries. It is not plausible that they are thus without being so except from a smallest differing [unit] that appears in its larger [aggregate]; so it was established that it was.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage presents Maturidi's argument against infinite motion and in favor of a beginning. He first states that the claim of infinite motion has already been refuted elsewhere. He then argues that any motion we observe passes away, and because it ends, it must have had a beginning. If a motion ends, the series of motions up to that point cannot be infinite because an infinite series would have no end. Thus, every finite motion implies a starting point. The second argument is from observation of substances: we see that all substances have differing boundaries (limits). This cannot be accidental; there must be a smallest unit of difference that becomes apparent in larger aggregates. This points to a finite structure and thus a created origin. The phrase 'so it was established that it was' indicates the proof of a beginning.

Key Terms
حركات (ḥarakāt)
نهاية (nihāyah)
ابتداء (ibtidā')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that Maturidi denies the existence of motion or that motion is impossible.
Correction: Maturidi only denies an infinite regress of motion, not motion itself. He affirms that motion exists and ends, but argues that an infinite chain of motions is impossible, so motion must have a starting point.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi's argument, why cannot motion have an infinite past?
A) Because all motion ends, and what ends cannot be preceded by an infinite series.
B) Because motion is impossible.
C) Because God directly creates all motion without any sequence.
D) Because motion is an accident.

Key Takeaway
Every motion that ends must have a beginning; therefore the universe's motion is not eternal but created.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the universe itself had a beginning, then your life, too, has a definite start and end. Are you living as if your days are just an endless routine, or do you recognize that every moment is a finite gift from the Creator? What will you do today to live with that awareness?
?
Maturidi proves that every ended motion implies a beginning. Think of a significant change in your life that has ended—a relationship, a stage, a season. Does that ending remind you that it had a purpose and a beginning? Are you grateful for what was, or are you stuck in a cycle of regret, treating it as if it were infinite?
?
The argument shatters the notion of eternity for the world. What does that mean for your own pursuits? If everything in this world will cease, are you investing in what will outlast it—your relationship with the Eternal? Or are you chasing things that, like a motion that ends, will leave you empty when they are gone?

Proof of a Beginning from the Limits of Substances
Maturidi uses the differing limits of substances to argue for their beginning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument from the differing boundaries of substances to prove that they have a beginning.

Core Idea
The observed differences in the limits of substances imply a smallest unit, showing that substances are created.

Maturidi presents a second argument based on our observation of substances (jawāhir). We see that all substances have different boundaries—they vary in size and shape. This variance cannot be random; it must originate from a smallest difference that accumulates. This points to a finite, created structure, implying that substances have a beginning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage carefully, then the literal translation. Notice that the passage begins with 'Moreover', indicating it is a second argument.
2
Observe the key point: substances differ in boundaries
Maturidi states that we see all substances (jawāhir) with differing boundaries (ḥudūd) in our visual perception.
The term 'jawāhir' here refers to individual atoms or discrete entities.
3
Understand the reasoning: differences require a foundation
He argues that it is not plausible for these differences to exist without being derived from a smallest differing unit. That smallest unit becomes apparent in the larger aggregate.
This is an argument from composition: large differences come from small ones.
4
Conclude: this indicates a beginning
Maturidi concludes 'so it was established that it was', meaning that the existence of substances has a beginning because their composition in finite parts points to being created.
The phrase 'thabita an kāna' is concise: 'it was established that it was', i.e., existence from nothing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ رحمه الله : أما القول بحركات لا نهاية لها فقد بينا فيما تقدم فسادها. ما لا يشك في حركة انقضت إلا أنها نهاية ما تقدم من الحركات، حتى لا يكون شيء مما تقدم هذه. وإذا ثبت نهاية الفناء لها والانقضاء لم يجز أن يتناهي الانقضاء لما لا يتناهي له الابتداء، ثبت لذلك الابتداء. وبعد، فإنا رأينا الجواهر كلها في رأى العين متفاوتة الحدود. لا يحتمل أن تكون هي كذلك على غير أن يكون كذلك إلا عن أقل متفاوت يظهر في أكثره ذلك؛ فثبت أن كان.
The Shaykh, may God have mercy on him, said: As for the statement about motions (ḥarakāt) that have no end, we have already shown its corruption earlier. There is no motion that has passed away that is doubted except that it is an end of the motions that preceded it, so that nothing of what preceded this one remains. And when the end of its passing away and termination is established, it is not permissible that the termination be ending for what has no beginning; thus the beginning is established for it. Moreover, we see that all substances (jawāhir), in the eye's view, have differing boundaries. It is not plausible that they are thus without being so except from a smallest differing [unit] that appears in its larger [aggregate]; so it was established that it was.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage presents Maturidi's argument against infinite motion and in favor of a beginning. He first states that the claim of infinite motion has already been refuted elsewhere. He then argues that any motion we observe passes away, and because it ends, it must have had a beginning. If a motion ends, the series of motions up to that point cannot be infinite because an infinite series would have no end. Thus, every finite motion implies a starting point. The second argument is from observation of substances: we see that all substances have differing boundaries (limits). This cannot be accidental; there must be a smallest unit of difference that becomes apparent in larger aggregates. This points to a finite structure and thus a created origin. The phrase 'so it was established that it was' indicates the proof of a beginning.

Key Terms
جواهر (jawāhir)
حدود (ḥudūd)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the differing boundaries of substances are eternal and inherent, without requiring a cause.
Correction: Maturidi argues that these differences must come from a smallest unit of difference, implying finite composition and thus a created origin.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi conclude from the observation that substances have differing boundaries?
A) That they must have a smallest unit of difference, indicating a beginning.
B) That they are infinite and uncreated.
C) That they are all identical at a fundamental level.
D) That boundaries are illusions.

Key Takeaway
The differing limits of substances point to a finite structure and thus a beginning of all created things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every difference in the world—every shape, size, and color—points to a smallest unit that indicates a beginning, then what does your own unique existence say about your purpose? Are you living as if you were created intentionally or as if you are accidental?
?
The argument proves that creation had a beginning. How does this truth reshape your view of your own life's starting point? Do you live with the awareness that your existence is not random but willed by a Creator, and how does that affect your daily choices?
?
Maturidi says it is impossible for the diversity to be without cause. Consider your own talents, circumstances, and struggles—are you treating them as random or as signs from the Creator? What would change if you truly believed that every detail has a purpose?

The Principle of Revelation and Inference in Kalam
Maturidi states the principle of relying on revelation or inference from the present to the absent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Maturidi's foundational methodological principle for acquiring knowledge about unseen realities: either through authentic revelation (sam‛) or through sound inference (istidlāl) from the observed world.

Core Idea
True knowledge of the unseen comes from revelation or inference from the seen.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi lays down the epistemological foundation for his theological method. He asserts that when dealing with claims about stars and natural phenomena, one must rely either on revelation, which is validated by proofs of truth, or on rational inference that moves from the observable present to the unobservable absent. This principle frames the entire discussion of causality and divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Imam Maturidi uses a very concise style here.
2
Identify the two sources of knowledge
The passage presents two sources: revelation (sam‛) and inference (istidlāl). Distinguish between them and note how Maturidi prioritizes revelation without dismissing inference.
3
Understand the logic of inference
Maturidi explains that if one uses inference from the present to the absent, the cause must have the same attribute as the effect. Ask yourself why this is necessary and how it refutes the opponents' claim.
This step connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ رحمه الله : والأصل في هذا عندنا وفيما ذكر من النجوم والطبائع أن لا يخلو من أن يرجع في ذلك إلى سمع - وفيها سماع أهل التوحيد أثبت، لما معهم براهين الصدق - أو يستدل بالحاضر الموجود على الغائب. فإن كان هذا طريقه فيجب - إذ الموجود على حال وبالوجود اعتباره - أن يكون الذي به وجد بهذه الصفة؛ فيبطل قولهم في حدوث
The Shaykh, may Allah have mercy on him, said: The principle in this according to us and regarding what was mentioned of the stars and natures is that one cannot refrain from referring in that to revelation – and in it the hearing of the people of tawhid is more firmly established, for they have proofs of truth – or inferring from the present existent to the absent. If this is his method, then it necessarily follows – since the existent is in a state and by existence its consideration – that that by which it exists is of this attribute; so their statement regarding the occurrence is invalid
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage presents the author's (al-Shaykh, i.e., Imam Maturidi) methodological principle for dealing with claims about the stars and natural phenomena. He states that one must either rely on revelation (sam‛), which is stronger because the people of tawhid possess proofs of truth, or use inference from the observable (al-hadir al-mawjud) to the unobservable (al-gha'ib). If one chooses the latter method, then since the observable exists in a particular state, the cause of that existence must also possess the same attribute. This principle, when applied, refutes the opponents' claim about the occurrence of something (حدوث) because the observable evidence does not support their causal theory. The sentence is incomplete but conveys the core argument.

Key Terms
سمع
حاضر
غائب
وجود
براهين الصدق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author rejects inference altogether and only accepts revelation.
Correction: Maturidi allows inference (istidlāl) as a valid source, but he prioritizes revelation because it is accompanied by proofs of truth.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the first source of knowledge about stars and natures?
A) Sensory experience
B) Revelation (sam‛)
C) Rational speculation
D) Consensus

Key Takeaway
Imam Maturidi's epistemological principle establishes that theological knowledge must be grounded either in revelation verified by proofs or in sound inference from the created world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you seek to understand matters of the unseen (like the nature of Allah or the soul), do you rely more on revelation or on your own reasoning? In what ways might this imbalance affect the certainty of your faith?
?
The passage says the people of tawhid have proofs of truth that strengthen their acceptance of revelation. What proofs of truth do you personally have that confirm the Quran and Sunnah in your heart? Are you actively seeking such proofs, or are you content with blind imitation?
?
Imagine you are faced with a claim about science or nature that contradicts a clear teaching of Islam. Would you immediately reject the scientific claim, attempt to reconcile, or reconsider your understanding of the religion? How does this passage's principle guide your approach?

The Three States of Sensory Perception
Maturidi classifies sensory perception into three states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three states of sensory perception and how Maturidi grounds them in sensory experience.

Core Idea
Perception varies by conditions: turning, fixing with barriers removed, or mixture.

Maturidi begins by establishing a foundational principle: a human has multiple directions, each facing a corresponding direction of the perceived object. He then classifies sensory perception into three states, showing that it is not absolute but varies by conditions. This passage grounds epistemology in sensory experience, preparing for discussions of reliable knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the logical flow: a principle, then three states, then a concluding remark about sensory certainty.
2
Identify the three states
Maturidi lists three states: (1) turning the direction away, (2) fixing the direction with all barriers removed, (3) a mixture. Identify how each state affects perception.
The first state yields no perception, the second yields full perception, the third yields varying degrees.
3
Reflect on the principle
The principle is that each direction perceives only its corresponding direction. An affliction or barrier disrupts this. Consider how this applies to everyday sensory experience.
4
Understand the sensory grounding
Maturidi emphasizes that all this is known by the senses and true. There is no disagreement in the science of sense. Reflect on why this foundation is important for theology.
This establishes that sensory knowledge is contingent on conditions, not absolute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ رحمه الله : } والأصل في هذا أن الإنسان إذا اشتمل على حدود وجهات، فكل جهة منه تقابل جهة من المدرك لا يدرك بتلك الجهة غير الجهة التي قابلته. فإذا اعترضت الآفة في جهته التي بها يدرك مقابلها أو غشى مقابلها شيء ستره فيذهب مقدار من الجهة ،ومقابلها فيكون كالإدراك بغير الجهة التي لذلك النوع من الإدراك. فتكون الأحوال ثلاثة : تقليب الجهة لا يدرك به شيئًا ألبتة، وتقريرها مع ارتفاع السواتر كلها فيدرك به حقيقة المدرك، أو الاختلاط؛ فعلى تفاوت ذلك يتفاوت الدرك. وكل ذلك حق في الحس معلوم بالحس؛ فلم يرد في علم الحس اختلاف ألبتة في الحقيقة. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
The shaykh (may God have mercy on him) said: The principle in this is that when a human encompasses boundaries and directions, each direction of him faces a direction of the perceived object; he does not perceive by that direction other than the direction that faced him. So when an affliction occurs in his direction by which he perceives its opposite, or something covers its opposite and veils it, then a portion of the direction and its opposite goes away, and it becomes like perception without the direction proper to that type of perception. So the states are three: turning the direction—one does not perceive anything at all; fixing it with all barriers removed—one perceives the reality of the perceived object; or mixture. According to the variation of that, perception varies. And all of that is true in sense, known by sense; so there has not occurred in the science of sense any difference at all concerning the reality. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies sensory perception. Maturidi begins by stating the principle: a human has multiple directions (جهات), each facing a corresponding direction of the object. Perception only occurs for the direction that faces the object. If an affliction (آفة) affects that direction or something covers the object, perception is diminished. Then he enumerates three states: (1) turning the direction away results in no perception; (2) fixing the direction with all barriers removed results in full perception of reality; (3) a mixture of these. Perception varies accordingly. He emphasizes that all this is known by the senses and is true; there is no disagreement in the science of sense. This establishes the foundation for distinguishing reliable perception from unreliable due to afflictions.

Key Terms
حدود (boundaries)
جهات (directions)
مدرك (perceived object)
آفة (affliction)
سواتر (barriers)
درك (perception)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sensory perception is absolute and uniform; it does not vary by conditions.
Correction: Maturidi demonstrates that perception varies by direction, afflictions, and barriers. The three states show that perception can be absent, full, or mixed depending on conditions.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, how many states of sensory perception are there?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) Four

Key Takeaway
Sensory perception is not uniform; it depends on conditions such as direction, afflictions, and barriers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a truth you have been avoiding. Is it possible that you are 'turning your direction' away from it? What would it take to 'fix your direction with all barriers removed' and face it fully?
?
When you fail to perceive a lesson in an event, do you tend to blame the event itself rather than examine the condition of your own heart? What 'affliction' or 'barrier' might be distorting your perception?
?
This lesson shows that perception is not absolute but conditional. In your relationship with the Qur'an, are you expecting uniform clarity or are you willing to adjust your 'direction' to receive what it offers? What barriers do you need to lift?

The Opponent's Distinction and Its Weakness
Maturidi presents the opponent's claim and answers about impossible dreams.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's argument that wakeful knowledge is acquired because in dreams one sees impossible things, and Maturidi's counter that the dreamer does not genuinely believe those impossibilities.

Core Idea
Dreaming the impossible does not prove wakeful knowledge is acquired.

In this passage, Maturidi presents an opponent's attempt to distinguish dream from wakefulness by noting that in dreams one sees things that are intellectually impossible, like being dead while knowing. The opponent claims this shows wakeful knowledge is acquired. Maturidi responds by pointing out that the dreamer does not genuinely believe the impossibility—he sees it but does not hold it as real.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the first part of the Arabic passage where the opponent raises his distinction and Maturidi gives the immediate answer about belief.
Focus on the exchange: the opponent says impossible visions distinguish dream, Maturidi says the dreamer does not believe them.
2
Understand the opponent's claim
The opponent claims that knowledge of wakefulness's soundness is acquired, not necessary. His proof: in dreams we see impossibilities.
3
Examine Maturidi's answer
Maturidi explains that when a person sees himself dead in a dream, he does not actually believe he is dead—the impossibility is not genuinely entertained.
4
Consider the counter about true dreams
The opponent acknowledges that some dreams are true (by angel, etc.), which might seem to weaken the distinction. Maturidi leaves this for the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم عارض نفسته بما يرى النائم فيخرج على [ضد] ما يرى، فلعل أمر اليقظان على هذا، أو ما يعلم ذا من ذا . فزعم أن الذي يفرق بين الأمرين أنه يرى ما لا يصح في العقل في حال النوم، نحو أن يرى نفسه ميتا - والميت لا يعلم - أو يرى رأسه مُلقى في حجره ومثله لا يحتمل رؤية اليقظان.

فإن قيل : كيف يتوهم النائم المحال، وهو لا يثبت في الوهم؟ قيل : عند ما يرى نفسه في المنام لا يعتقدها حية ميتة ، وذلك هو المحال ؛ وكذلك إذا رأى رأسه ملقى لا يتوهمه في مكانين. وزعم أن العلم بصحة ما في اليقظة وفساد ما في النوم اكتساب، دليله ما ذكرت. قال: وقد يرى في المنام ما يصح؛ ذلك إنها [ يكون] بمَلَك يُريه أو بما ذلك في الإصحاء ، أو بعض ذلك .

{ قال الفقيه رحمه الله : } والأصل في هذا ما في الأول؛ إن النائم ذو آفة يعرفها بما يعلم به يقظته، وذلك حق الحس. إنه يرى في النوم مضطرًا، وفي اليقظة لا . وكذلك يبقى [فيه] ألم ما يُضرب في حال اليقظة، ويعرف لذة ما به يغتذى . وليس بيننا وبين هؤلاء في هذه الأحوال مسألة، إنما بيننا إلزام حق اليقظة وتحقيقه بضرورة بما ذكرنا . ثم تغيّر ذلك إنما ذلك للآفات التي تعترض. وجملته أن الطبيعة أو النجوم أو الأغذية لا يُحتمل أن تولّد ذلك، ولا فيها ما يوجب ذلك، وأن لكل شيء من ذلك مضرة ومنفعة . وما به الغلبة والاعتدال فلا يحتمل وجود مثله بالطبع ولا بالنجم ، من حيث خروج ذلك على ما فيه من الحكمة والإتقان؛ وما يوجبه الطبع لا يحتمل ذلك. وقد مربيان ذلك . والله الموفق.
Then he contradicted himself with what the sleeper sees, which comes out contrary to what he sees. Perhaps the matter of the wakeful person is like this, or he does not know which is which. He claimed that what distinguishes between the two matters is that he sees in sleep what is not valid in the intellect, like seeing himself dead—while the dead does not know—or seeing his head thrown in his lap, and the like of that the wakeful person cannot see. If it is said: How does the sleeper imagine the impossible, while it does not become established in imagination? He said: When he sees himself in the dream, he does not believe it is alive or dead, and that is the impossible; likewise, if he sees his head thrown, he does not imagine it in two places. And he claimed that knowledge of the soundness of wakefulness and the corruption of sleep is acquired, and its proof is what I mentioned. He said: And sometimes one sees in a dream what is sound; that is either by an angel showing him, or by something in wakefulness, or some of that. The faqih (may God have mercy on him) said: The principle in this is as in the first [argument]; the sleeper is one with an affliction that he knows by what he knows of his wakefulness, and that is the truth of sense. He sees in sleep involuntarily, and in wakefulness he does not. Likewise, the pain of a blow remains in him in the state of wakefulness, and he knows the pleasure of what he eats. There is no issue between us and them concerning these states; rather, between us is the affirmation of the truth of wakefulness and its verification by necessity based on what we have mentioned. Then the change of that is only due to the afflictions that occur. In sum: nature, or stars, or foods cannot generate that, nor do they contain what necessitates that; and everything in that has harm and benefit. What has dominance and balance cannot exist by nature or by star, due to its exceeding what is in it of wisdom and precision; and what nature necessitates cannot do that. And we have already explained this. And God is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage contains an objection-and-answer followed by a refutation. The opponent tries to distinguish dream from wakefulness by noting that in dreams one sees impossible things (e.g., being dead while knowing). He answers that the dreamer does not genuinely believe those impossibilities. He claims that knowledge of wakefulness's soundness is acquired. Maturidi responds by returning to the first principle: the sleeper has an affliction (آفة) known by comparison to wakeful sensory experience. The sleeper sees involuntarily, while in wakefulness he does not; physical sensations like pain remain in wakefulness but are absent in sleep. There is no real dispute; the truth of wakefulness is known by necessity (ضرورة). Changes in perception are due to afflictions. Maturidi then generalizes: nature, stars, and food cannot generate such distinctions because they lack the wisdom and precision to produce the observed balance and dominance. This reaffirms that sensory knowledge of wakefulness is immediate and necessary, not acquired through reasoning.

Key Terms
نائم (sleeper)
يقظان (wakeful)
محال (impossible)
وهم (imagination)
اكتساب (acquired)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Wakeful knowledge is acquired through reasoning because in dreams we see impossible things.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the dreamer does not actually believe the impossible, so the distinction does not prove acquisition.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi's answer, why does the dreamer's seeing an impossibility not distinguish dream from wakefulness?
A) Because the dreamer is not conscious.
B) Because the dreamer does not genuinely believe the impossibility.
C) Because the impossibility is only in the intellect.
D) Because the dreamer is always wrong.

Key Takeaway
Seeing impossibilities in dreams does not make wakeful knowledge acquired; the dreamer does not actually believe those impossibilities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you wake from a vivid dream, do you ever doubt whether you are truly awake? How does Maturidi's point that the dreamer does not believe the impossible help you regain certainty in your wakeful knowledge? Will you now dismiss such doubts more firmly?
?
The opponent argued that impossible dreams prove wakeful knowledge is weak. Maturidi shows the error. Has a dream ever led you to question a fundamental truth about yourself (e.g., your identity or beliefs)? How does this lesson strengthen your trust in your intellect—and what action will you take to rely on that trust today?

Method of Learning

Wakefulness Known by Sensory Necessity
Maturidi affirms sensory necessity and refutes natural causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's affirmation that the truth of wakefulness is known by sensory necessity, not acquired reasoning, and that natural causes cannot produce this distinction.

Core Idea
The truth of wakefulness is known by sensory necessity, not by acquired proof.

After refuting the opponent's distinction, Maturidi reaffirms his core principle: the sleeper has an affliction (آفة) that is recognized only by comparison to wakeful experience. The truth of wakefulness is known by necessity through the senses, not by acquired reasoning. He further argues that nature, stars, and food cannot generate such a precise distinction because they lack wisdom and precision.

Guided Path
1
Read Maturidi's main argument
Read from 'قال الفقيه' to the end. Understand the principle about the sleeper's affliction.
This is the core of Maturidi's position.
2
Grasp the necessity argument
Maturidi says the truth of wakefulness is verified by necessity (ضرورة), not by acquired reasoning. The sleeper sees involuntarily, while wakeful experience is voluntary and consistent.
3
Examine the rejection of natural causes
Maturidi argues that nature, stars, and food cannot generate the distinction between dream and wakefulness, because they lack the wisdom and precision to produce such a consistent and balanced state.
4
Connect to the overall methodology
This lesson shows Maturidi's reliance on immediate sense experience as a foundation for knowledge, rejecting both rationalist and naturalist explanations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم عارض نفسته بما يرى النائم فيخرج على [ضد] ما يرى، فلعل أمر اليقظان على هذا، أو ما يعلم ذا من ذا . فزعم أن الذي يفرق بين الأمرين أنه يرى ما لا يصح في العقل في حال النوم، نحو أن يرى نفسه ميتا - والميت لا يعلم - أو يرى رأسه مُلقى في حجره ومثله لا يحتمل رؤية اليقظان.

فإن قيل : كيف يتوهم النائم المحال، وهو لا يثبت في الوهم؟ قيل : عند ما يرى نفسه في المنام لا يعتقدها حية ميتة ، وذلك هو المحال ؛ وكذلك إذا رأى رأسه ملقى لا يتوهمه في مكانين. وزعم أن العلم بصحة ما في اليقظة وفساد ما في النوم اكتساب، دليله ما ذكرت. قال: وقد يرى في المنام ما يصح؛ ذلك إنها [ يكون] بمَلَك يُريه أو بما ذلك في الإصحاء ، أو بعض ذلك .

{ قال الفقيه رحمه الله : } والأصل في هذا ما في الأول؛ إن النائم ذو آفة يعرفها بما يعلم به يقظته، وذلك حق الحس. إنه يرى في النوم مضطرًا، وفي اليقظة لا . وكذلك يبقى [فيه] ألم ما يُضرب في حال اليقظة، ويعرف لذة ما به يغتذى . وليس بيننا وبين هؤلاء في هذه الأحوال مسألة، إنما بيننا إلزام حق اليقظة وتحقيقه بضرورة بما ذكرنا . ثم تغيّر ذلك إنما ذلك للآفات التي تعترض. وجملته أن الطبيعة أو النجوم أو الأغذية لا يُحتمل أن تولّد ذلك، ولا فيها ما يوجب ذلك، وأن لكل شيء من ذلك مضرة ومنفعة . وما به الغلبة والاعتدال فلا يحتمل وجود مثله بالطبع ولا بالنجم ، من حيث خروج ذلك على ما فيه من الحكمة والإتقان؛ وما يوجبه الطبع لا يحتمل ذلك. وقد مربيان ذلك . والله الموفق.
Then he contradicted himself with what the sleeper sees, which comes out contrary to what he sees. Perhaps the matter of the wakeful person is like this, or he does not know which is which. He claimed that what distinguishes between the two matters is that he sees in sleep what is not valid in the intellect, like seeing himself dead—while the dead does not know—or seeing his head thrown in his lap, and the like of that the wakeful person cannot see. If it is said: How does the sleeper imagine the impossible, while it does not become established in imagination? He said: When he sees himself in the dream, he does not believe it is alive or dead, and that is the impossible; likewise, if he sees his head thrown, he does not imagine it in two places. And he claimed that knowledge of the soundness of wakefulness and the corruption of sleep is acquired, and its proof is what I mentioned. He said: And sometimes one sees in a dream what is sound; that is either by an angel showing him, or by something in wakefulness, or some of that. The faqih (may God have mercy on him) said: The principle in this is as in the first [argument]; the sleeper is one with an affliction that he knows by what he knows of his wakefulness, and that is the truth of sense. He sees in sleep involuntarily, and in wakefulness he does not. Likewise, the pain of a blow remains in him in the state of wakefulness, and he knows the pleasure of what he eats. There is no issue between us and them concerning these states; rather, between us is the affirmation of the truth of wakefulness and its verification by necessity based on what we have mentioned. Commentary
This passage contains an objection-and-answer followed by a refutation. The opponent tries to distinguish dream from wakefulness by noting that in dreams one sees impossible things (e.g., being dead while knowing). He answers that the dreamer does not genuinely believe those impossibilities. He claims that knowledge of wakefulness's soundness is acquired. Maturidi then generalizes: nature, stars, and food cannot generate such distinctions because they lack the wisdom and precision to produce the observed balance and dominance. This reaffirms that sensory knowledge of wakefulness is immediate and necessary, not acquired through reasoning.

Key Terms
آفة (affliction)
ضرورة (necessity)
طبيعة (nature)
نجم (star)
حكمة (wisdom)
إتقان (precision)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Natural phenomena like stars or food cause the distinction between dream and wakefulness.
Correction: Maturidi argues that nature lacks the wisdom and precision to produce such a consistent distinction.

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi say the truth of wakefulness is known?
A) By acquired proof
B) By sensory necessity
C) By consensus
D) By revelation

Key Takeaway
The truth of wakefulness is known by immediate sensory necessity, not by acquired reasoning, and cannot be explained by imperfect natural causes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you trust your senses as certain, and when do you doubt them? Based on this lesson, what would Maturidi say about sensory knowledge being a foundation for truth in your daily life?
?
Think of a time when an 'affliction' (like fatigue or emotion) clouded your perception. Did you seek to correct it by returning to a baseline state of wakefulness? How does this passage challenge you to identify and remove obstacles to clear perception of truth?
?
This passage argues that nature and stars cannot produce precision—only wisdom can. Where in your life have you attributed good order to chance or nature instead of recognizing divine wisdom? What would change if you saw God's hand in every consistent, balanced experience?

Method of Learning
intermediate
5 min
Maturidi's Counter-Argument: Combination over Separation
Maturidi counters dualists' claim about things tending to separate, offering an alternative based on combination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's critique of the dualists' argument from evidence and his principle that claims about the unseen must be grounded in the witnessed world.

Core Idea
Maturidi shows that the dualists' conclusion is arbitrary and their method violates the witness principle.

In this passage, Maturidi addresses a dualist argument that things tend to separate based on unseen evidence. He counters by suggesting that combination is equally plausible and more coherent logically. He then exposes a deeper methodological flaw: the dualists' reasoning would allow absurd consequences, undermining their whole approach.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage once
Read the Arabic and translation to get the flow of argument. Notice the structure: challenge, claim, counter, principle.
2
Identify the dualists' claim
Find where the dualists say they know by evidence that things separate and return to their origin.
This is after 'فإن قالوا'
3
Understand Maturidi's alternative
Maturidi says 'بل شأنهم الاجتماع' and gives reasons. Why does he think combination is more plausible?
He uses the idea that combination brings strength.
4
Grasp the epistemological principle
The last part 'ولو جاز...' introduces a key principle. What is it? Why does it undermine the dualists?
It shows that without a witness, absurd claims would be possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال: لم تُعاينوا شيئًا غير ممتزج من خير وشر، ولم يرد لكم خبر يحتمل الصدق فإن قالوا : علمنا بالأدلة أن شأن الأشياء التفرق، وكل شيء يرجع إلى أصل جوهره قال الشيخ رحمه الله : يقال : بل شأنهم الاجتماع، فمنتهى كل على أصل جوهره ، وإذا كان وقع هذا قد اجتمع، فاجعل ذلك أبداً كذلك. ويقال: إذ التفرق تبدد، والاجتماع تأكد وقوة لم لا كان شأنهم الاجتماع. ولا قوة إلا بالله. ولو جاز تثبيت ما لا شاهد له في الشاهد، مع كونه من جوهره ، لجاز القول بفعل الحواس على ضد المعروف، أو الدرك بأضداد ما به الدرك.
And it is said: You have not observed anything unmixed of good and evil, nor has any report come to you that could be true. If they say: We know through evidence that the state of things is separation, and everything returns to the origin of its substance—the Shaykh (God have mercy on him) said— it is said: Rather, their state is combination; the final result of each is on the origin of its substance. And if it is the case that it (mixing) has occurred, then make it always so. And it is said: Since separation is dispersal, and combination is strengthening and power, why is their state not combination? And there is no power except with God. And if it were permissible to establish something without a witness in the witnessed world, while it is from its substance, then it would be permissible to say that the senses act contrary to what is known, or that perception is through opposites of what perception is.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage presents a counter-argument to the dualists' claim that they know by evidence (adilla) that things tend to separate and return to their original substance. Maturidi counters that one could equally claim that things tend to combine (ijtimāʿ), and that the final result of each is according to its origin. If mixing has occurred, why not assume it remains so always? Since separation leads to dispersal and dispersion while combination brings strength, it is more plausible that their state is combination. Then he raises a general epistemological principle: if one could establish something about the unseen without any basis in the witnessed world (shāhid), one could also claim absurdities like the senses acting contrary to their known function. This undermines the dualists' method of reasoning from the unseen.

Key Terms
معاينة
خبر
أدلة
تفرق
اجتماع
جوهر
شاهد
غائب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi rejects all analogical reasoning.
Correction: Maturidi only rejects the misuse of analogy when applied to the unseen without a basis in the witnessed world.

Checkpoint
What is the flaw Maturidi identifies in the dualists' claim that they know by evidence that things separate?
A) They have no evidence at all.
B) They can equally claim that things combine.
C) They rely on false reports.
D) They ignore the senses.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi argues that the dualists' use of evidence is arbitrary and their method of reasoning from the unseen is flawed without a basis in the witnessed world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The dualists claimed that things tend to separate, but Maturidi pointed out they had no witness for that. Think about a belief you hold about the unseen—like the nature of good and evil—that you've never questioned. Are you sure it is grounded in what you have witnessed? If not, will you reconsider it?
?
Maturidi argues that if we allow claims about the unseen without a witness, we could even claim that the senses work backwards. When in your life have you accepted a claim about the unseen (in faith or daily reasoning) without checking its basis in the witnessed? What would it take for you to demand that basis?
?
This lesson forces you to see that your own reasoning can be arbitrary. What is one area where you rely on 'evidence' that might actually be just your preference? How can you test it against the witnessed world as Maturidi did?

The Blackness Increase Dilemma
Maturidi questions dualists about whether blackness increases when darkness is added to light.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma Maturidi presents to dualists regarding the increase of blackness.

Core Idea
Adding blackness to darkness reveals the inconsistency of dualist reasoning.

Maturidi begins by posing a seemingly simple question about blackness increase. The dualists' answer forces them into a contradiction, exposing the flaws in their theory of natures.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the argument.
2
Identify the two answers
Notice the two possible answers to Maturidi's question: either blackness does not increase or it does.
Each answer leads to a different problem.
3
Examine the consequences
If the dualist says no, they contradict themselves. If yes, they must specify what increases.
4
Reflect on the implication
Why is this a problem for dualism? It shows their categories are insufficient.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسئل عن سواد الظلمة إذا زيد على سواد النور، هل زاد في السواد شيئًا ؟ فإن قالوا : لا ، صيروا ما كثر هو الذي لم يكثر . فإن قالوا ازداد قيل أهو النور أو الظلمة أو غيرهما ؟ فإن قال بالأولين فازداد النور أو الظلمة، وذلك بعيد؛ إذ يزداد كل واحد منهما بالجوهر الآخر. وإن قال غيرهما، أثبت للأمرين غيرا. ثم ما يدريهم أن ليس في النور أو الظلمة زيادة على تلك الأجناس الخمسة، وهم لا يعلمون بجميع الغائب؟ فإن ادّعى الاستدلال بالشاهد على الغائب أبطل قوله في التفرق وارتفاع النهاية؛ لأنه لم يشهد ذلك. فإن قال: علمنا بالرسل، فبطل قوله بالتفرق، إذ البعثة لا تكون إلا لجمع؛ وذلك جميع.
And they were asked about the blackness of darkness when added to the blackness of light: does it increase the blackness at all? If they say: No, then they cause what increased to be that which did not increase. If they say: It increased, it is said: Is it light or darkness or something else? If he says the first two, then light or darkness increased, and that is unlikely, since each increases by the other substance. If he says something else, he affirms for the two matters something else. Then what makes them know that there is no increase in light or darkness beyond those five types, while they do not know all the unseen? Then if he claims deduction from the witnessed to the unseen, his statement about separation and the removal of infinity is invalidated, because he did not witness that. If he says: We know through the messengers, then his statement about separation is invalidated, because mission (baʿtha) occurs only for gathering (jamʿ); that is everything.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This final passage addresses the problem of knowing the unseen (ghāʾib) through analogy from the witnessed (shāhid). Maturidi poses a question about the blackness of darkness and light: if you add them, does the blackness increase? The dualist must be consistent. Then he raises the issue of the five types (ajnās) mentioned earlier—how do they know there is no increase beyond these? This shows their reliance on faulty analogy. He then critiques their method of deducing unseen properties from witnessed ones. If they claim to know by evidence from the witnessed, their claims about separation (tafarruq) and the removal of infinity (irtafāʿ al-nihāya) are baseless because they are not witnessed. Alternatively, if they claim knowledge through messengers (rusul), then their doctrine of separation is refuted because the purpose of sending messengers is to gather people (jamʿ) around truth, not to separate.

Key Terms
سواد
زيادة
نور
ظلمة
جوهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The question is about physical color mixing.
Correction: It is a logical argument about the implications of dualist theory.

Checkpoint
What happens if the dualist says the blackness does not increase?
A) They avoid contradiction.
B) They make what increased equal to what did not.
C) They affirm a third thing.
D) They prove light is darkness.

Key Takeaway
The blackness dilemma shows that dualism leads to logical contradictions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine your own beliefs about good and evil, do you ever assume they are independent forces in a way that leads to similar contradictions? How might this argument help you see the necessity of a single source?
?
If you were pressed to explain why evil exists, would your reasoning hold up under logical scrutiny like the blackness dilemma? What would it take for you to abandon a comfortable but inconsistent position?
?
What 'increase' in your life have you attributed to a secondary cause rather than the Creator? Are you willing to trace every increase back to its true source, even when it costs you a narrative you prefer?

Critique of Dualist Epistemology
Maturidi critiques dualist claims to know the unseen and shows their inconsistency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's critique of dualist claims to know unseen realities through analogy or revelation.

Core Idea
Dualists cannot know the unseen consistently without undermining their own doctrine.

Maturidi extends his critique by questioning how dualists can claim knowledge about the unseen. He shows that their methods are either self-defeating or conflict with their principles.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation to understand the argument.
2
Identify the two sources of knowledge
Maturidi considers two possible methods: deduction from witnessed or knowledge through messengers.
3
Understand why deduction fails
Deduction from witnessed is invalid because the dualist has not witnessed separation or infinity.
4
Understand why messengers refute separation
Messengers are sent to gather people, not to separate; so appeal to messengers contradicts dualism.
5
Consider the overall implication
The dualist has no consistent way to know the unseen, undermining their entire system.

The Wisdom in Creating Harmful and Ugly Things
Maturidi explains the wisdom behind creating harmful and ugly things for moral discernment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the divine wisdom behind the creation of harmful substances, ugly sights, and sensory defects as a means for humans to discern good from evil and to know the unseen through analogy with the seen.

Core Idea
God created evil to teach us to recognize good.

Maturidi addresses a deep question: why would a wise God create harmful and ugly things? His answer is that without these sensory contrasts, humans would lack the basis for moral and practical discernment. This lesson unpacks his argument that such creations are necessary for human intellect to grasp the difference between good and evil and to infer the unseen from the seen.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Pay attention to Maturidi's logical flow.
The passage is dense; read it twice if needed.
2
Identify the key components
Note the three types of things Maturidi mentions: harmful substances, ugly sights, and defects in the senses. Ask yourself why each is necessary.
These are examples of sensory evils.
3
Trace the analogical argument
Maturidi argues that humans use sensory experience to understand what is not directly sensed. Reflect on how this reasoning applies to beliefs about the unseen.
This is a key epistemological point in Maturidi's thought.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما يجب في الحكمة من خلق الجواهر المؤذية والمناظر القبيحة وخلق الآفات في الحواس فهو بسبب أن البشر كلهم قد اعتقدوا شيئًا غاب عن حواسهم إما نفيا أو إثباتا؛ منهم من دانوا، ومنهم من تجاهل وحصل على الشهوات فإذا لم يخلق فيما يقع على الحواس ما ذكرنا لم يعرفوا القبيح من الحسن ولا المؤذى من النافع؛ وإذا لم يعقلوا ذلك لم يحتمل عقولهم درك القبيح من الحسن ولا المؤذى من الملذ. فخلق كذلك ليمثلوا بما تقع عليه الحواس ما لا تقع عليه، ليصير كل معتقد غاب عن البصر على ما عليه معروفاً بما يشاهد. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And what is necessary in wisdom [regarding] the creation of harmful substances and ugly sights and the creation of defects in the senses is because all humans have believed in something absent from their senses, either by negation or affirmation; some of them have religion, and some of them have ignored and attained desires. So if what we mentioned had not been created in what falls upon the senses, they would not know the ugly from the good nor the harmful from the beneficial; and if they did not comprehend that, their intellects could not bear the discernment of the ugly from the good nor the harmful from the pleasurable. So He created thus so that they may analogize by what falls upon the senses to what does not fall upon them, so that every belief absent from sight becomes known as it is by what is witnessed. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage explains the divine wisdom behind creating harmful substances, ugly sights, and defects in the senses. Maturidi argues that all humans hold beliefs about unseen things, either affirming or denying them. Some follow religion, others follow desires. If God had not created these harmful and ugly things, humans would not be able to distinguish good from evil or harmful from beneficial. Their intellects could not grasp these distinctions. Therefore, God created them so that by analogy from sensory experience, humans can understand what is not directly sensed. Thus, any belief about the unseen becomes known through comparison with what is witnessed.

Key Terms

الجواهر المؤذية
المناظر القبيحة
الآفات في الحواس
الحسن والقبيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi is justifying the existence of evil as something good in itself.
Correction: Maturidi is not justifying evil; he is explaining its purpose as a necessary means for human moral and intellectual discernment.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why did God create harmful and ugly things?
A) To test humans
B) To enable humans to distinguish good from evil through sensory experience
C) As punishment
D) To confuse humans

Key Takeaway
Maturidi argues that harmful and ugly things were created to enable humans to distinguish good from evil and to know the unseen through analogy with the seen.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
