?
The passage says that without these contrasts, your intellect could not bear moral discernment. Have you ever excused your own moral failures by blaming your lack of understanding? How does this passage challenge you to take responsibility for learning from the difficult experiences God has placed in your path?
?
You claim to believe in the unseen. But are you actively using the visible world—its good and its evil—as a means to strengthen that belief, or have you become numb to the signs around you? What specific step will you take today to practice the analogy from seen to unseen that Maturidi describes?

The Origin of Human Knowledge: Teaching, Not Intellect
Maturidi enumerates domains of human knowledge and argues they originate from teaching.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that according to Maturidi, the wide range of human knowledge and skills can only be explained by an original teaching, not by the unaided intellect.

Core Idea
Human knowledge of trades, languages, and crafts originates from teaching, not from intellect.

Maturidi opens with a sweeping survey of the many areas of human life—from trades and languages to medicine and child-rearing—that are essential for both worldly and religious wellbeing. He observes that human intellect alone cannot discover these; instead, they are learned from others, pointing to an original act of teaching. This lesson walks through this empirical argument.

Guided Path
1
Survey the domains
Read through the list of areas Maturidi mentions: trades, paths, languages, names, reproduction, child-rearing, nutrition, crafts, etc. Notice how many aspects of life depend on knowledge that is not innate.
2
Understand the claim
Maturidi concludes that these are 'clear evidence' that their origins are 'teaching and indication, not extraction of intellects.' Reflect on why he sees this as compelling evidence.
3
Consider the implication
If all this knowledge comes from teaching, then there must be an original teacher. This sets the stage for the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتخضع. ثم في أنواع التجارات التي لا يقوم لهم دين ولا دنيا إلا بها. ثم في ما فرق حوائجهم في البلدان الذي ليس في طبعهم ولا في عقولهم ما يدلهم أو يبين لهم في كل حاجة أنها أين تطلب؛ ثم في معرفة طرقها، إذ ليس في العقل ما يدل على مكانها ولا على طرقها؛ ثم في تعرف الألسن التي بها قامت النفس للمعاش وفهم المعاد. ثم في تعرف الأسماء التي لولا هي ما فهمت حاجة ولا أمكن أحدا معرفة موضعها. ثم في وجوه أسباب التناسل، وفي معرفة تربية الصغار؛ ثم في العلم بتدبير أغذية ما ليس يتطرق، ثم بظهور تعلم الخلق بعضهم من بعض جميع ما ذكرت من الألسن والأسماء والحرف والطب والصناعات كلها وطرق البلدان ورياضة الدواب وكيفية استعمالها؛ وجميع ما ذكرت هو الدليل البين على أن أصولها تعليم وإشارة لا استخراج العقول. والله الموفق. فهذا مع الأمر المعروف الموجود من فزع بعض إلى بعض عند النوائب وما يجربهم من الأمور المهمة للاستعانة برأيهم والصدور عن مشورتهم بما عندهم لهم فضل في العلم ثم تعليم فنون الآداب، ثم قيام كل بما يستفيد أنواع العلم من درس الكتب والاستماع إلى الحكماء. فدل ذلك أنهم لم يروا بعقولهم كفاية عن الاستعانة وأداء حاجاتهم جميعا. فلم في العقل الفزع إلى ناصح صدوق، وذلك ظن الخلق بأولئك أنه وصل إليهم العلوم على ألسن هؤلاء. فعلى ذلك أمر الدين والدنيا، وعلى ذلك علم السحر. وتعتبر جواهر الأشياء بأنواع المعالجات، وعلوم محاربة أعداء الدين والأموال، كلها مستفادة في الأمر الظاهر من الألسن، وما عنها يوجد. فأول ذلك تعليم يكون من العليم الحكيم.
And it submits. Then regarding the types of trades by which their religion and world cannot stand except through them. Then regarding what distributes their needs in the lands, when neither in their nature nor in their intellects is there something to guide them or show them in every need where it is sought; then regarding knowledge of its paths, since there is not in the intellect what indicates its place nor its paths; then regarding learning the languages by which the soul stands for livelihood and understanding the afterlife. Then regarding learning the names without which no need would be understood nor could anyone know its location. Then regarding the aspects of the causes of reproduction, and regarding knowledge of raising children; then regarding knowledge of managing nourishments that are not [naturally] accessible, then regarding the appearance of creatures learning from one another all that I mentioned of languages, names, crafts, medicine, all industries, paths of lands, training of beasts, and how to use them; and all that I mentioned is the clear evidence that their origins are teaching and indication, not extraction of intellects. And God grants success. So this, along with the known matter of some resorting to others at calamities and what befalls them of important matters to seek help from their opinion and to follow their counsel due to what they have of excellence in knowledge, then teaching the arts of etiquette, then each one undertaking what he acquires of types of knowledge from studying books and listening to the wise. So that indicates that they did not see in their intellects sufficiency from seeking help and fulfilling all their needs. So it is necessary in the intellect to resort to a truthful advisor, and that is the assumption of creatures concerning those that knowledge reached them upon the tongues of these. So upon that are the matters of religion and the world, and upon that is the science of magic. And the essences of things are considered by types of treatments, and the sciences of fighting enemies of religion and wealth, all are acquired in the apparent matter from tongues, and from what is found from them. So the first of that is teaching from the All-Knowing, the Wise.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage is a cumulative argument for the necessity of divine teaching in all domains of human life. Maturidi begins by listing various areas of human need—trades, pathways, languages, names, reproduction, child-rearing, nutrition, and all crafts and sciences—and observes that humans cannot derive knowledge of these from their intellects alone. Instead, they must learn from one another, which points to an original teaching and indication (تعليم وإشارة) rather than intellectual extraction. He then reinforces this by noting how people naturally seek guidance from the wise in times of need, showing that they recognize their intellectual insufficiency. This pattern applies to both worldly and religious matters, including even magic and medicine. The conclusion is that the first principle is teaching from the All-Knowing, the Wise (العليم الحكيم), thereby establishing the need for prophetic guidance.

Key Terms
تعليم
إشارة
استخراج العقول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That humans can acquire all necessary knowledge through their own reasoning without external teaching.
Correction: Maturidi argues that the diversity and specificity of human knowledge—like languages and crafts—cannot be derived from reason alone, so they must originate from teaching.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the origin of the various sciences and crafts that humans use?
A) Independent intellectual discovery
B) Teaching and indication from others
C) Innate instinct
D) Random trial and error

Key Takeaway
Maturidi uses the observable fact that humans learn trades, languages, and crafts from each other to prove that all such knowledge originates from teaching, not from unaided reason.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a problem in your life—whether in your work, relationships, or faith—do you assume your own intellect is sufficient to solve it, or do you turn to the teaching of Allah and His Messenger? What areas of your life have you been relying on your own reasoning instead of seeking guidance? Will you commit to asking for divine teaching before acting today?
?
The passage says that even the names of things and basic trades are learned from others. Have you ever thanked Allah that you were taught language by your parents and community, rather than having to discover it yourself? How would your life be different if you had to invent every word? Let that realization lead you to gratitude and humility.
?
Maturidi points out that people learn from one another. Is there someone in your life you have neglected to teach—a child, a sibling, a friend? What is one practical thing you can teach them this week, with the awareness that you are an instrument of Allah's teaching? Write it down and commit.

The Need for a Divine Teacher
Maturidi uses social evidence to argue for divine teaching.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the human practice of seeking counsel from the wise to argue for the necessity of a truthful advisor, culminating in the need for divine teaching.

Core Idea
Since humans seek guidance from each other, they must ultimately seek it from the All-Knowing.

In the second part of his argument, Maturidi points to the common human behavior of turning to the wise in times of difficulty. This shows that people recognize their intellectual insufficiency even in worldly matters. He then extends this to all areas—religion, magic, medicine, and warfare—concluding that the first teacher is the All-Knowing, the Wise. This lesson unpacks that final step.

Guided Path
1
Observe human behavior
Notice how people naturally resort to those with knowledge during calamities and important matters. This is an observable pattern.
2
Extend the pattern
Maturidi applies this same logic to religion and worldly affairs, even to magic and fighting enemies. All such knowledge is acquired from others.
3
Reach the conclusion
The first teaching must come from the All-Knowing, the Wise, establishing the necessity of divine guidance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتخضع. ثم في أنواع التجارات التي لا يقوم لهم دين ولا دنيا إلا بها. ثم في ما فرق حوائجهم في البلدان الذي ليس في طبعهم ولا في عقولهم ما يدلهم أو يبين لهم في كل حاجة أنها أين تطلب؛ ثم في معرفة طرقها، إذ ليس في العقل ما يدل على مكانها ولا على طرقها؛ ثم في تعرف الألسن التي بها قامت النفس للمعاش وفهم المعاد. ثم في تعرف الأسماء التي لولا هي ما فهمت حاجة ولا أمكن أحدا معرفة موضعها. ثم في وجوه أسباب التناسل، وفي معرفة تربية الصغار؛ ثم في العلم بتدبير أغذية ما ليس يتطرق، ثم بظهور تعلم الخلق بعضهم من بعض جميع ما ذكرت من الألسن والأسماء والحرف والطب والصناعات كلها وطرق البلدان ورياضة الدواب وكيفية استعمالها؛ وجميع ما ذكرت هو الدليل البين على أن أصولها تعليم وإشارة لا استخراج العقول. والله الموفق. فهذا مع الأمر المعروف الموجود من فزع بعض إلى بعض عند النوائب وما يجربهم من الأمور المهمة للاستعانة برأيهم والصدور عن مشورتهم بما عندهم لهم فضل في العلم ثم تعليم فنون الآداب، ثم قيام كل بما يستفيد أنواع العلم من درس الكتب والاستماع إلى الحكماء. فدل ذلك أنهم لم يروا بعقولهم كفاية عن الاستعانة وأداء حاجاتهم جميعا. فلم في العقل الفزع إلى ناصح صدوق، وذلك ظن الخلق بأولئك أنه وصل إليهم العلوم على ألسن هؤلاء. فعلى ذلك أمر الدين والدنيا، وعلى ذلك علم السحر. وتعتبر جواهر الأشياء بأنواع المعالجات، وعلوم محاربة أعداء الدين والأموال، كلها مستفادة في الأمر الظاهر من الألسن، وما عنها يوجد. فأول ذلك تعليم يكون من العليم الحكيم.
And it submits. Then regarding the types of trades by which their religion and world cannot stand except through them. Then regarding what distributes their needs in the lands, when neither in their nature nor in their intellects is there something to guide them or show them in every need where it is sought; then regarding knowledge of its paths, since there is not in the intellect what indicates its place nor its paths; then regarding learning the languages by which the soul stands for livelihood and understanding the afterlife. Then regarding learning the names without which no need would be understood nor could anyone know its location. Then regarding the aspects of the causes of reproduction, and regarding knowledge of raising children; then regarding knowledge of managing nourishments that are not [naturally] accessible, then regarding the appearance of creatures learning from one another all that I mentioned of languages, names, crafts, medicine, all industries, paths of lands, training of beasts, and how to use them; and all that I mentioned is the clear evidence that their origins are teaching and indication, not extraction of intellects. And God grants success. So this, along with the known matter of some resorting to others at calamities and what befalls them of important matters to seek help from their opinion and to follow their counsel due to what they have of excellence in knowledge, then teaching the arts of etiquette, then each one undertaking what he acquires of types of knowledge from studying books and listening to the wise. So that indicates that they did not see in their intellects sufficiency from seeking help and fulfilling all their needs. So it is necessary in the intellect to resort to a truthful advisor, and that is the assumption of creatures concerning those that knowledge reached them upon the tongues of these. So upon that are the matters of religion and the world, and upon that is the science of magic. And the essences of things are considered by types of treatments, and the sciences of fighting enemies of religion and wealth, all are acquired in the apparent matter from tongues, and from what is found from them. So the first of that is teaching from the All-Knowing, the Wise.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage is a cumulative argument for the necessity of divine teaching in all domains of human life. Maturidi begins by listing various areas of human need—trades, pathways, languages, names, reproduction, child-rearing, nutrition, and all crafts and sciences—and observes that humans cannot derive knowledge of these from their intellects alone. Instead, they must learn from one another, which points to an original teaching and indication (تعليم وإشارة) rather than intellectual extraction. He then reinforces this by noting how people naturally seek guidance from the wise in times of need, showing that they recognize their intellectual insufficiency. This pattern applies to both worldly and religious matters, including even magic and medicine. The conclusion is that the first principle is teaching from the All-Knowing, the Wise (العليم الحكيم), thereby establishing the need for prophetic guidance.

Key Terms
ناصح صدوق
العليم الحكيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That seeking guidance from others is optional or that reason is sufficient for all matters.
Correction: Maturidi shows that even in worldly affairs, people naturally seek counsel, proving that intellectual sufficiency is false. This applies equally to religion, requiring divine teaching.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi conclude is the first source of all teaching?
A) The intellect
B) Wise people
C) The All-Knowing, the Wise
D) Books

Key Takeaway
Maturidi concludes that since all knowledge is acquired through teaching, the first teacher must be God, who is All-Knowing and Wise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you truly relied on your own intellect alone in a major decision? After reading this passage, do you still think that was wise? Who are you actually resorting to: a 'truthful advisor' or your own limited understanding? Will you now turn to God before seeking human counsel?
?
All the knowledge you have—your profession, your skills, even your understanding of religion—has been taught to you by others. Does that humble you? Or do you secretly pride yourself on your own intelligence? How does recognizing that every teacher is a link to the All-Knowing change the way you view your own knowledge? Will you thank God for every lesson you've learned?
?
The passage mentions that even the science of magic is acquired through teaching. This shows that no knowledge is self-derived. What area of your life do you still think you can handle without divine guidance? Are you willing to admit that you need God's teaching even in what seems ordinary? Will you actively seek His guidance today?

The Limitation of Reason as a Created Means
Maturidi defines reason as a created cause with limits, subject to obscurity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's view that reason is a created, limited means of perception, and that it requires proof (burhān) to reach sound conclusions.

Core Idea
Reason is a created tool with limits; it needs proof to guide it.

Maturidi begins by clarifying the nature of the human intellect. He emphasizes that reason, like all created things, has a defined limit and is subject to the same obscurities that affect other perceptual faculties. This sets the stage for understanding why rational inquiry must be grounded in sound methodology and divine assistance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concluding phrase 'lā quwwata illā billāh' — it sets a tone of reliance on Allah.
2
Identify the key claims
Identify the three main claims: (1) reason is a created cause, (2) it has a limit like other means of perception, (3) its material is reflection upon causes, and the best reflection is supported by proof.
3
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: If reason is limited and created, what does that mean for theological reasoning? Why does Maturidi emphasize proof (burhān) and reliance on Allah?
This connects to the learning objective about the need for proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فعلى ذلك العقل، إذ هو سبب مخلوق، له حد كغيره من أسباب الإدراك، يعترضه ما يعترض غيره، مع غموض الأشياء واستغلاقها. ومن مادته النظر في الأسباب؛ أعلى ذلك مما يُسمع من كلام الحكماء، وأحقهم من له على حكمته برهان. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Upon that, the intellect, since it is a created cause, has a limit like other means of perception; what affects others affects it, along with the obscurity and complexity of things. And from its material is reflection upon causes; the most appropriate of that is what is heard from the speech of the wise, and the most deserving among them is one whose wisdom has a proof. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage defines the nature and scope of the human intellect (al-ʿaql) in a theological context. Maturidi begins by affirming that reason is a created cause (sabab makhluq), meaning it is contingent and not absolute. Like all other faculties of perception, it has a defined limit (hadd) and is subject to the same obstacles that affect other means of knowing. Moreover, the objects of knowledge are often obscure (ghumūḍ) and impenetrable (istighlāq). The material (māddah) of the intellect is reflection (naẓar) upon causes, and the best instance of this is the discourse of the wise—provided their wisdom is supported by proof (burhān). The concluding phrase 'lā quwwata illā billāh' emphasizes human dependence on divine aid. Thus Maturidi establishes that reason, though valuable, is inherently limited and requires external guidance.

Key Terms
العقل (al-ʿaql)
السبب المخلوق (al-sabab al-makhluq)
الحد (al-hadd)
النظر (al-naẓar)
البرهان (al-burhān)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Reason is an absolute, uncreated faculty that can know everything without aid.
Correction: Maturidi states reason is a created cause with limits, like other means of perception, and its conclusions require supporting proof and reliance on Allah.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the status of the human intellect?
A) It is an uncreated, absolute faculty
B) It is a created cause with limits like other means of perception
C) It is capable of knowing everything without aid
D) It is irrelevant to theological knowledge

Key Takeaway
Reason is a limited, created tool; its proper use involves reflection on causes and seeking proof, always with reliance on Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what area of your life do you most trust your own reasoning above seeking proof from the Quran and Sunnah? What concrete step can you take today to change that?
?
Are you willing to admit that your intellect is limited and needs divine guidance? What would that admission cost you in terms of pride or control?
?
How does the phrase 'la quwwata illa billah' challenge your daily reliance on your own abilities? When will you say it with full conviction this week?

Two Ways to Know the Unseen: Inference and Report
Maturidi presents two means of knowing beyond sense: inference and report.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two divinely established means for knowing beyond sensory experience: inference from the seen and authoritative report, and why report is necessary for matters of religious command.

Core Idea
Allah gave two keys to the unseen: inference from the seen and report from the truthful.

Maturidi continues his epistemology by presenting the tools Allah has given us to know what lies beyond our senses. While direct perception is limited, we can infer causes from effects (like smoke indicating fire) and we can rely on reports from trustworthy sources (like distant lands or prophetic messages). This passage establishes that for religious commands and prohibitions, report is indispensable, and accepting it leads to affirming prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic text and its literal translation. Notice the structure: Maturidi first states the two means, gives examples, then applies to religious knowledge.
2
Identify the two means
Locate the two terms: الاستدلال (inference) and الخبر (report). Understand their definitions and examples.
Inference works when there is a necessary connection between the seen and unseen.
3
Connect to prophethood
Notice how Maturidi argues that affirmation of prophethood is necessary because we need reports for non-sensible matters like commands and prohibitions.
This shows the rational basis for accepting prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجه آخر، إن الله جل ثناؤه جعل السبب الذي به دَرْك كل خارج عن الحس وجهين. أحدهما الاستدلال بالذي عاين إذا اتصل الغائب بالذي عاين، كاتصال الدخان بالنار، وضياء الشمس بها، وكاتصال أثر الفعل بالفاعل نحو الكتابة والبنيان ونحو ذلك. والثاني الخبر ينبئ عن حال ذلك، نحو البلدان النائية والأحوال المتغيرة والأمور النازلة، معروف ذلك عند جميع العقلاء. وبذلك معرفة الانسان الأجناس والفصول والأنواع، وأنواع الطب واللسان وعلوم الصناعات والحروب وغير ذلك. ثم تعرّف الأمر والنهي، والوعد والوعيد، فيما ليس بمحسوس دليله، لا وجه لإدراكه إلا بالخبر، وذلك نحو المباح والمحظور، وما فيه عادة كل شيء من مختلف الأحوال، فيلزم في نحو هذا القول بالخبر. وفيه إيجاب القول بالرسالة.
Another aspect: Allah—glory be to Him—made the means by which everything beyond sense is perceived two. One of them is inference from what one has witnessed when the unseen is connected to what was witnessed, like the connection of smoke to fire, and the light of the sun to it, and like the connection of the trace of action to the agent, such as writing and building and so on. And the second is a report that informs about the state of that, such as distant lands, changing conditions, and occurring events—this is known among all rational people. And by this is man's knowledge of genera, differentiae, species, and the types of medicine, language, crafts, warfare, and other things. Then commands and prohibitions, promise and threat are known in matters whose proof is not sensible; there is no way to perceive them except by report, such as the permissible and the prohibited, and what is customary for everything among different states. So in such matters, it is necessary to accept the report. And in this is the affirmation of prophethood.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage presents a second avenue for knowledge beyond sensory perception. Maturidi states that Allah has established two means for grasping the supersensible. The first is inference (istidlāl) from the witnessed (al-ʿāyin) to the unseen (al-ghāʾib) when there is a necessary connection, e.g., smoke indicates fire, light indicates the sun, and an effect indicates a cause (like writing indicates a writer). The second is report (khabar) that conveys information about things not directly perceived, such as distant lands or past events—this is universally accepted. Through these two methods, humans know categories, medicine, language, etc. Moreover, matters of commands, prohibitions, promise, and threat—whose proof is not sensory—can only be known through report. This includes rulings on permissibility and prohibition. Therefore, acceptance of reports is necessary, and this logically entails affirming prophethood (al-risālah) because prophets bring such reports.

Key Terms
الاستدلال
الخبر
الغائب
المعاين
الرسالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Reason alone can know all moral truths.
Correction: Maturidi emphasizes that for non-sensible matters like commands and prohibitions, only report (revelation) can provide knowledge, so reason alone is insufficient.

Checkpoint
What are the two means of knowing things beyond sense according to Maturidi?
A) Intuition and dreams
B) Inference from the seen and report
C) Debates and analogies
D) Inspiration and philosophy

Key Takeaway
Allah has provided two ways to know the unseen: inference from the seen and report from trustworthy sources, and the latter is essential for understanding divine commands and affirming prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a religious command that seems irrational to your mind, do you instinctively rely on your own reasoning or on the report of the Prophet? What does your choice reveal about your trust in the unseen?
?
Think of a time you accepted something unseen through inference (like a medical diagnosis from symptoms). Are you equally quick to accept Allah's commands through the report of the Messenger? If not, what is the root of that hesitation?
?
If you consistently prioritize inference over revelation in matters of morality and obligation, are you not effectively denying the need for prophethood? How can you actively train yourself to submit to the report (khabar) even when your limited senses disagree?

Rejection of Prophethood as Disguised Whim
Maturidi refutes the claim that reason forbids prophethood by diagnosing it as whim disguised as reason.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi exposes that objections to prophethood based on reason often stem from subjective whims rather than genuine rationality.

Core Idea
Rational rejection of prophethood is often just disguised whim.

Maturidi now addresses a specific objection: that reason itself prohibits prophethood. He argues that such a claim actually reveals ignorance of what reason truly is. The denier, according to Maturidi, is not using reason but is merely following his own desires and aversions, inverting true judgments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the structure: Maturidi first paraphrases the objection, then diagnoses its flaw, then prescribes a remedy.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the denier's error
What does Maturidi say causes the denier to issue false judgments? Look for the phrase 'ما هوته نفسه' and 'جهل بالعقل'.
3
Reflect on the cure
Maturidi says the denier should learn the true nature of reason. Ask yourself: Why would that solve the problem?
His judgments would be invalidated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويُناظر فيما قال: «إنه مما يحرمه العقل من المديح. [فهذه] حق لمن ذلك علمه مما [يوجبه] العقل أو الطبيعة: أن يجعل الكل ما هوته نفسه وما نفرت عنه طبيعته بضده. فيقلب الأحكام عن حقائقها، ويبيّن أنه عن جهل بالعقل خرجت قضاياه. فحق مثله أن يعلم حقيقة العقل، فيُبطل بحكمه ويمقت نفسه بجهله العقل من الهوى. والله الموفق.
And he argues regarding what he said: 'It is something that reason prohibits from praise.' This is true for one who knows that—whether reason or nature necessitates—he makes everything [conform to] what his soul desires and what his nature dislikes as its opposite. So he inverts judgments from their realities, and it becomes clear that his statements issued from ignorance of reason. So it is right for someone like him to know the true nature of reason, so that his judgment would be invalidated and he would despise himself for his ignorance of reason from whim. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage continues the refutation of the denier. Maturidi responds to the denier's claim that prophethood is something that reason prohibits (yuḥarrimuhu al-ʿaql). He states that if one truly understands reason or nature, one would realize that the denier's argument merely reflects his own subjective desires (hawā nafsihi) and aversions. The denier thus inverts true judgments, replacing them with whims. His statements arise from ignorance (jahl) of the true nature of reason. Therefore, such a person should learn what reason really is; then he would recognize the invalidity of his own reasoning and would despise himself for confusing reason with caprice. Maturidi concludes by affirming that Allah grants success.

Key Terms
العقل (al-ʿaql)
الهوى (al-hawā)
الجهل (al-jahl)
الحقائق (al-ḥaqāʾiq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that rational objections to prophethood are objective and well-founded.
Correction: Maturidi points out that such objections may actually be driven by subjective whims and ignorance of reason's true nature.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say causes the denier to issue invalid judgments?
A) Knowledge of reason
B) Whims and aversion of his nature
C) Careful study of miracles
D) Following the majority

Key Takeaway
Claims that reason forbids something may actually be driven by personal whims, and true understanding of reason prevents such confusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you dismissed a religious truth because it felt 'unreasonable' to you? Could that feeling have been your whim disguised as reason?
?
What would it mean for you to truly know the nature of your own intellect? Are you willing to let your judgments be invalidated if they are built on ignorance?
?
This lesson exposes a dangerous habit of the soul: inverting truth to match desire. Where in your life today are you most vulnerable to this inversion? What will you do to correct it by the end of this week?

The Dilemma of Skepticism and the Basis of Belief
Maturidi presents a dialectical argument against absolute skepticism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi exposes the incoherence of absolute skepticism and establishes the necessity of belief as a starting point for knowledge.

Core Idea
Absolute skepticism is self-defeating: skeptics cannot deny belief without contradicting themselves.

Maturidi engages with a hypothetical skeptic who denies all belief. Through a dialectical exchange, he shows that thoroughgoing skepticism is logically untenable. This passage demonstrates the foundational role of belief in any rational discourse, and it sets the stage for the kalam project of establishing true belief in Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the dialectical exchange
Follow the back-and-forth between Maturidi and the skeptic. Notice how the skeptic is forced into a dilemma.
2
Analyze the two responses
Consider what happens if the skeptic says 'no' and then if he says 'yes'. How does Maturidi use each response to undermine skepticism?
Pay attention to the logical consequences.
3
Reflect on the foundation
Read the concluding statement about the foundation of skepticism. How does Maturidi characterize the skeptic's position?
This ties the argument together.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال له: تعتقد شيئًا ألبتة؟ فإن قال: لا، أقر أنه لم يعتقد تكذيب من ذكر، ولا أنه هو، ولا هو حي أو ميت، فتكلّفه الأجوبة والمعارضات خطأ. وإن قال: نعم، قيل: لعلك تعتقده بما لم تبلغ قوة دركك وعلمك بالأشياء مبلغ الإحالة؛ إذ قد رأيت كثيرًا من المعتقدين بطل اعتقادهم، فلعل طبيعتك أرتك ذلك الفساد؛ ويجوز أن تكون في الطبائع طبيعة نقية تدرك كذلك فيما اعتقدت؛ ويظهر جهلك بعد. فمهما قال من شيء فهو له في جميع ما أنكر جواب. وأصله أن كل من استحار الخروج من المعارف والتفوه بغير الموجود في الطبائع بلا شيء سوى أنه لم يكن أو لعله يكون أبطل سبيل تثبيت شيء ألبتة أو نفيه، ويكون في حد الشاكين في البيان كله ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then it is said to him: Do you believe something at all? If he says: No, he acknowledges that he does not believe in the denial of the one mentioned, nor that he is that one, nor that he is alive or dead, so burdening him with answers and objections is wrong. And if he says: Yes, it is said: Perhaps you believe it based on something that has not reached the strength of your perception and knowledge of things to the level of impossibility; since you have seen many believers whose belief proved false, perhaps your nature showed you that corruption; and it is possible that among the natures there is a pure nature that perceives correctly what you believed; and your ignorance will appear later. So whatever he says of anything, it is for him—against all that he denies—an answer. And its foundation is that whoever is perplexed about leaving knowledge and uttering what does not exist in nature without anything other than that it is not or perhaps it will be, has invalidated the way of affirming anything at all or denying it, and becomes among the doubters in all discourse. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical argument against absolute skepticism. Maturidi imagines an interlocutor who is asked whether he believes anything at all. If he denies all belief, then he cannot consistently deny or affirm any particular proposition, so engaging him in argument is futile. If he admits to believing something, then his belief may be based on insufficient evidence or could be mistaken, as many beliefs have proven false. Maturidi then points out that the skeptic's claim that nothing can be known is self-refuting: whatever he says can be turned back on him. The root of the problem is that anyone who retreats from knowledge and speaks contrary to the realities of nature, without any basis except that something is not or might be, has destroyed the possibility of affirming or denying anything. Such a person joins the ranks of the doubters in all discourse.

Key Terms
اعتقاد
شك
طبيعة
معرفة
إحالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi is rejecting all forms of doubt and inquiry.
Correction: Maturidi is specifically targeting absolute skepticism that denies any knowledge, not healthy skepticism or inquiry.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what happens if the interlocutor says he believes nothing at all?
A) He is praised for honesty.
B) He must still answer objections.
C) He has no basis to deny or affirm anything, so engaging him is pointless.
D) He is considered a true monotheist.

Key Takeaway
Absolute skepticism is logically self-defeating because it undermines the very basis for making any truth claim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever doubted something so deeply that you felt paralyzed, unable to affirm or deny? What did that do to your soul?
?
When you catch yourself questioning everything, what is the one belief you find you cannot let go of without breaking apart?
?
If you were asked 'Do you believe something at all?' and you answered 'Yes', what is the basis of that belief? Is it firm enough to withstand the skeptic's attack?

The Rational Necessity of Accepting Reports
Maturidi establishes the rational necessity of accepting reliable reports for human life.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that intellect must accept widely transmitted reports of significant events, and how this principle applies to prophethood.

Core Idea
Reports of great events spread inevitably; rejecting them destroys knowledge of this world and the next.

This lesson explores Imam Maturidi's foundational argument for the acceptance of reports (akhbar). He argues that rejecting all reports would undermine the purpose of hearing and speech, and would prevent us from obtaining basic necessities. From there, he shows that reports of major events—like a king's death or prophetic miracles—spread inevitably, confirming their truth.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic source and its translation to grasp Maturidi's argument as a whole.
2
Identify the key premise
Notice that Maturidi begins with the rational necessity of accepting reports to preserve the function of hearing/speech and to enable access to food and medicine.
3
Examine the scaling principle
Understand that reports spread according to the magnitude of the event; major events become widely known even if people try to conceal them.
4
Connect to prophethood
See how this principle validates the widespread reports of prophetic miracles and conquests.
5
Reflect on the implication
Appreciate that this is a rational argument for the reliability of tawatur (mass transmission) in religious matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
{قال الشيخ رحمه الله:} والأصل في هذا أن الأخبار التي لزم في العقول قبولها لما في رد ذلك بطلان حكمة السمع واللسان وفيه زوال المعاش والمعاد وانقطاع الوصول إلى الأغذية والأدوية التي بها حياة الأبدان. ثم كانت الأخبار تتفاقم في الانتشار على قدر الأمور التي عنها الإخبار في العظم، نحو ملك لو قتل لانتشر أمره بالضرورة حتى لو أحب الناس كتمان مثله لما قدروا عليه، وكذلك الخارجة من المعارف المعتادة؛ وفيما يقل خطره أو يجري على المعتاد لا يظهر ظهوره، بل لعله لا يذكر. معروف ذلك في الخلقة، وعلى ذلك انتشرت أخبار الفتوح وقهر الملوك. فعلى مثل هذا أمر الرسل؛ لأنهم جاءوا بالأمور العظام.
{Al-Shaykh, may Allah have mercy on him, said:} The foundation in this is that reports which the intellects must accept because rejecting that would nullify the wisdom of hearing and speech, and in it is the ruin of livelihood and the afterlife, and the cutting off of access to foods and medicines by which the life of bodies is sustained. Then reports increase in spread according to the magnitude of the matters being reported about, like a king: if he were killed, his affair would spread necessarily, even if people loved to conceal the like of it, they would not be able to do so; and likewise the emergence of extraordinary miraculous knowledge; and regarding what is of little significance or occurs habitually, it does not appear manifestly, rather perhaps it is not mentioned. This is known in creation, and on that basis the reports of conquests and the subjugation of kings spread. And similarly is the matter of the messengers, because they came with great matters.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage is a foundational principle regarding the acceptance of reports (akhbar). Maturidi argues that intellects must accept certain reports because rejecting all reports would nullify the purpose of hearing and speech, and would make it impossible to obtain essential needs like food and medicine. Reports spread in proportion to the importance of the events: a king's death becomes widely known even if people try to conceal it. Similarly, miraculous events become widely known. In contrast, trivial or habitual events may not be mentioned. The prophets are like this: they brought great matters, and their reports spread widely, confirming their truth.

Key Terms
الأخبار
السمع واللسان
المعاش والمعاد
الفتوح
قهر الملوك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think this argument justifies accepting all reports without scrutiny.
Correction: Maturidi only argues for accepting reports of great significance that spread widely, not every report.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why must intellects accept some reports?
A) Because all reports are true.
B) Because rejecting all reports would destroy the wisdom of hearing and speech and prevent access to necessities.
C) Because reports are always from reliable sources.
D) Because the Prophet ordered it.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi establishes that reports of great, transformative events spread inevitably and must be accepted by reason, proving the reliability of prophetic traditions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You rely on reports every day: who made your phone, what food is safe to eat, where your next paycheck comes from. If you rejected all such reports, your life would collapse. Now—does the report of the Prophet sound any less certain to you? If it truly matters for your eternal life, why does it not feel as immediate as a news alert?
?
The king's death spreads inevitably because people cannot contain news of such magnitude. But the message of Islam has reached you, personally. Has it spread through your life with the same force making you act? Or have you let it become background noise, a trivial thing you barely mention?
?
Maturidi says rejecting reports cuts you off from food and medicine. But rejecting the prophetic report cuts you off from the afterlife. You have not starved yourself physically; are you starving your soul by ignoring the only report that tells you how to reach the next life?

The Rational Necessity of Accepting Reports
Maturidi states the rational necessity of accepting reports due to the wisdom of hearing and speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi holds that accepting reports is a rational necessity grounded in the wisdom of hearing and speech and the preservation of worldly and religious life.

Core Idea
Rejecting reports nullifies the purpose of hearing and speech.

This passage establishes one of the foundations of Maturidi's epistemology: the necessity of accepting reports. Maturidi grounds this necessity in reason itself, arguing that rejecting reports would destroy the very purpose of human communication and threaten both worldly and spiritual well-being. Read carefully to see how he connects the rejection of reports to the loss of livelihood and afterlife.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage identifying the main claim
The passage begins by stating that reason must accept reports. Underline the phrase that expresses this.
The phrase is 'الأخبار التي لزم في العقول قبولها'.
2
Note the two consequences of rejecting reports
Maturidi gives two major consequences: (1) nullification of the wisdom of hearing and speech, and (2) loss of livelihood and afterlife. Write them down.
3
Reflect on the connection to food and medicine
Maturidi specifically mentions foods and medicines as dependent on reports. Why do you think he chose these examples?
This highlights the practical necessity of reports for survival.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ رحمه الله : والأصل في هذا أن الأخبار التي لزم في العقول قبولها لما في رد ذلك بطلان حكمة السمع واللسان وفيه زوال المعاش والمعاد وانقطاع الوصول إلى الأغذية والأدوية التي بها حياة الأبدان. ثم كانت الأخبار تتفاقم في الانتشار على قدر الأمور التي عنها الإخبار في العظم، نحو ملك لو قتل لانتشر أمره بالضرورة حتى لو أحب الناس كتمان مثله لما قدروا عليه، وكذلك الخارجة من المعارف المعتادة؛ وفيما يقل خطره أو يجري على المعتاد لا يظهر ظهوره، بل لعله لا يذكر. معروف ذلك في الخلقة، وعلى ذلك انتشرت أخبار الفتوح وقهر الملوك . فعلى مثل هذا أمر الرسل ;
The Shaykh, may God have mercy on him, said: The foundation in this is that reports (al-akhbar) which reason (al-'uqul) must accept because rejecting them entails the nullification of the wisdom of hearing and speech, and in it is the loss of livelihood and the afterlife (al-ma'ash wa al-ma'ad), and the cutting off of access to foods and medicines by which the life of bodies is sustained. Then reports increase in spread according to the magnitude of the matters about which the report is given, like a king if he is killed, his matter spreads necessarily, even if people loved to conceal the like of it, they would not be able to; and likewise [reports] that depart from customary knowledge; and in matters of little significance or that proceed customarily, it does not appear, indeed it is perhaps not mentioned. That is well-known in [human] nature, and accordingly the reports of conquests and the vanquishing of kings spread. And upon this model is the matter of the messengers.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage is a foundational argument for the necessity of accepting reports. Maturidi states that reason must accept reports because rejecting them would destroy the very purpose of the faculties of hearing and speech (sam' and lisan), which are given for communication. Furthermore, it would lead to the ruin of both worldly life (ma'ash) and the afterlife (ma'ad), since people rely on reports for food and medicine. He then explains that reports spread in proportion to the importance of the event: a king's murder becomes known by necessity, even if people tried to hide it. Similarly, events outside normal custom spread widely, while trivial or routine matters remain obscure. This is a known pattern in human nature, as seen in the spread of reports of conquests and victories over kings. He concludes that the same principle applies to the messengers.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi establishes a rational foundation for accepting reports, linking it to the very purpose of human faculties and survival.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your hearing and speech are gifts. How often do you use them to transmit or receive reports carelessly? This lesson says rejecting reports nullifies their wisdom. What one change will you make today to honor the purpose of these faculties?
?
Imagine your life without relying on any report from others—food, medicine, directions, even prayer times. Could you survive? This passage forces you to admit that your very existence depends on accepting reports. Does that humble your pride in self-sufficiency? What will you do with that humility?
?
The passage ties rejecting reports to loss of both this life and the afterlife. Have you ever dismissed a religious report because it was inconvenient or uncomfortable? How will you ensure that your reason submits to the truth of reports from God and His messenger, especially when they challenge your desires?

The Proportional Spread of Reports
Maturidi explains that reports spread proportionally to the importance of events.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that reports spread in proportion to the importance of the events they convey, and how this applies to the messengers.

Core Idea
Important events inevitably become widely known; trivial ones remain obscure.

Having established the necessity of reports, Maturidi now explains a natural phenomenon: the spread of reports depends on the magnitude of the event. This principle is observed in everyday life, from the murder of a king to the reports of conquests, and it applies to the messages brought by the prophets. Pay attention to the examples he gives.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Focus on the phrase 'ثم كانت الأخبار تتفاقم في الانتشار' and the examples that follow.
2
Identify the three types of events mentioned
Maturidi contrasts the spread of reports about: (a) a king's murder, (b) events outside custom, and (c) trivial or routine matters. List them.
3
Connect to the pattern of conquests and messengers
He then says reports of conquests spread, and that this is the pattern of messengers. Why do you think he draws this analogy?
The reports of messengers are of great importance, so they spread widely.

Method for Debating Selective Belief in Prophets
Maturidi outlines a debating method for selective belief in prophets.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational method for engaging with those who accept some prophets but reject others, based on asking them for the criterion they used to accept any prophet.

Core Idea
The proof for one prophet must apply to all prophets.

This passage from Imam Maturidi introduces a key argumentative strategy. When faced with someone who selectively believes in some prophets while denying others, the first step is to ask them to articulate the reason (المعنى) they accept the prophets they do. This creates a consistent rational standard that either validates all prophets or none.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation, to grasp Maturidi's concise instruction.
The passage is short but dense; note the key term 'المعنى' (the meaning/reason).
2
Identify the key question
Maturidi proposes that we ask them about the 'meaning' (المعنى) for which they affirmed the prophets they accept. What is that question exactly?
3
Reflect on the logical implication
If they can state a criterion, that same criterion must apply to the prophets they deny. This forces consistency. What happens if they cannot state a criterion?
This method assumes that acceptance of prophethood must be based on a rational ground.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم القول فيها بيننا وبين المقرين بالرسل جملة والمنكرين لبعضهم على الإشارة: أن نسألهم عن المعنى الذي له أقروا به أو أقر به سلفهم. فإن أشاروا إلى معنى على تحقيق ذلك
Then the statement in this matter between us and those who affirm messengers in general and those who deny some of them upon specification: that we ask them about the meaning for which they affirmed him or their predecessors affirmed him. If they point to a meaning upon realization of that...
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage introduces a new argumentative strategy. The author states that when debating with people who accept some messengers (like the People of the Book who accept earlier prophets but deny Muhammad) but deny others, the method is to ask them: what is the reason (المعنى) that led them to affirm the prophet they do accept, or that led their predecessors to do so? If they can identify a clear rational criterion (على تحقيق ذلك), then that same criterion should apply to the prophet they deny. The passage is cut off, but the intended argument is that the proof for one prophet should logically extend to all prophets, as the basis for accepting prophethood is the same.

Key Terms
المقرون بالرسل
المنكرون لبعضهم
على الإشارة
المعنى
على تحقيق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Different prophets require different types of proof.
Correction: Maturidi argues that the rational basis for accepting any prophet must be universal and applied consistently across all prophets.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the first step in debating with someone who accepts some prophets but denies others?
A) Ask them to provide miracles for the prophet they deny
B) Ask them about the reason they accepted the prophet they do believe in
C) Quote scripture to prove the denied prophet is true
D) Accuse them of inconsistency

Key Takeaway
The consistent application of the same rational criterion for accepting prophethood is a powerful tool in debate with selective believers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you accept all the prophets and their messages without exception, or do you find yourself favoring certain teachings while ignoring others? What would the method of this lesson reveal about your own heart?
?
Have you ever dismissed a command from Allah because it seemed difficult, while readily accepting other commands that suited you? How does Maturidi's principle of consistency challenge your selective obedience?
?
Consider the prophets you find easy to follow and those you struggle with. Is your acceptance based on a rational criterion or on your own desires? Ask yourself honestly: if you apply the same standard to all, would you be consistent?

God's Sovereignty and the Purpose of Creation
Maturidi opens with a doxology praising God's unique attributes and the test of human actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi's doxology establishes the theological framework for discussing human actions, the nature of God's attributes, and the purpose of creation as a test.

Core Idea
God's sovereignty and wisdom frame the test of human actions.

Maturidi begins his work not with a dry proposition, but with a doxology that grounds all subsequent theology. This praise of God's unique attributes and the purpose of creation sets the stage for understanding human responsibility and divine justice. The passage reveals that God's actions are not subject to question, while humans are tested through wisdom and foolishness.

Guided Path
1
Read the doxology
Read the Arabic and translation to appreciate the tone and content. Notice the flow from praise to purpose.
The doxology is not just ritual; it carries theological weight.
2
Identify key attributes of God
Notice how God is described: unique in pre-eternity and divinity, exclusive in perpetuity and lordship, possessor of clear proof and great dominion. These attributes establish His absolute sovereignty.
These attributes are foundational for Maturidi's theology.
3
Understand the purpose of creation
See why foolishness and wisdom are possible in humans: so they may be tested through questioning and recompense, and desire wisdom. This frames human actions as a divine test.
The test is tied to God's justice and wisdom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة في أفعال الخلق وإثباتها السفه، الحمد لله المتوحد بالقدم والإلهية، المتفرد بالدوام والربوبية، ذي البرهان المنير والملك الكبير؛ الذي فطر الخلق بقدرته، وصرفهم بحكمته على سابق علمه ومشيئته، وتقلب كل بريته في مواهبه وإحسانه. أنشأ الأشياء كيف شاء، لا يسأل عما يفعل وهم يسألون [الأنبياء، ٢٣/٢١]، لما يتمكن منهم السفه والحكمة، ليجروا بالسؤال ثم بالجزاء عن [؟] ويرغبوا في الحكمة. ونسأله أن يكرمنا بالتوفيق، ويجدد عزمنا بالتسديد، وينور قلوبنا بالتوحيد، فإنه حميد مجيد.
Question concerning the actions of the creation and proving foolishness. Praise be to God, the Unique in pre-eternity and divinity, the Exclusive in perpetuity and lordship, the Possessor of clear proof and great dominion; who created the creation by His power, and directed them by His wisdom according to His prior knowledge and will, and the turning of all His creatures in His gifts and beneficence. He brought things into being as He willed. He is not asked about what He does, but they are asked [Surah al-Anbiya 21:23], because foolishness and wisdom are possible in them, so that they may be drawn by questioning and then by recompense from/for [؟] and desire wisdom. And we ask Him to honor us with success, and renew our resolve with guidance, and illuminate our hearts with tawhid, for He is Praiseworthy and Glorious.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage is a doxology and introduction. It begins with a heading stating the topic: a question concerning human actions and proving foolishness. Then it praises God as unique in pre-eternity and divinity, exclusive in perpetuity and lordship, possessing clear proof and great dominion. He created creation by His power and directs them by His wisdom according to His prior knowledge and will. He brings forth things as He wills; He is not questioned about what He does, but they are questioned (Quran 21:23). Because foolishness and wisdom are possible in them, so that they may be tested by questioning and then by recompense, and desire wisdom. Then the author asks God for success, resolve, and illumination of hearts with tawhid. This establishes the framework for the subsequent discussion.

Key Terms
التوحيد
السفه
الحكمة
المحنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The doxology is merely rhetorical praise without theological content.
Correction: Maturidi's opening doxology establishes key theological principles: God's unique sovereignty, the purpose of creation as a test, and the framework for discussing human actions.

Checkpoint
According to the doxology, what is the reason God created humans with the capacity for foolishness and wisdom?
A) to test them with questioning and recompense
B) to make them perfect
C) to show His power
D) to make them miserable

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's doxology grounds the discussion of human actions in God's absolute sovereignty and the purpose of testing through wisdom and foolishness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You read that God is not asked about what He does, but you will be asked. What specific actions in your life are you currently avoiding accountability for? How does this verse (21:23) change your perspective on your own choices today?
?
The passage says that foolishness and wisdom are possible in you, and that your actions are part of a test. Which of your daily decisions most clearly reflect wisdom, and which reflect foolishness? Will you take one step to replace a foolish habit with a wise one today?
?
Maturidi asks God to illuminate his heart with tawhid. In what area of your life is your tawhid weakest—where do you act as if you are not being watched or questioned? What is one concrete action you can take this week to strengthen that area?

The Two Means of Knowledge: Direct Perception and Revelation
Maturidi enumerates the two means of knowledge and their relationship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that according to Imam Maturidi, knowledge is attained through two means: direct sensory perception (al-`iyān) and revelation (al-sam`), and that direct perception serves as the foundation and verifier for revelation.

Core Idea
Direct perception is the bedrock; revelation is verified by it.

Imam Maturidi lays out the epistemological framework for attaining true knowledge. He explains that God has created two paths: the clear and apparent, and the hidden. This lesson presents these two means and shows how they relate, especially how revelation depends on direct perception for verification.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the parallel structure: 'two aspects' and 'two divisions'.
2
Identify the two divisions
The passage explicitly states the path has two divisions: direct perception (العيان) and revelation (السمع). Find these terms and note their definitions.
3
Understand the relationship
Notice that direct perception is described as the foundation (أصلا) for hidden matters, and revelation's truth is known through the evidence of direct perception. Ask yourself why this ordering matters.
This connects to the learning objective about the dependency of revelation on perception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى ذلك جعل الأسباب التي بها التوصل لهم إلى الأصل الذي به يرتقى إلى كل درجة وينال كل فضيلة وهو العلم على وجهين على الظاهر البين والخفى المستور، ليتفاضل بذلك أولوا العقل على قدر تفاضلهم في الاجتهاد واحتمال ما كرهته الطباع ونفرت عنه النفس. وعلى ذلك جعل سبيله قسمين: أحدهما العيان الذي هو أخص الأسباب، وهو الذي ليس معه جهل، ليكون أصلا لما خفى منه. والثاني السمع الذي عن دلالة الأعيان يعرف صدقه وكذبه.
And upon that He made the means by which they attain the origin, with which one rises to every degree and attains every virtue, i.e. knowledge, in two aspects: the apparent clear and the hidden concealed, so that people of intellect may excel according to their degree of effort and endurance of what nature disliked and the soul recoiled from. And upon that He made His path two divisions: one of them is direct perception which is the most specific means, and which has no ignorance with it, so that it may be a foundation for what is hidden from it. The second is revelation, the truthfulness of which is known through the evidence of direct perception.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage defines the two means of attaining knowledge. Maturidi states that God made the causes by which one reaches the origin (الأصل) which is knowledge. He made these causes two types: the apparent (الظاهر البين) and the hidden (الخفى المستور), so that people of intellect may excel according to their effort and endurance. Then He made His way two divisions: one is direct perception (العيان) which is the most specific cause and involves no ignorance, serving as the foundation for what is hidden. The second is revelation (السمع), whose truthfulness is known through the evidence of direct perception.

Correction: Maturidi states that revelation's truth is known through the evidence of direct perception, establishing a dependency.

A) It is the basis for verifying the truthfulness of revelation
B) It is irrelevant
C) It is superior to revelation
D) It is only for worldly matters

Key Takeaway
The two means of knowledge are direct perception and revelation, with direct perception serving as the foundation and criterion for verifying revelation.

Maturidi says revelation’s truth is known through direct perception. Have you ever tested a religious claim against your own intellect and senses? If not, what fear holds you back from using the very tool (direct perception) that Allah made the foundation of knowledge?

Maturidi's Two Paths to Knowledge: Direct Perception and Revelation
Maturidi divides knowledge into direct perception and revelation, then subdivides revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's classification of the two fundamental paths to knowledge—direct perception and revelation—and how revelation is further divided into clear and ambiguous categories for theological methodology.

Core Idea
Knowledge comes through direct perception as the foundation and revelation as its guided extension.

Imam Maturidi begins his epistemological framework by identifying two primary ways humans come to know religious truths: direct sensory observation and divine revelation. He then refines revelation into categories that guide how we interpret scripture. This passage establishes the methodological principles that shape Maturidi's approach to theology and divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once without translation to absorb Maturidi's concise style. Then read the literal translation, noting how the key terms (العيان, السمع, محكم, متشابه, مفسر, مبهم) are used.
Pay attention to the phrase 'وعلى ذلك جعل سبيله قسمين' which sets up the twofold division.
2
Identify the two main paths
The passage states two parts: direct observation ('iyan) and revelation (sam'). Notice how 'iyan is described as the most specific means without ignorance, serving as a foundation for what is hidden.
Maturidi emphasizes the certainty of direct perception before moving to revelation.
3
Understand the subdivision of revelation
Revelation is further divided into two pairs: muhkam/mutashabih (clear/ambiguous) and mufassar/mubham (interpreted/obscure). This classification helps determine how to handle ambiguous verses.
Consider how this relates to the broader Islamic hermeneutical tradition.
4
Analyze the purpose of these divisions
Maturidi explains that these categories clarify the limits of knowledge—when to refrain and when to act. He also introduces the concept of divine testing through both submission and inquiry.
The phrase 'محنة الوقوف' refers to a trial of pausing when knowledge is not attainable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى ذلك جعل سبيله قسمين. أحدهما العيان الذي هو أخص الأسباب، وهو الذي ليس معه جهل، ليكون أصلاً لما خفى منه. والثاني السمع الذي عن دلالة الأعيان يعرف صدقه وكذبه. ثم جعل السمع قسمين: محكم ومتشابه ومفسر ومبهم، ليبين منتهى المعارف من الكف فيما يجب ذلك والإقدام فيما يلزمه. ومن حمل المبهم على المفسر ولزوم المحكم وعرض المتشابه عليه ما أمكن أن يكون ما فيه مما يلزم تعرفه ومما إليه حاجة بأهل المحنة، أو ترك الخوض في ذلك فيما أمكن الغناء عن تعرف حقيقة ما فيه، فيكون محنة الوقوف؛ إذ الله تعالى أن يمتحن بوجهين بالتسليم مرة وبالطلب ثانيا، وإنما على العبد الطاعة في قدر الأمر.
And upon that, He made its path two parts: one of them is direct observation, which is the most specific of means and is that with which there is no ignorance, to be a foundation for what is hidden from it. And the second is revelation, which through the evidence of observed things its truth and falsehood are known. Then He made revelation two parts: muhkam and mutashabih, and mufassar and mubham, to clarify the utmost of cognitions regarding refraining where that is obligatory and undertaking what is necessary. And whoever carries the ambiguous upon the interpreted, and adheres to the muhkam, and presents the mutashabih to it as much as possible, then what is in it includes what is necessary to know and what the people of trial have need of, or refraining from delving into that where it is possible to be free of needing to know its reality, so that it becomes a trial of standing still; for God Almighty tests in two ways: by surrender once and by seeking another time, and upon the servant is obedience to the extent of the command.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage defines the two fundamental paths to knowledge in Maturidi's epistemology: direct perception ('iyan) and revelation (sam'). Direct perception is the most basic and certain means, serving as the foundation for hidden knowledge. Revelation is validated by empirical evidence. Then revelation is subdivided into clear/unambiguous (muhkam) and ambiguous (mutashabih), and explicit (mufassar) and obscure (mubham). This division serves to guide human effort: one must interpret the ambiguous in light of the clear, and refrain when no resolution is possible, accepting that God tests humans through both submission and inquiry.

Key Terms
العيان
السمع
محكم
متشابه
مفسر
مبهم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that revelation is independent of empirical evidence and can be understood without reference to observed reality.
Correction: Maturidi explicitly states that the truth and falsehood of revelation are known 'through the evidence of observed things' ('an dalalat al-a'yan), showing that revelation is validated by empirical knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the role of direct perception ('iyan) in relation to revelation?
A) It is equal to revelation.
B) It is the foundation for understanding hidden knowledge.
C) It is only for sensory experience.
D) It is not necessary for faith.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi establishes that knowledge is rooted in direct perception and revelation, with revelation further categorized to guide interpretation, reflecting a balanced methodology of reason, textual clarity, and humble submission when ambiguity remains.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a Quranic verse that is difficult to understand, do you immediately try to impose your own interpretation, or do you first compare it with clear verses (muhkam) and seek guidance from scholars? Which of God's two tests—surrender to ambiguity or active seeking—do you find harder in your spiritual life, and why?
?
Maturidi says that direct perception ('iyan) is the foundation of knowledge. How much time do you actually spend contemplating the natural world as evidence of God, rather than relying solely on what you've been taught? What concrete step will you take this week to increase your reflection on God's signs in creation?
?
Are there matters of faith that you have neglected to investigate because you assumed they are too ambiguous or beyond your capacity? Could that avoidance be a form of disobedience rather than a virtuous surrender? Identify one such issue and commit to studying it with the help of clear texts and reliable teachers.

Why Analogy Requires God's Creation of Actions
Maturidi deploys a dilemma argument about qiyas to prove creation of actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma that Maturidi uses to prove that qiyas (analogical reasoning) itself necessitates belief in God's creation of human actions.

Core Idea
Without analogy, knowledge of God is impossible; with it, we see actions are created.

Imam Maturidi presents a concise but powerful argument that forces the opponent into a dilemma. Either you accept analogical reasoning or you do not. If you reject it, you lose any rational basis for knowing God. If you accept it, consistency demands that you also accept that God creates human actions. This short passage from Kitab al-Tawhid shows how foundational qiyas is to Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage slowly, then the literal translation. Let the words of Imam Maturidi sink in before you analyze them.
Notice the phrase 'مما لا يخلو من أن يكون مستعملاً' which sets up a dilemma.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
Find the two conditional statements: 'if it is not used' and the implied 'if it is used'. Maturidi shows the consequence of each branch.
The first branch leads to invalidity of the opponent's knowledge of the Maker.
3
Understand the analogical inference
Maturidi then describes how if we do use qiyas, we find that the same meanings (like contingency) present in the world are also present in human actions. This leads to the conclusion that actions are created by God.
The key is 'جميع المعاني التي هي للعالم بأعراضه موجودة في أفعال الخلق'.
4
Reflect on the consequence of rejecting the conclusion
If one denies that actions are created, then knowledge of creation becomes reliant only on revelation, which defeats the purpose of rational proof.
This is the force of the argument: denial undermines both reason and revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً إن القياس مما لا يخلو من أن يكون مستعملاً فيما نحن فيه أو لا؛ فإن كان لا يستعمل بطل مذهب الخصوم في معرفة الصانع، لارتفاع الحواس عنه، فيجب معرفته بذلك، وهو على الاستدلال بالشاهد ثم نجد جميع المعاني التي هي للعالم بأعراضه موجودة في أفعال الخلق. فلئن لم يجب القول بخلقها لم يجز معرفة خلق ألبتة إلا بالسمع، فيجب به.
And also, indeed analogy (al-qiyas) cannot be free from being used in what we are in or not; if it is not used, then the doctrine of the opponents in knowing the Maker becomes invalid, because the senses are removed from Him, so it is necessary to know Him by that, and it is by inference from the witnessed. Then we find all the meanings which are in the world through its accidents exist in the actions of the creation. So if the statement of their creation is not obligatory, then knowledge of creation would not be allowed at all except by hearing, so it is necessary by it.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage presents an argument based on qiyas (analogical reasoning). Maturidi states that qiyas must either be used in this matter or not. If not used, then the opponents' position becomes invalid because God cannot be known through the senses; rational inference is required. If qiyas is used, then we observe that all the meanings (e.g., contingency) found in the world, including its accidents, are also found in human actions. Therefore, by analogy, just as the world must have a creator, human actions must also be created by God. If one denies that actions are created, then knowledge of creation would be limited to revelation (sam‘) alone, which defeats the purpose of rational proof.

Key Terms
القياس
استدلال
شاهد
خلق الأفعال
سمع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some argue that qiyas is not applicable to God or that it is unnecessary for knowing Him.
Correction: Maturidi shows that without qiyas, rational knowledge of God becomes impossible because God is beyond sensory perception. Thus, qiyas is indispensable.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say would happen if qiyas is not used in proving God's creation of actions?
A) The opponents' doctrine in knowing the Maker would be invalid.
B) Knowledge through senses would suffice.
C) Revelation alone would be sufficient.
D) Qiyas is optional and not necessary.

Key Takeaway
The use of analogical reasoning (qiyas) forces one to accept that God creates human actions, as the same signs of contingency are present in both the world and actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every action you take, including this very thought, is created by Allah? If so, how does that change the way you view your own choices and responsibilities?
?
If you were to reject the use of analogical reasoning in this matter, would you still have a rational basis for believing in God? Examine your own faith: is it built on reflection or on imitation?
?
The argument forces you to choose: either accept that God creates your actions, or lose the rational foundation for knowing Him. Which horn are you willing to embrace, and what does that say about your intellectual honesty?

Maturidi's Approach to Refuting the Mu'tazila
Maturidi introduces his refutation of the Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's methodological critique of the Mu'tazila and his intention to refute them using both rational inquiry and scriptural evidence.

Core Idea
Maturidi exposes the Mu'tazila's arrogance and their distance from true kalām.

Maturidi begins his refutation by exposing the Mu'tazila's self-deception. They imagine themselves the champions of kalām, but in reality, they are far from even being ordinary practitioners. This passage sets the stage for a systematic refutation grounded in correct reasoning and careful interpretation of the Qur'an.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm and tone come through before you interpret.
Notice Maturidi's strong rhetorical style.
2
Identify the key accusations
Maturidi accuses the Mu'tazila of arrogance and self-deception. Find phrases that show their false claim to being masters of kalām and their distance from even basic competence.
3
Understand Maturidi's twofold approach
Maturidi plans to expose their deviation in rational method (حقيقة النظر) and clarify the verses they have misinterpreted. This shows his method combines reason and revelation.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نذكر ما تعلق به هذه الفرقة التي ظنت أنهم فرسان الكلام، وأنهم المخصوصون في العلم به من بين الأنام؛ ليعلموا بذلك جرأتهم في الدعوى وبعدهم عند التحصيل عن احتمال اسم عوام أهله، فضلا عن مجاورة خطاء حذاقهم. ونظهر إن شاء الله تعالى لمن تأمل ما ذكرت عدولهم عما توجبه حقيقة النظر، ونبين ما استتروا به من الآيات، ليعلم أنهم لو وقفوا على طرف منها لنالوا خير الدارين، فضلا من أن يظفروا بحقيقتها. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then we will mention what this sect clung to, who thought that they are the horsemen of kalām, and that they are the ones specially distinguished in knowledge of it among people; so that they might know thereby their audacity in their claim and their distance, upon investigation, from bearing the name of the common people of its people, let alone surpassing the errors of their experts. And we will show, God willing, to whoever contemplates what I have mentioned, their deviation from what the reality of investigation requires, and we will clarify what they concealed of the verses, so that it may be known that if they had come across a portion of them, they would have attained the good of both abodes, let alone attaining their reality.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage is an introduction by Maturidi to his refutation of the Muʿtazila. He begins by stating his intention to recount the arguments of this sect, who falsely imagine themselves to be the champions of kalām and the experts in it. He aims to expose their audacity and how far they are from even being considered ordinary practitioners of kalām, let alone surpassing the errors of their own experts (literally 'the errors of their knowledgeable ones'). He then declares that by the will of God, he will show their deviation from proper reasoning and uncover the verses of the Qur'an that they have concealed or misinterpreted. His goal is to demonstrate that if they had understood even a portion of these verses, they would have achieved success in this world and the next, let alone grasping their true meaning.

حقيقة النظر
آيات

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Mu'tazila are the true experts in kalām.
Correction: Maturidi argues they are far from even being ordinary practitioners, let alone experts.

The Consensus of Muslims as Sufficient Proof
Maturidi states that transmitted Muslim consensus is sufficient proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the transmitted consensus of Muslims that Allah is Creator and everything else is created serves as a sufficient proof for those who reason fairly.

Core Idea
The collective testimony of Muslims is a sufficient proof for the oneness of Allah.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi asserts that the sum of what Muslims have transmitted about Allah's attributes—that He is Creator, Almighty, Lord, and God—constitutes a sufficient proof. He emphasizes that this proof is clear for anyone who reasons with fairness, without any disturbance or bias. Maturidi also hints at further elaboration to come.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, noticing Maturidi's confident tone.
2
Identify the core claim
What does Maturidi say is a sufficient proof?
Look for the phrase 'daliil kaaf'.
3
Reflect on the conditions
He mentions 'for those who reason fairly' and 'without disturbance or inclination.' What do these conditions imply?
This proof does not depend on personal bias.
4
Notice Maturidi's methodology
He uses transmitted consensus as a foundation.
This is a reliance on naql (transmitted knowledge).
5
Anticipate further details
He promises to mention more later.
This suggests the discussion will continue.
