Refuting the Argument that the Prophet's Era Sets a Precedent for All Matters
Maturidi refutes the claim that absence of a practice in the Prophet's time prohibits later revelation or rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of the claim that the absence of a practice during the Prophet's time makes it invalid, and why this argument is self-defeating.

Core Idea
If absence in the Prophet's era invalidated later rulings, most of the Quran would be invalid.

Maturidi here addresses an opponent who argues that since something was not present in the time of the Prophet, it cannot be valid. He shows that this implies clarification cannot come before an event, and that revelation cannot address issues not yet disputed—which would undermine the Quran itself. This passage is a powerful critique of a rigid methodological principle.

Guided Path
1
Understand the opponent's claim
The opponent says 'It was not so in the time of the Messenger of God,' meaning that the absence of a practice in that era is a proof against its validity.
This is a common argument used by those who reject later theological developments.
2
Identify Maturidi's logical implication
Maturidi points out that this claim implies two things: that clarification (bayān) cannot come before an event occurs, and that revelation cannot address matters that were not preceded by dispute.
This exposes the hidden assumptions in the opponent's argument.
3
See the contradiction with the Quran
If those assumptions were true, then most of the Quran would be invalid, because many verses were revealed about future events or after disputes arose.
This is a powerful reductio ad absurdum.
4
Grasp the rebuttal about condemnation
Maturidi adds that if a verse were to be revealed against the opponents, it would condemn them and describe what they deny about God, so that verse would be binding on them—even though they do not act on it.
This highlights the hypocrisy of using selective precedent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله: لم يكن في عهد رسول الله كذا، فكأنه قال: لا يجوز ورود البيان في الشيء قبل وقوعه، وأن البيان لا يَرِد فيما لم يسبق فيه التنازع. وذلك يدفع جميع آيات القرآن وما عليه الأمر المعتاد. وبعد، فإن الآية لو نزلت فيهم النزلت في ذمهم، ووصفت فيما نفوا عن الله. فذلك لازم لهم. مع أن الآية لا يعمل بها
And his saying: 'It was not so in the time of the Messenger of God,' it is as if he said: 'It is not permissible for clarification to come concerning a thing before its occurrence, and that clarification does not come concerning that which was not preceded by dispute.' And that negates all the verses of the Quran and what is customary. Moreover, if the verse had been revealed against them, it would have been revealed in condemnation of them, and described what they negated about God. So that is necessary for them. Even though the verse is not acted upon.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage refutes an opponent who argues that since something (probably a theological point) was not present in the time of the Prophet, it cannot be valid. Maturidi says this implies that clarification (bayān) cannot precede the event, and that revelation cannot occur unless there is prior dispute—which would invalidate most of the Quran, as many verses address things that had not yet happened. Furthermore, he argues that if a verse were to be revealed against the Mu'tazila (the opponent), it would condemn them and describe what they deny about God, so that verse would be binding on them—even though they do not act on it. The point is that the opponent's principle is self-defeating because it would undermine their own reliance on Quranic verses that were revealed after disputes arose, and also would render irrelevant verses that they themselves use.

Key Terms
عهد رسول الله
بيان
تنازع
آيات
ذم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only what was present in the Prophet's time is valid for all time.
Correction: Maturidi shows this would invalidate much of the Quran, proving that later clarifications and rulings are permissible.

Checkpoint
What is Maturidi's main objection to the argument that a ruling is invalid because it wasn't present in the Prophet's time?
A) He says the Prophet's era is irrelevant
B) He argues that this would invalidate most of the Quran, which was revealed to address new situations
C) He claims the opponents themselves invented the argument
D) He says the Prophet actually did address everything

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that the argument from absence in the Prophet's time would invalidate much of the Quran, thus it is self-defeating.

Defending the Apparent Meaning of the Verse on Human Action
Maturidi refutes an opponent's interpretation of Q. 37:96 and defends the apparent meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's method of defending the apparent meaning (zahir) of the Qur'an against interpretive diversion, specifically regarding the verse 'Allah created you and what you do.'

Core Idea
The apparent meaning of the Qur'an cannot be diverted without evidence.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi addresses an opponent who tries to interpret the verse 'Allah created you and what you do' (Q. 37:96) as referring to idols rather than human actions. Maturidi demonstrates a key principle of Sunni hermeneutics: the apparent meaning of the Qur'an stands unless compelling evidence forces a reinterpretation. He also exposes the opponent's inconsistency in applying general statements selectively, using a vivid analogy.

Guided Path
1
Understand the opponent's deviation
Notice that the opponent avoids the direct question about the ruling of the verse and instead shifts focus to the type of acts. Maturidi points out this evasion.
The opponent is likely Abu'l-Qasim al-Ka'bi, a Mu'tazili.
2
Examine the analogy of inconsistency
Maturidi compares the opponent to someone who says 'God is in every place' but denies it in filthy places. This shows how the opponent uses absolute statements selectively.
This illustrates a methodological flaw: applying a general principle only where convenient.
3
Identify the opponent's proof
The opponent cites Q. 37:96 and interprets 'what you do' as referring to their carved idols, using other verses as parallels.
The opponent's interpretation seeks to remove human actions from divine creation.
4
Analyze Maturidi's refutation
Maturidi insists that the apparent meaning of the verse is the creation of action, and it cannot be diverted to something else without clear evidence. He further argues that the blame upon the idolaters is for their actions (carving and worshipping), not just the idols themselves.
This reinforces the doctrine that human actions are created by Allah.
5
Note the incomplete conclusion
The passage ends mid-sentence, but the core argument is complete enough to understand Maturidi's position. The missing part likely continues the refutation.
The source notes that the text is incomplete.

ثم من حيده أن سأل عن حكم الآية، فأعرض عن ذلك واشتغل في الإجابة عن نوع الأفعال. وحقيقته أن يقول به في الجملة، وعند التفسير فيما يقبح لا يقول؛ كما يقول: الله في كل مكان؛ فإذا سئل عنه في الحشوش والأمكنة القذرة أبي ذلك. ثم هو رب كل شيء وإله كل شيء، ثم عند التفسير فيما يقبح يأبى، إلا أنه لما يقصر بحيث ذلك الإطلاق يدفع أصله، فمثله ما نحن فيه. والله الموفق. ثم احتج لخصمه بقوله: والله خلقكم وما تعملون، قال: يريد به آلهتم كقوله: أتعبدون ما تنحتون، وكقوله: تلقف ما يأفكون. قال الشيخ رحمه الله: نقول وبالله التوفيق: ظاهر الآية ذكر خلق العمل، فلم يجز صرف ذلك إلى غيره إلا بالبيان. مع ما في جميع ما ذكر نحتهم داخل، وكذلك إفكهم كما ذكر، وبه عوبوا لا بذلك الشيء فقط، حيث فعلوا ثم عبدوا، فكأنهم عبدوا فعلهم، فمثله ما نحن فيه. وأيضا إنه لو صرح بالآية الهتهم بعد أن ذكر معمولا، فإذا لم يكن
Then from his deviation, he asked about the ruling of the verse, but he turned away from that and engaged in answering about the type of acts. The reality is that he says it in general, but when interpreting regarding what is ugly, he does not say it; just as he says: God is in every place; but when asked about him in privies and filthy places, he denies that. Then He is the Lord of everything and the God of everything, then when interpreting regarding what is ugly, he refuses, except that when it comes down to that, that absolute expression contradicts its principle. So the like of that is our case. And God is the granter of success. Then he argued for his opponent with His statement: 'And Allah created you and what you do' (Q. 37:96). He said: He means by it their gods, like His statement: 'Do you worship what you carve?' (Q. 37:95), and His statement: 'It swallows what they faked.' The Shaykh (may Allah have mercy on him) said: We say, and with God is success: The apparent meaning of the verse mentions the creation of action, so it is not permissible to divert that to other than it except with evidence. Also, in all that he mentioned, their carving is included, and likewise their faking as he mentioned, and they are blamed not only for that thing, for they did and then worshipped, so it is as if they worshipped their own action. So the like of that is our case. Also, if the verse explicitly referred to their gods after mentioning action, then if it was not
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection from an opponent (probably Abu'l-Qasim al-Ka'bi). The opponent avoids the direct question about the verse and focuses on types of acts. Maturidi points out that the opponent uses an absolute statement (e.g., 'God is in every place') but refuses to apply it to dirty places. Similarly, they acknowledge God as Lord of everything but refuse to attribute evil to Him in detail. The opponent then cites the verse 'Allah created you and what you do' and interprets 'what you do' as referring to their carved idols. Maturidi refutes this by stating that the apparent meaning of the verse is the creation of action, and that cannot be diverted without evidence. He further argues that the blame upon the idolaters is for their actions of carving and worshipping, not just the idols themselves. Thus, the verse stands as evidence for the creation of human actions.

Correction: Maturidi argues that the apparent meaning is the creation of action, and it cannot be diverted without evidence. He further shows that the blame upon the idolaters is for their actions, implying that actions are created.

D) He remains silent on the matter

Key Takeaway

Maturidi shows the opponent's inconsistency: saying God is everywhere but not in dirty places. Where in your own life do you claim to believe something about Allah's attributes (like His all-encompassing mercy or justice) but then deny it in specific situations, especially those involving your own sin or someone else's? ?

The Sufficiency of the Proof of Temporality
Maturidi explains the methodological focus of Ahl al-Tawhid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ahl al-Tawhid focus on the temporal origination of the world as a sufficient proof for the existence of the Creator.

Core Idea
One proof is enough: the world's temporality proves its Creator.

Maturidi begins this passage by clarifying the unique methodology of Ahl al-Tawhid. They do not need separate arguments for the world's creation and the Creator; they consider the proof of temporality to encompass both. This introduction sets the stage for why their argument is efficient and convincing.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation to absorb the author's own words.
Notice the contrast between 'حدث العالم' (temporal origination) and 'خلق العالم' (creation).
2
Identify the focus of Ahl al-Tawhid
The text says they spoke about the temporal origination of the world and the establishment of its Creator. Mark the key terms.
They did not trouble themselves with a separate argument for creation.
3
Understand the sufficiency argument
They saw the first as sufficient over the second. Reflect on why establishing temporality implies a Creator.
The logic: if the world is temporal, it must have a creator.
4
Consider the counterfactual
The text says if they didn't see sufficiency, they would have made another argument. Realize that would be problematic.
The phrase 'لكل إليه حاجة' suggests each part would be dependent, leading to impossibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الفقيه رحمه الله : نقول وبالله التوفيق: أهل التوحيد إنما تكلموا في حدث العالم وثبات محدثه، ولا أحد تكلف القول بخلق العالم وثبات خالقه. فلولا أنهم رأوا بالأول كفاية عن الثاني، وجعلوا ثبات الحدث دليلاً مقنعا في الخلق، لصنعوا مثله؛ لأن لكل إليه حاجة، وذلك ممتنع . وقد احتج بخلق القرآن بالتبعض والتجزئة، فمثله في كل الأعراض قائم، فيلزم القول به. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
The faqih, may God have mercy on him, said: We say, and with God is success: The people of tawhid only spoke about the temporal origination of the world and the establishment of its Creator, and no one troubled himself to speak about the creation of the world and the establishment of its Creator. Were it not that they saw in the first sufficiency over the second, and made the establishment of temporality a convincing proof of creation, they would have done the like; for each has a need, and that is impossible. And he argued by the creation of the Qur'an through division and partition, and its like exists in all accidents, so it necessitates affirming it. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
Maturidi, through the voice of 'al-faqih', clarifies the methodology of Ahl al-Tawhid. They speak about the temporal origination (ḥudūth) of the world and the existence (thubūt) of its Creator, not about the creation of the world and the existence of the Creator separately. They consider the proof of temporality sufficient to prove the Creator, so there is no need for a separate argument. He then mentions an argument about the creation of the Qur'an: if one argues that the Qur'an is created because it consists of parts (taḥalṣīṣ) and divisions, then the same logic applies to all accidents, leading to the conclusion that all accidents are created. This reminds of the kalām argument from accidents. The passage ends with a formula of reliance on God's power.

Key Terms
أهل التوحيد
حدث العالم
ثبات محدثه
كفاية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One must prove the world's creation and the Creator's existence separately.
Correction: The proof of temporality suffices; it simultaneously proves the world had a beginning and therefore a Creator.

Checkpoint
What do Ahl al-Tawhid primarily speak about according to the passage?
A) The creation of the world and the existence of the Creator
B) The temporal origination of the world and the establishment of its Creator
C) The attributes of God alone
D) The creation of the Qur'an

Key Takeaway
Ahl al-Tawhid efficiently prove the Creator through the world's temporality without needing separate arguments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you satisfied with the single proof of temporality, or do you find yourself seeking multiple evidences before your heart settles? What does that say about your trust in God's signs?
?
If you were to present the proof of God's existence to someone, would you rely on the temporality argument alone, or would you add others? Why might you feel the need to add?
?
The passage warns against unnecessary multiplication of proofs because 'each has a need.' Where in your own life do you overcomplicate matters, adding more when one clear truth is enough? Will you commit to simplicity and sufficiency in your faith?

The Argument from Division: Creation of the Qur'an
Maturidi presents a proof for creation of the Qur'an via division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the concept of division and partition to argue for the creation of the Qur'an and its implication for all accidents.

Core Idea
Whatever consists of parts is created—so the Qur'an, like all accidents, is created.

Maturidi then presents a specific argument regarding the creation of the Qur'an. He points out that if someone argues the Qur'an is created because it has parts (letters, words), then the same logic applies to all accidents (ʿaraḍ). This reinforces the kalām position that everything temporal is created.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic and its translation. Focus on the key terms 'تبعض' and 'تجزئة'.
These terms denote division into parts and partition.
2
Identify the argument
The argument is: the Qur'an is created because of division and partition. Understand this reasoning.
Anything composite is temporal and thus created.
3
Note the generalization
The text says 'its like exists in all accidents.' This implies the argument applies to all accidents, not just the Qur'an.
This shows consistency in the kalām view of accidents.
4
Conclude the necessity
The conclusion: 'it necessitates affirming it.' Affirming the createdness of the Qur'an follows necessarily.
The final phrase is a formula of reliance on God.

Were it not that they saw in the first sufficiency over the second, and made the establishment of temporality a convincing proof of creation, they would have done the like; for each has a need, and that is impossible. How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

How does the temporality of all that you are and have humble your standing before the Eternal? Will you today view your own words and actions as fleeting signs that point to the Creator?

Knowledge of God Requires Ijtihad, Not Compulsion
Maturidi's second argument: knowledge of God is through ijtihad, not compulsion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's second argument against compulsion: that knowledge of God and faith require ijtihad and inference, and cannot be compelled without undermining faith and obedience.

Core Idea
Faith is a choice, not a force—it requires reasoning and effort.

Maturidi continues his refutation of compulsion by focusing on how we come to know God. He argues that the very nature of faith and knowledge of God requires personal effort and reasoning, not compulsion. This passage uses a logical argument to show that compulsion would nullify the meaning of obedience and even undermine our knowledge of the observable world.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice Maturidi's dense logical style.
2
Identify Maturidi's key claims
Find the statement that knowledge is through ijtihad and inference, the conditional argument about compulsory knowledge, and the point about obedience.
These form the core of the argument.
3
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself why compulsion would invalidate faith as obedience. Consider how Maturidi uses the verse to reinforce his point.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني أن طريق معرفة وحدانية الله والإيمان به وبرسله طريق الاجتهاد والاستدلال وذلك نوع ما لا يحتمل الاضطرار. ولو جاز علم الاضطرار فيما ليس في الخلقة احتماله لجاز نفي علم الاضطرار فيما كان ذلك طريقه، فيبطل علم العيان. مع ما كان ذلك كله طاعة وائتمارًا، والجبر يسقط ذلك كله، فيصير في التحصيل كأنه قال: «لو شاء لمنعكم عن الإيمان ملة واحدة». وهذا خلف من القول، وإنما أخبر أنه لو شاء لجمعكم على الهدى، وقد آمن بعضهم بالاختيار، ولو كان ثمة جبر البقية لم يكن ليجمعهم، ولكن يمنعهم عما أبوا به من دينه وطاعته، وذلك بعيد وحش.
And the second: that the path to knowing the oneness of God and faith in Him and His messengers is the path of ijtihad and inference, and that is a type that does not admit of compulsion. And if compulsory knowledge were permissible in what is not in the nature to accept it, then it would be permissible to negate compulsory knowledge in what is its path, and thus knowledge by direct observation would be invalid. Moreover, all that is obedience and following command, and compulsion negates all that, so it becomes in effect as if He said: 'If He willed, He would prevent you from faith by one nation.' This is a false statement. Rather, He informed that if He willed, He would gather you upon guidance, and some have believed by choice. And if there were compulsion for the rest, He would not gather them, but would prevent them from what they rejected of His religion and obedience. And that is far-fetched and ugly.
Source pages p. 375

Commentary
This passage contains the second aspect of the refutation of compulsion. Maturidi states that the path to knowing God's oneness and faith is through ijtihad (independent reasoning) and istidlal (inference), which by nature cannot be accomplished by compulsion. If compulsory knowledge were possible in matters that require effort, then one could deny compulsory knowledge in matters that are known directly, thereby undermining knowledge from direct observation. Furthermore, all acts of faith are acts of obedience and following command; compulsion (jabr) would nullify this entire category, reducing God's statement 'if He willed' to a meaningless threat. The verse actually indicates that God would gather people to guidance if He willed, but some have believed by choice; if others were compelled, they would not be 'gathered' but rather prevented, which contradicts the verse.

Key Terms
الاجتهاد
الاستدلال
الاضطرار
الجبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That faith can be compelled by God or others
Correction: Faith requires ijtihad and choice, so it cannot be compelled; compulsion would nullify obedience and undermine knowledge.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi say that faith cannot be by compulsion?
A) Because God is not powerful enough
B) Because faith requires ijtihad and inference, which are not compulsory
C) Because the Quran forbids compulsion
D) Because all knowledge is innate

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's second argument shows that compulsion contradicts the nature of faith as a voluntary act of reasoning and obedience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you pressured yourself or others into religious acts out of guilt or fear rather than conscious choice? How does Maturidi's argument challenge you to rethink that approach? What concrete step can you take this week to replace compulsion with reasoned faith in your own practice?
?
Think of someone you know who has distanced themselves from Islam. Could your own attempts to 'compel' them back—through criticism, guilt trips, or ultimatums—have actually pushed them further? How can you instead honor their God-given capacity for choice while still inviting them with wisdom?
?
Maturidi says compulsion would negate obedience. In what areas of your life have you been 'going through the motions' without the ijtihad of the heart? What would it mean for you to truly choose your faith again—not as habit, but as a conscious act of reasoning and love?

The Revealed Basis of Faith
Maturidi reports the opponent's argument on the revelatory basis of the name of iman.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the methodological argument that the Murji'ah and Khawarij agreed the name of iman is based on revelation, not linguistic analogy.

Core Idea
The name of faith is revealed, not reasoned.

In studying the theological debate about faith and sin, one encounters a crucial methodological question: How do we know who is a believer? The opponent argues that even the Murji'ah and Khawarij—often disagreeing on many points—agree that the designation of 'iman' comes from revelation (sam'), not from human reasoning. This passage presents that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the structure of the argument become clear before dissecting it.
2
Identify the key claim
Notice that the opponent begins by noting an agreement between the Murji'ah and Khawarij. This is rhetorically powerful—two opposing sects agree on this point.
3
Understand the methodological principle
The passage asserts that the name of iman is not derived by qiyas (analogical reasoning) from language, but rather from sam' (revelation). Grasp what this means for theological method.
Qiyas here refers to linguistic analogy, not juristic analogy.
4
Examine the conclusion and claim
The opponent concludes that no one can be named a believer or disbeliever without evidence from tawatur (widespread transmission) or ijma' (consensus). He claims this is sufficient proof for his position.
Consider why this might not be sufficient—what about explicit Quranic verses on hypocrites?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم احتج في نفي اسم الكفر والإيمان عن صاحب الكبيرة أن المرجئة والخوارج اتفقوا على أن اسم الإيمان لا يوجد بالقياس مما أوجبته اللغة وإنما كان من جهة السمع، فلا تجوز التسمية بواحد إلا بالتواتر بالسمع أو الإجماع، وزعم أن هذه حجة كافية.
Then he argued in negating the name of kufr and iman from the major sinner that the Murji'ah and the Khawarij agreed that the name of iman is not established by qiyas from what language necessitated, but rather it is from the side of revelation. So it is not permissible to name anyone except by widespread transmission of revelation or consensus. And he claimed that this is a sufficient proof.
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage presents an argument from the opponent (likely al-Ka'bi) that both the Murji'ah and the Khawarij agree on a key methodological principle: the name of iman (faith) is not derived by qiyas (analogical reasoning) based on linguistic principles, but rather it is established only through sam' (revelation). Consequently, one cannot label a person as a believer or disbeliever unless there is explicit textual evidence from revelation transmitted widely (tawatur) or by consensus (ijma') of the community. The opponent uses this agreement to argue that since there is no explicit text labeling the major sinner (sahib al-kabira) as either a kafir or a mu'min, he falls into an intermediate category. Maturidi here is likely reporting this argument for the purpose of refuting it elsewhere, though the immediate text does not include his response. The passage is important for understanding the methodological debate about the basis of religious naming.

Key Terms
إيمان
قياس
سمع
تواتر
إجماع
مرجئة
خوارج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The agreement between Murji'ah and Khawarij implies they have the same definition of iman.
Correction: The text only says they agree on the methodological basis (the name of iman is from revelation), not on the definition or scope of iman.

Checkpoint
According to the reported agreement between the Murji'ah and the Khawarij, what is the basis for the name of iman?
A) Linguistic qiyas (analogy).
B) Language alone.
C) Revelation (sam') and consensus (ijma').
D) Reason and logic.

Key Takeaway
The opponent argues that the name of iman is determined solely by revelation and consensus, not by analogy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you judge someone's faith, do you rely on what Allah has revealed or on your own reasoning? Recall a specific instance where you labeled someone's iman based on personal opinion rather than a clear text. Will you change that judgment now?
?
If the name of iman is only from revelation, what does that expose about how you understand your own faith? In what area of your life have you let culture or personal preference define what it means to be a believer instead of the Qur'an and Sunnah?
?
You have learned that both the Murji'ah and Khawarij agree on this principle. How does their agreement challenge your tendency to dismiss others' views in religious matters? Are you willing to accept a truth even if it comes from those you disagree with? What would that require of you tomorrow?

Method of Learning
intermediate
6 min
Obedience Is by Command, Not Delight
Maturidi's first aspect: obedience is by command, not delight

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi distinguishes obedience as acting upon a command, not merely causing pleasure or delight, and how this refutes the notion that sinners obey Satan.

Core Idea
Obedience is acting on command, not causing delight.

Maturidi begins his multi-aspect response to a question about obedience by clarifying a fundamental principle: obedience is defined by the act of following a command, not by the emotional satisfaction it produces. This distinction is crucial because even Satan can delight in sin, but that does not make the sin an act of obedience to him. Let's explore this first aspect carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then its translation. Notice that Maturidi states there are several aspects and then gives the first one. Pay attention to the key terms: طاعة (obedience), أمر (command), شيطان (Satan), لذة (pleasure).
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the main argument
Maturidi argues that the sinner's action does not constitute obedience to Satan, even though Satan delights in it. Find the sentence that defines obedience and see how it supports this claim.
The definition is: 'والطاعة هي التي تؤدى على الأمر' (obedience is that which is performed upon command).
3
Analyze the second point
Maturidi adds a second point: human desires are gifts from Allah, and it is impossible for Allah to obey humans. Why does he mention this? How does it strengthen the overall argument?
This point eliminates the possibility that obedience could be defined by satisfying someone's desires.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that this aspect is not the way to know obedience. Ask yourself: what would be the correct way? This prepares us for the subsequent aspects in the original text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول في ذلك بوجوه. أحدها أن ليس في ذلك طاعة للشيطان وإن كان هو يسر به ويتلذذ لشؤم طبعه وسوء اختياره، إذ لم يكن الذي يتعاطاه يفعله لأمره ودعائه إليه، والطاعة هي التي تؤدى على الأمر لا على ما يسر ويتلذذ ؛ لأن للعباد، فيما أعطاهم الله الشهوات، لذات وسرورا، ومحال وصف الله بالطاعة لهم، أو يمكن الأمر منهم إياه بالفعل. دل أن ليس ذلك الوجه هو سبيل معرفة الطاعة. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Then we say concerning that with several aspects. One of them is that there is not in that any obedience to Satan, even though he delights in it and enjoys it due to the ill omen of his nature and his bad choice, because the one who engages in it does not do it for his command and his call, and obedience is that which is performed upon command, not upon what is delighted in and enjoyed. Because the servants have, in what Allah has given them of desires, pleasures and joys, and it is impossible to describe Allah with obedience to them, or that they command Him with an action. This indicates that that aspect is not the way of knowing obedience. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 465

Commentary
This passage opens Maturidi's multi-aspect response. The first aspect distinguishes between obedience (ta'a) as acting on a command and mere satisfaction Satan derives from sin. The sinner does not act on Satan's command, so it is not obedience. Moreover, human desires are gifts from Allah, and it is absurd to say Allah obeys humans. Therefore, delight alone does not make something obedience.

Key Terms
طاعة (obedience)
أمر (command)
شيطان (Satan)
لذة (pleasure)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if an action brings delight to Satan, it must be obedience to Satan.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that obedience is defined by acting on a command, not by the delight it causes. Satan's delight is incidental, not the defining factor.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi's first aspect, what defines obedience?
A) The outcome of the action
B) The pleasure it gives to the one called to
C) Performing the action upon command
D) The intention to please oneself

Key Takeaway
Delight alone does not make an action obedience; obedience is defined by acting on a command.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider an action you do that you enjoy but know is not commanded by Allah. Have you ever justified it by saying 'it feels good' or 'it doesn't harm anyone'? How does Maturidi's definition of obedience challenge that excuse? What will you do about that action today?
?
Think of a sin you commit regularly. Do you ever feel that because you don't consciously intend to obey Satan, it's not really disobedience to Allah? How does Maturidi's point that obedience is defined by command, not intent, apply to your situation? What step can you take to align your actions with Allah's command?
?
We often seek pleasure as a measure of what is good. But Maturidi says pleasure is not the criterion for obedience. Is there an area of your life where you have prioritized your own pleasure over Allah's command? What is one specific change you can make to prioritize command over comfort?
